Defining 'Living' and the Proof from Essence Types
Razi defines 'living' and presents the proof from classification of essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of 'living' and the Ash'ari proof that life is a real attribute based on the classification of essences.

Core Idea
Life is not merely the ability to act; it is a distinct attribute making action possible.

Razi begins by presenting two views on divine life: the Mu'tazili view that 'living' is a mere description, and the Ash'ari view that it is a real attribute. He then provides a proof from the observable difference between living and non-living beings, showing that an additional attribute must account for this difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the two definitions
Carefully read the Mu'tazili and Ash'ari definitions of 'living' in Arabic and English. Notice the key difference: the Ash'ari adds that life is an attribute subsisting in the essence.
Abu al-Husayn al-Basri is the Mu'tazili; 'our companions' refers to the Ash'aris.
2
Examine the proof from essence types
The proof divides essences into two kinds: those that can know and have power (living) and those that cannot (inanimates). Both kinds are equal in essentiality, so the difference must be due to an additional attribute.
Ask yourself: why can't the essence itself account for the difference?
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that life is a real attribute (sifa) distinct from the essence. This refutes the Mu'tazili view that life is just a description.
This is the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة عشرة في كونه تعالى حيا مذهب أبي الحسين أن الحي هو الذي لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر. وقال أصحابنا: الحياة صفة قائمة بالذات لأجلها لا يمتنع على الذات أن تعلم ويصح عليه أن يقدر. وحجة أصحابنا على قولهم : أن الذوات على قسمين : منها ما يعلم ويقدر، ومنها ما لا يصح ذلك عليه، وهي الجمادات والقسمان متساويان في الذاتية، ولولا امتياز ما يصح عليه أن يعلم ويقدر عما لا يصح عليه ذلك وإلا حصل هذا التفاوت. قال أبو الحسين البصري: إنا قد دللنا على أن ذاته مخالفة لسائر الذوات لنفس ذاته المخصوصة، فلم لا يجوز أن تكون صفة العالمية والقادرية في حق الله تعالى معللة بذاته المخصوصة ؟ وهذا سؤال حسن. والمعتمد لنا أن نقول : قولك : الحي هو الذي لا يمتنع عليه أن يعلم ويقدر هذا إشارة إلى نفي الامتناع والامتناع سلب ونفي الامتناع سلب السلب، فيكون أمرًا ثبوتيًا، ثم هذا الأمر الثبوتي ليس هو نفس الذات ؛ لأنا إذا علمنا انتهاء الممكنات إلى واجب الوجود لذاته فقد علمنا ذاته و بعد ما علمنا هذا الأمر - أعني قولنا لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر - والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعدوم يثبت أن كونه تعالى حيا صفة حقيقية قائمة بذاته، وهو المطلوب.
The fourteenth issue: On His being exalted alive. The opinion of Abu al-Husayn is that the living is that which is not impossible for it to know and have power. And our companions said: Life is an attribute subsisting in the essence, due to which it is not impossible for the essence to know, and it is valid for it to have power. And the proof of our companions for their statement: Essences are of two kinds: among them that which knows and has power, and among them that which that is not valid of, and they are the inanimates. And the two kinds are equal in essentiality, and were it not for the distinction of that which it is valid for it to know and have power from that which it is not valid for it, this variation would not occur. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri said: We have proven that His essence is different from other essences by its own specific essence, so why is it not permissible that the attribute of worldliness and poweriness in the case of God be caused by His specific essence? And this is a good question. And the relied-upon [proof] for us is to say: Your saying: The living is that which is not impossible for it to know and have power—this indicates the negation of impossibility, and impossibility is a negation, and the negation of impossibility is a negation of negation, so it becomes a positive matter. Then this positive matter is not the essence itself; because when we know that possible things end in a necessary existent by essence, we have known His essence. And after we have known this matter—that is, our saying: It is not impossible for it to know and have power—and the known is other than what is not existent, it is established that His being exalted alive is a real attribute subsisting in His essence. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument on the attribute of life (hayat) for God. It begins by presenting the position of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (a Mu'tazili) that 'living' is defined as that which is not impossible for it to know and have power. Then the Ash'ari position (our companions) is given: life is an attribute (sifa) subsisting in the essence, because of which knowledge and power are possible. The proof offered is that essences are divided into two categories: those that can know and have power (like living beings) and those that cannot (inanimates). Since both are equal in essentiality, the difference must be due to an attribute. Abu al-Husayn objects that if God's essence is unique, its uniqueness could suffice to explain knowledge and power. Razi acknowledges this as a good question. Then Razi provides the relied-upon response: the definition of 'living' involves a negation (of impossibility); negation of negation is affirmative. This affirmative thing is not the essence itself, because we know the essence from the contingency argument (ending in a necessary existent), and then we know this additional matter (the negation of impossibility), which is other than the essence. Therefore, life is a real attribute. The argument is a rejoinder to the Mu'tazili position.

Key Terms
حي
حياة
امتناع
صفة
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life is merely a description of the ability to know and act, not a real attribute.
Correction: The Ash'ari proof shows that since essences are equal in essentiality, the difference between living and non-living must be due to an additional attribute, namely life.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari proof, why is life not identical to the essence?
A) Because essences are equal in essentiality but differ in capability, so an attribute is needed.
B) Because the essence is unknowable.
C) Because life is a negation of impossibility.
D) Because the Mu'tazili rejected life as an attribute.

Key Takeaway
Life is a real attribute of God, distinct from His essence, as shown by the distinction between living and non-living beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's life is a real attribute and not just His essence, do you truly believe He is alive in a way that transcends your imagination? Or do you mentally reduce His life to a metaphor? How might this affect your awareness of His presence in your daily actions?
?
Your own ability to know and act is a gift from the Living God. Are you using that gift to seek knowledge of Him and act in obedience? Or are you leaving it idle like the inanimates? What will you do today to change that?
?
The proof shows that the difference between living and non-living requires an additional attribute. What attributes of God have you ignored or undervalued? Will you commit to reflecting on one divine attribute each day this week and letting it shape your worship?

Responding to the Mu'tazili Objection: Life as a Positive Attribute
Razi addresses the Mu'tazili objection and provides the relied-upon proof for life as a real attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili objection that God's unique essence could account for knowledge and power, and the Ash'ari response that life is a positive attribute distinct from the essence.

Core Idea
Life is not a negation but a positive attribute, proven by the logic of double negation.

After presenting the proof from essence types, Razi addresses a powerful objection from Abu al-Husayn: if God's essence is unique, why is an extra attribute needed? Razi acknowledges the objection's strength, then delivers the definitive Ash'ari response based on the logical structure of the definition of 'living'.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and acknowledge its strength
Abu al-Husayn argues that since God's essence is unique, its uniqueness could directly explain knowledge and power without an extra attribute. Razi calls this 'a good question'.
This shows Razi's intellectual honesty.
2
Understand the logical transformation
The definition 'not impossible' involves a negation (impossibility is a negation), and the negation of negation yields an affirmative. Thus 'not impossible' is a positive reality.
This is the key move: turning a seemingly negative description into a positive attribute.
3
Recognize why this positive reality is not the essence
We know God's essence through the contingency argument (possible things end in a necessary existent). The positive reality 'not impossible' is additional to that essence, so it must be a distinct attribute.
Thus life is a real attribute, not a mere negation or the essence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة عشرة في كونه تعالى حيا مذهب أبي الحسين أن الحي هو الذي لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر. وقال أصحابنا: الحياة صفة قائمة بالذات لأجلها لا يمتنع على الذات أن تعلم ويصح عليه أن يقدر. وحجة أصحابنا على قولهم : أن الذوات على قسمين : منها ما يعلم ويقدر، ومنها ما لا يصح ذلك عليه، وهي الجمادات والقسمان متساويان في الذاتية، ولولا امتياز ما يصح عليه أن يعلم ويقدر عما لا يصح عليه ذلك وإلا حصل هذا التفاوت. قال أبو الحسين البصري: إنا قد دللنا على أن ذاته مخالفة لسائر الذوات لنفس ذاته المخصوصة، فلم لا يجوز أن تكون صفة العالمية والقادرية في حق الله تعالى معللة بذاته المخصوصة ؟ وهذا سؤال حسن. والمعتمد لنا أن نقول : قولك : الحي هو الذي لا يمتنع عليه أن يعلم ويقدر هذا إشارة إلى نفي الامتناع والامتناع سلب ونفي الامتناع سلب السلب، فيكون أمرًا ثبوتيًا، ثم هذا الأمر الثبوتي ليس هو نفس الذات ؛ لأنا إذا علمنا انتهاء الممكنات إلى واجب الوجود لذاته فقد علمنا ذاته و بعد ما علمنا هذا الأمر - أعني قولنا لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر - والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعدوم يثبت أن كونه تعالى حيا صفة حقيقية قائمة بذاته، وهو المطلوب.
The fourteenth issue: On His being exalted alive. The opinion of Abu al-Husayn is that the living is that which is not impossible for it to know and have power. And our companions said: Life is an attribute subsisting in the essence, due to which it is not impossible for the essence to know, and it is valid for it to have power. And the proof of our companions for their statement: Essences are of two kinds: among them that which knows and has power, and among them that which that is not valid of, and they are the inanimates. And the two kinds are equal in essentiality, and were it not for the distinction of that which it is valid for it to know and have power from that which it is not valid for it, this variation would not occur. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri said: We have proven that His essence is different from other essences by its own specific essence, so why is it not permissible that the attribute of worldliness and poweriness in the case of God be caused by His specific essence? And this is a good question. And the relied-upon [proof] for us is to say: Your saying: The living is that which is not impossible for it to know and have power—this indicates the negation of impossibility, and impossibility is a negation, and the negation of impossibility is a negation of negation, so it becomes a positive matter. Then this positive matter is not the essence itself; because when we know that possible things end in a necessary existent by essence, we have known His essence. And after we have known this matter—that is, our saying: It is not impossible for it to know and have power—and the known is other than what is not existent, it is established that His being exalted alive is a real attribute subsisting in His essence. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument on the attribute of life (hayat) for God. It begins by presenting the position of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (a Mu'tazili) that 'living' is defined as that which is not impossible for it to know and have power. Then the Ash'ari position (our companions) is given: life is an attribute (sifa) subsisting in the essence, because of which knowledge and power are possible. The proof offered is that essences are divided into two categories: those that can know and have power (like living beings) and those that cannot (inanimates). Since both are equal in essentiality, the difference must be due to an attribute. Abu al-Husayn objects that if God's essence is unique, its uniqueness could suffice to explain knowledge and power. Razi acknowledges this as a good question. Then Razi provides the relied-upon response: the definition of 'living' involves a negation (of impossibility); negation of negation is affirmative. This affirmative thing is not the essence itself, because we know the essence from the contingency argument (ending in a necessary existent), and then we know this additional matter (the negation of impossibility), which is other than the essence. Therefore, life is a real attribute. The argument is a rejoinder to the Mu'tazili position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life is a pure negation (the absence of impossibility).
Correction: The negation of impossibility is itself a positive matter (amr thubūti), not a negation.

Checkpoint
What is the logical status of 'negation of impossibility' according to the relied-upon proof?
A) It is a negation.
B) It is a positive matter.
C) It is the essence itself.
D) It is an unknown quality.

Key Takeaway
The definition of living involves a double negation, which yields a positive reality that must be a real attribute distinct from the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is alive,' do you truly treat His life as a real, active reality that affects your daily choices—or is it just a label? In what specific situation today will you consciously rely on the fact that He knows all and has all power?
?
Razi calls the Mu'tazili objection 'a good question.' Are you willing to seriously examine doubts about your faith, or do you avoid them? What objection to an article of your belief have you never honestly considered, and how will you seek to answer it?
?
The double negation argument shows that God's life is not a simple absence of something, but a powerful positive reality. Is there an area of your life where you treat God as absent or passive—as if He is not fully aware or able? What will you do this week to remind yourself of His active, living involvement?

Knowledge and Power: Concepts Other Than the Essence
Razi states the minimal claim and analyzes knowledge as a relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's minimal claim that the concepts of divine knowledge and power are distinct from the essence, and how he grounds this in the relational nature of knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a relation to the known, and is therefore other than the essence.

Razi begins by lowering the bar: he only insists that the concept of 'knowing' is not identical to the concept of the essence. He then offers a precise analysis of knowledge as a relation between knower and known. This passage clarifies the actual point of dispute between the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila regarding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the minimal claim
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify Razi's statement of his minimal claim: 'the concept of His being knowing... is not the same concept as His essence.'
Razi suggests that even the Mu'tazila might agree on this.
2
Understand the relational analysis
Notice how Razi defines knowledge: it is a specific relation (nisba) and addition (idafa) between the knower and the known, called 'ta'alluq' by theologians. He claims that this relation is other than the essence.
This is the core of his argument.
3
Compare the two positions
Identify the two views: Razi's own (knowledge = relation, distinct from essence) and the Ash'ari view (three things: essence, attribute as real quality, and then a relation). Observe the nuance.
Razi does not specify which group he belongs to, but notes his companions' view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة عشرة في إثبات أن الله تعالى علما وقدرة أعلم أنا لا ندعي في هذه المسالة أزيد من أن المفهوم من كونه تعالى عالمًا قادرًا حيا ليس نفس المفهوم من ذاته، بل هو أمر مغاير لذاته فإن كانت المعتزلة تساعد على هذا القدر فقد حصل الوفاق وزال الخلاف واعلم أن أكثر المتكلمين تخبطوا في تعيين محل النزاع في هذه المسألة. وتحقيق الكلام أن نقول : إن كل من علم أمرًا من الأمور فإنه لا بد وأن يحصل بين العلم وبين المعلوم نسبة مخصوصة وإضافة مخصوصة هذه الإضافة هي التي يعبر عنها المتكلمون بالتعلق فيقولون: العلم متعلق بالمعلوم، وعندنا أن العلم عبارة عن نفس هذا التعلق وعن نفس هذه الإضافة المخصوصة وندعي أن هذه الإضافة والنسبة مغايرة لنفس الذات، فالعلم مع هذه الإضافة المخصوصة أمران لا أمر واحد. وجماعة من أصحابنا اثبتوا أمورًا ثلاثة : الذات والعلم - وهو صفة حقيقية قائمة بالذات - ثم أثبتوا لهذه الصفة هذه النسبة، وهذه الإضافة وهذا التعلق، فيكون هذا التعلق حاصلا بین تلك الصفة وبين المعلوم.
The fifteenth issue: On proving that God has knowledge and power. Know that we do not claim in this issue more than that the concept of His being exalted knowing, powerful, living is not the same concept as His essence, but it is something other than His essence. So if the Mu'tazila agree on this much, then agreement is reached and disagreement is removed. And know that most of the theologians have been confused in specifying the point of dispute in this issue. And the precise point is to say: Indeed, whoever knows something, there must occur between the knowledge and the known a specific relation and a specific addition. This addition is what the theologians call 'ta'alluq' (relation), so they say: knowledge is related to the known. And with us, knowledge is nothing but this relation and this specific addition, and we claim that this addition and relation are other than the essence itself. Thus knowledge with this specific addition are two things, not one thing. And a group of our companions have affirmed three things: the essence, and knowledge—which is a real attribute subsisting in the essence—then they affirmed for this attribute this relation, this addition, and this 'ta'alluq', so this 'ta'alluq' occurs between that attribute and the known.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a nuanced exposition of the issue of divine attributes. Razi begins by stating the minimal claim he insists upon: the concept (mafhum) of God being knowing, powerful, living is not identical to the concept of His essence; rather, it is something other (mughtayir) than the essence. He suggests that if the Mu'tazila agree on this, then the dispute is resolved, because the Mu'tazila denied distinct attributes. He notes that most theologians have been confused about the precise point of disagreement. Then he provides a precise analysis: knowledge involves a specific relation (nisba) and addition (idafa) between the knower and the known. The theologians call this relation 'ta'alluq'. Razi's own view is that knowledge is exactly this relation and addition, and this relation is other than the essence. Therefore, knowledge (with the relation) and the essence are two things, not one. He then notes that a group of his school (presumably Ash'aris) affirm three things: the essence, knowledge as a real attribute subsisting in the essence, and then a relation (ta'alluq) between that attribute and the known. Thus, they distinguish between the attribute and its relation.

Key Terms
علم
قدرة
صفة
تعلق
إضافة
مغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the disagreement is about whether God has knowledge or power at all.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the dispute is about whether the concepts of these attributes are identical to the essence or other than it.

Checkpoint
What is the minimal claim Razi makes in this issue?
A) Knowledge and power are identical to the essence.
B) The concept of God being knowing is other than the concept of His essence.
C) Knowledge is a relation that is identical to the essence.
D) The Mu'tazila are completely wrong.

Key Takeaway
Razi's minimal claim is that divine attributes are conceptually distinct from the essence, grounded in the relational nature of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah knows', do you ever pause to consider that His knowledge is not merely an extension of His essence but a real, relational attribute? How does that affect your trust that He knows you personally and intimately?
?
This lesson distinguishes between God's essence and the concept of His knowledge. Have you ever unconsciously thought of Allah's power as just His essence acting without any distinct reality? If His power is a real attribute, how should that change your feeling of dependence on Him?
?
Razi argues that even the concept of knowing is other than essence. If you truly believe that, then your knowledge of God – what you conceive – is always incomplete. Does that humble you? Will you commit to seeking deeper understanding of His names and attributes, not settling for shallow familiarity?

Razi on the Minimum Entities for Divine Attributes
Razi enumerates varying positions on the number of entities: essence, knowledge, worldliness, relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's analysis of different theological positions on the number of entities (essence, knowledge, worldliness, relation) and his conclusion that only essence and a relation (awareness/knowledge) must be affirmed.

Core Idea
Simplicity in divine attributes: only essence and relation are necessary.

Razi examines the views of al-Baqillani and others on how many entities (essence, knowledge, worldliness, relations) must be affirmed. He finds the issue confused and asserts that only the essence and a single relation (awareness/knowledge) are necessary. This lesson walks through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The Arabic ends abruptly; focus on the clear part.
2
Identify the positions
The passage presents al-Baqillani's possible view of four entities, and two scenarios leading to four or five. Note how the relation (ta'alluq) is assigned to worldliness or knowledge.
3
Understand Razi's critique
Razi notes that most earlier scholars did not investigate these distinctions, leaving the matter confused. See how he dismisses the complexity.
4
Grasp Razi's own minimal position
Razi asserts that only essence and the relation (shu'ur/ilm) are necessary. Affirming other entities (like worldliness as separate) is not required. This is his main point.
The phrase 'and the addition, which is' is incomplete but the intended meaning is clear.
5
Reflect on the principle of parsimony
Ask yourself why Razi prefers the minimal view. This reflects a methodological principle in kalam: do not multiply entities without necessity.
Connects to the core idea of simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني فظاهر كلامه يشعر بإثبات أمور أربعة الذات والعلم ثم العلم يوجب العالمية - هذه أمور ثلاثة - ثم ههنا أمر آخر، وهو أنهم أثبتوا هذا التعلق للعالمية لا للعلم أو للعلم لا للعالمية، وعلى هذا التقدير يكون الحاصل هناك أمورًا أربعة وأما إن أثبتوا التعلق للعالمية وللعلم فيكون الحاصل هناك أمورًا خمسة الذات والعلم والعالمية وتعلق العلم وتعلق العالمية، وأكثر من تقدمنا ما بحثوا عن هذه الفروق، قلنا : بقيت مخبطة غير محصلة. والذي ندعيه ونقوله : أنه لا بد من إثبات الذات ولا بد من إثبات النسبة المسماة بالشعور وبالعلم، وأما إثبات سائر الأمور فذاك مما لا والإضافة، وهي
As for al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, his apparent statement suggests affirming four things: the essence, and knowledge, then knowledge necessitates worldliness—these are three things—then here is another matter, which is that they affirmed this relation (ta'alluq) to worldliness and not to knowledge, or to knowledge and not to worldliness. On this estimation, what is obtained there are four things. But if they affirmed the relation to worldliness and to knowledge, then what is obtained there are five things: the essence, knowledge, worldliness, the relation of knowledge, and the relation of worldliness. And most of those before us did not investigate these distinctions. We say: it remains a confused and unattained knot. And what we claim and say: it is necessary to affirm the essence and necessary to affirm the relation called 'shu'ur' (awareness) and 'ilm' (knowledge). As for affirming the other matters, that is something that [cannot] and the addition, which is
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on divine attributes by classifying the different positions on how many entities to affirm. Razi begins with al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's view, which appears to imply four things: essence, knowledge (as attribute), worldliness (al-`alimiyya) which knowledge entails, and a relation (ta'alluq). There is ambiguity whether the relation is attached to worldliness or to knowledge. If to one only, then there are four entities; if to both, then five. Razi notes that most earlier scholars did not examine these nuances, leaving the matter confused. Then he states his own position: the minimum necessary is to affirm the essence and the relation, which he calls 'shu'ur' (awareness) or 'ilm' (knowledge). Affirming anything beyond that (like worldliness as a separate entity) is not required. The passage ends with the phrase 'and the addition, which is' suggesting the text is cut off, but the intended meaning is clear: only essence and relation need be affirmed. This lesson presents a key analysis of the different theological positions, with Razi advocating for parsimony.

ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That affirming more entities (like worldliness as a separate attribute) is more accurate or necessary.
Correction: Razi argues that only essence and a relation (awareness/knowledge) are necessary; other entities are not required. Simplicity is preferred.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the minimum that must be affirmed?
A) Essence, knowledge, worldliness, and their relations.
B) Essence and the relation called awareness/knowledge.
C) Essence alone.
D) Five entities as per al-Baqillani's possible view.

Key Takeaway
Razi reduces the divine attribute discussion to the bare minimum: essence and a single relation of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you feel a need to add complexity to feel secure in your faith? Reflect on Razi's minimal model: essence and relation. Can you accept that simplicity is sufficient, or does it make you uneasy? How does this affect your trust in God's self-disclosure?
?
What unnecessary distinctions or complications have you added to your understanding of divine attributes? Are you willing to let them go and rest in the essence and the relation of knowledge that God knows? What would that require of you?
?
Razi critiques those who multiply entities without investigation. In your own theological reflections, are you prone to adopt positions out of habit or authority rather than clarity? Will you examine your beliefs to see if they pass Razi's test of necessity?

Divine Attributes

The Minimal Claim: Attributes as Additional to Essence
Razi introduces the minimal claim and surveys positions on the attribute-essence relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's minimal claim that the concept of God's attributes is additional to His essence, and the different positions of theologians on the number of entities (essence, attribute, relation) involved.

Core Idea
The concept of an attribute is not the same as the concept of the essence.

This lesson begins the fifteenth issue of Razi's Arba'in, where he tackles the nature of God's attributes. Rather than diving into complex proofs, Razi first clarifies the minimal point of disagreement: that the concept of being knowing or powerful is not identical to the concept of the essence itself. He then shows how theologians have confused the issue by multiplying the entities involved.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's opening claim
Read the first part of the passage where Razi states his minimal claim: the concept of God as knowing, powerful, and living is not the same as His essence, but something additional.
Notice he says 'al-mafhūm' – the concept, not the attribute itself.
2
Understand the role of the Mu'tazila
Razi says if the Mu'tazila agree on this minimal claim, disagreement vanishes. This shows the dispute is about the nature of the addition, not whether attributes exist.
The Mu'tazila are known for denying distinct attributes, but they might accept a conceptual distinction.
3
Grasp the concept of relation (ta'alluq)
Razi explains that knowledge involves a relation to the known. He identifies knowledge itself with this relation, which is additional to the essence.
This is Razi's own view: knowledge is the relation.
4
Survey the different positions
Razi lists the views of other theologians: some affirm three things (essence, attribute, relation), al-Baqillani's view suggests four or five, depending on how the relation is assigned.
These variations show the complexity of the issue.
5
Reflect on the unresolved confusion
Razi concludes that most earlier scholars did not investigate these distinctions, leaving the matter confused. This sets the stage for his own analysis.
Why do you think Razi highlights this confusion?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة عشرة في إثبات أن الله تعالى عالم قادر أعلم أنا لا ندعي في هذه المسألة أزيد من أن المفهوم من كونه تعالى عالمًا قادرًا حيًا ليس نفس المفهوم من ذاته، بل هو أمر مغاير لذاته فإن كانت المعتزلة تساعد على هذا القدر فقد حصل الوفاق وزال الخلاف واعلم أن أكثر المتكلمين تخبطوا في تعيين محل النزاع في هذه المسألة. وتحقيق الكلام أن نقول : إن كل من علم أمرًا من الأمور فإنه لا بد وأن يحصل بين العلم وبين المعلوم نسبة مخصوصة وإضافة مخصوصة هذه الإضافة هي التي يعبر عنها المتكلمون بالتعلق فيقولون: العلم متعلق بالمعلوم، وعندنا أن العلم عبارة عن نفس هذا التعلق وعن نفس هذه الإضافة المخصوصة وندعي أن هذه الإضافة والنسبة مغايرة لنفس الذات، فالعلم مع هذه الإضافة المخصوصة أمران لا أمر واحد. وجماعة من أصحابنا أثبتوا أمورًا ثلاثة : الذات والعلم - وهو صفة حقيقية قائمة بالذات - ثم أثبتوا لهذه الصفة هذه النسبة، وهذه الإضافة وهذا التعلق، فيكون هذا التعلق حاصلاً بين تلك الصفة وبين المعلوم. وأما القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني فظاهر كلامه يشعر بإثبات أمور أربعة الذات والعلم ثم العلم يوجب العالمية - هذه أمور ثلاثة - ثم ههنا أمر آخر، وهو أنهم أثبتوا هذا التعلق للعالمية لا للعلم أو للعلم لا للعالمية، وعلى هذا التقدير يكون الحاصل هناك أمورًا أربعة وأما إن أثبتوا التعلق للعالمية وللعلم فيكون الحاصل هناك أمورًا خمسة الذات والعلم والعالمية وتعلق العلم وتعلق العالمية، وأكثر من تقدمنا ما بحثوا عن هذه الفروق، قلنا : بقيت مخبطة غير محصلة.
The fifteenth issue: On proving that God, exalted, is knowing and powerful. I know that we do not claim in this issue more than that the concept of His being knowing, powerful, living is not the very concept of His essence, but it is a matter other than His essence. If the Mu‘tazila assist in this extent, then agreement has been achieved and disagreement has vanished. Know that most of the theologians have been confused in determining the locus of dispute in this issue. The verification of the discourse is that we say: Everyone who knows a thing among things, it is necessary that between the knowledge and the known there occurs a specific relation and a specific addition. This addition is what the theologians express as 'relation' (ta‘alluq), so they say: Knowledge is related to the known. And according to us, knowledge is nothing but this very relation and this very specific addition, and we claim that this addition and relation are other than the essence itself. Thus knowledge with this specific addition is two things, not one thing. And a group of our companions affirmed three things: the essence, and knowledge—which is a real attribute subsisting in the essence—then they affirmed for this attribute this relation, this addition, and this attachment, so this attachment occurs between that attribute and the known. As for Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, his apparent speech indicates affirming four things: the essence, then knowledge, then knowledge necessitates being-knowing—these are three things—then here is another matter, namely that they affirmed this attachment for the being-knowing (al-‘ālimiyya) not for knowledge, or for knowledge not for the being-knowing. On this estimation, what obtains there is four things. And if they affirm the attachment for both the being-knowing and knowledge, then what obtains there is five things: the essence, knowledge, being-knowing, the attachment of knowledge, and the attachment of being-knowing. Most of those before us did not investigate these distinctions. We said: It remains a confused [matter] not refined.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifteenth issue concerning the proof that God is knowing and powerful. Razi begins by stating his minimal claim: the concept of God as knowing, powerful, and living is not identical to the concept of His essence; it is something additional. He notes that if the Mu‘tazila agree on this, the disagreement disappears. He then observes that most theologians have been confused in defining the exact point of dispute. To clarify, he explains that whenever someone knows something, there is a specific relation between the knowledge and the known, which theologians call 'ta‘alluq' (relation). Razi declares that according to his view, knowledge is nothing but this relation itself, which is distinct from the essence. Thus knowledge together with this relation constitutes two things, not one. He then surveys the positions of his predecessors: some of his companions affirmed three things (essence, knowledge as an attribute, and the relation of knowledge to the known). Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's apparent view posits four things (essence, knowledge, being-knowing [‘ālimiyya], and then the relation either attributed to knowledge or to being-knowing), and if the relation is attributed to both, then five things. Razi notes that most earlier scholars did not delve into these distinctions, leaving the matter confused and unresolved.

نسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the dispute is about whether God has attributes at all, rather than about the conceptual relationship between attributes and essence.
Correction: Razi clarifies that his minimal claim is about the concept being additional, not about denying attributes. The Mu'tazila might accept this minimal claim, shifting the dispute to the nature of the addition.

Checkpoint
What is the basic claim Razi begins with in this passage?
A) God's knowledge is identical to His essence.
B) The concept of God's knowledge and power is additional to His essence.
C) The Mu‘tazila deny all attributes of God.
D) Knowledge and power are separate from the self.

Key Takeaway
Razi's minimal claim is that the concept of an attribute is additional to the essence, but the precise nature of that addition remains debated among theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the concept of God's knowledge is not identical to His essence, does that make His knowledge feel more like an attribute He chooses to have? How does that affect your trust that He knows everything about you, including your hidden thoughts?
?
The passage shows that even great scholars like al-Baqillani differed on the precise number and nature of entities involved. When you encounter disagreements among trusted scholars on theological details, does it unsettle you or deepen your humility? How do you respond?
?
Razi is content with a minimal claim and leaves the finer distinctions unresolved. Is there an area of your faith where you demand certainty beyond what is necessary? How might embracing some ambiguity actually strengthen your reliance on God rather than on your own understanding?

Razi's Decisive Proof that Attributes are Superadded
Razi gives his claim and the decisive proof for attribute superaddition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical proof that God's knowledge and power are not identical to His essence but are superadded attributes.

Core Idea
We can conceive of God's essence without His attributes, proving they are distinct.

Razi begins by stating his position: he affirms the essence and the relation called 'knowledge', but not other claims. He then offers what he calls the 'decisive proof' based on the fact that knowing the existence of the Necessary Existent does not automatically entail knowing that He is knowing and powerful in a specific sense. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's claim
Read the first part where Razi states what he affirms: the essence and the relation of knowledge. Note he does not engage with other claims.
2
Understand the decisive proof
The proof: we know that contingent things end in a Necessary Existent. But this knowledge alone does not give us knowledge that He is knowing and powerful in the specific sense. Since the known (the Necessary Existent) is other than the unknown (His specific attributes), the attributes are not identical to the essence.
This is a logical argument based on the distinction between two concepts.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Razi concludes: 'His being knowing and powerful is not identical to His essence.' This is the core claim of the proof.
4
Reflect on the significance
This proof establishes that attributes are superadded (zā'id) to the essence, a key position in Ash'ari theology.
It distinguishes Razi's view from those who say attributes are identical to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي ندعيه ونقوله : أنه لا بد من إثبات الذات ولا بد من إثبات النسبة المسماة بالشعور وبالعلم، وأما إثبات سائر الأمور فذاك مما لا ندعيه ولا نتعرض له. والدليل القاطع على ثبوت هذه المغايرة أنا إذا علمنا انتهاء الممكنات إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته لم يلزم من علمنا بهذا القدر علمنا بكونه تعالى عالمًا قادرًا بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم، فكان كونه تعالى عالمًا قادرًا ليس عين ذاته، ثم هذا المفهوم الزائد ليس أمرًا سلبياً، ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول : أنا نعلم بالضرورة أن كون العالم عالمًا عبارة عن نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم والمعلوم، وليست هذه النسبة وهذه الإضافة عبارة عن سلب شيء أو عن عدم شيء آخر. والثاني : وهو أن العلم لا يكون عبارة عن عدم أي شيء كان، بل عبارة عن عدم الجهل؛ لأن الجهل إن أريد به عدم العلم كان العلم عبارة عن عدم العدم، فيكون ثابتًا، وإن أريد به اعتقاد الشيء على خلاف ما هو عليه لم يلزم من عدم هذا المعنى حصول العلم؛ لأن الواحد منا قد يكون خاليًا عن الجهل بالشيء بمعنى أنه لا يكون عالمًا بالشيء، فثبت أن كونه تعالى عالمًا قادرًا أمر زائد على ذاته، وهو المطلوب.
And what we claim and say is that it is necessary to affirm the essence and it is necessary to affirm the relation called 'awareness' and 'knowledge'. As for affirming other matters, that is something we do not claim nor do we engage in. The decisive proof of the existence of this otherness is that when we know that contingent things end in an existent necessary of existence by itself, it does not follow from our knowledge of this extent our knowledge that He, exalted, is knowing and powerful in the meaning we mentioned. And the known is other than what is not known. Therefore His being knowing and powerful is not identical to His essence. Then this additional concept is not a negative matter. Two aspects indicate this: The first: we know necessarily that a knower's being knowing is an expression of a specific relation between the knower and the known, and this relation and this addition are not an expression of the negation of something or the absence of something else. The second: that knowledge is not an expression of the absence of anything whatsoever, but rather an expression of the absence of ignorance. Because if by 'ignorance' is meant the absence of knowledge, then knowledge would be an expression of the absence of absence, so it would be positive. And if by it is meant belief in a thing contrary to what it is, then from the absence of this meaning the occurrence of knowledge does not necessarily follow, because one of us may be free of ignorance of a thing in the sense that he is not knowing of that thing. So it is established that His being knowing and powerful is something additional to His essence, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's own positive claim and his decisive proof. He first states what he affirms: the essence and the relation called 'knowledge' or 'awareness'. He does not engage with further entities claimed by others. Then he gives the 'decisive proof': we know that contingent things depend on a Necessary Existent; this knowledge alone does not entail knowledge that He is knowing and powerful in the specific sense. Since we can conceive of a Necessary Existent without explicitly conceiving of His knowledge, the two concepts are distinct. He then argues that this additional concept is not merely negative (i.e., not a privation). He provides two aspects: First, we intuitively know that being knowledgeable is a relation between the knower and the known, not a negation. Second, knowledge cannot be the absence of anything, because if ignorance is the absence of knowledge, then knowledge would be the absence of that absence, which is a positive attribute. Alternatively, if ignorance is defined as false belief, then the absence of false belief does not automatically give knowledge (one can be simply unaware). Thus, knowledge is a positive, relational attribute superadded to the essence. Therefore, God's being knowing and powerful is additional to His essence.

Key Terms
مغايرة
نسبة
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi argues that attributes are completely separate entities from God's essence.
Correction: Razi only argues that attributes are not identical to the essence; he does not claim they are separate existing entities. In Ash'ari theology, attributes are not the essence nor other than the essence in existence.

Checkpoint
What does Razi's decisive proof rely on?
A) That knowing the Necessary Existent automatically gives knowledge of all His attributes.
B) That knowing the Necessary Existent does not give knowledge of His specific attributes.
C) That the attributes are identical to the essence.
D) That the attributes are created.

Key Takeaway
Razi's decisive proof shows that the concepts of God's essence and His attributes are distinct, establishing that attributes are superadded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing,' do you ever stop to think that this is a real attribute added to His essence? How does this realization affect your awe of Him? Make a commitment to ponder this distinction in your next prayer.
?
Razi argues that simply knowing the Necessary Existent does not automatically give you knowledge of His attributes. In your own faith journey, have you ever taken God's attributes for granted, assuming you know them just because you know He exists? What will you do to truly learn each attribute?
?
If the essence and attributes are distinct, then knowing God requires more than affirming His existence. Are you content with just knowing that God exists, or are you actively seeking to know His attributes? Write down one attribute you will study in depth this month and how you will apply it to your life.

Two Arguments that the Superadded Attribute is Positive
Razi provides two arguments to show the added attribute is not negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments from Razi that prove the superadded attribute of knowledge is a positive relational concept, not a mere negation.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a positive relation, not the absence of ignorance; that would be circular.

After establishing that the attribute is distinct from the essence, Razi preempts an objection: perhaps the added concept is just a negation or absence. In this passage, he provides two arguments to show that knowledge is a positive, relational attribute. The first argument appeals to our intuitive understanding of what it means to be knowing, and the second dismantles the notion that knowledge could be equated with the absence of ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read both arguments
Read the passage containing the two arguments (wajhān). The first is about the nature of knowledge as a relation; the second refutes the idea that knowledge is absence of ignorance.
2
Analyze the first argument
First argument: we know intuitively that 'being knowledgeable' is a specific relation between the knower and the known, not the negation of something else.
This is a direct appeal to common sense and intuition.
3
Analyze the second argument
Second argument: knowledge cannot be defined as the absence of ignorance. If ignorance means absence of knowledge, then knowledge would be absence of absence (double negation), making it positive. If ignorance means false belief, then absence of false belief doesn't guarantee knowledge (one can be unaware).
This is a logical analysis of definitions.
4
Conclude the lesson
Razi concludes that knowledge (and power) are positive, superadded attributes. This reinforces the conclusion from the decisive proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي ندعيه ونقوله : أنه لا بد من إثبات الذات ولا بد من إثبات النسبة المسماة بالشعور وبالعلم، وأما إثبات سائر الأمور فذاك مما لا ندعيه ولا نتعرض له. والدليل القاطع على ثبوت هذه المغايرة أنا إذا علمنا انتهاء الممكنات إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته لم يلزم من علمنا بهذا القدر علمنا بكونه تعالى عالمًا قادرًا بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم، فكان كونه تعالى عالمًا قادرًا ليس عين ذاته، ثم هذا المفهوم الزائد ليس أمرًا سلبياً، ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول : أنا نعلم بالضرورة أن كون العالم عالمًا عبارة عن نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم والمعلوم، وليست هذه النسبة وهذه الإضافة عبارة عن سلب شيء أو عن عدم شيء آخر. والثاني : وهو أن العلم لا يكون عبارة عن عدم أي شيء كان، بل عبارة عن عدم الجهل؛ لأن الجهل إن أريد به عدم العلم كان العلم عبارة عن عدم العدم، فيكون ثابتًا، وإن أريد به اعتقاد الشيء على خلاف ما هو عليه لم يلزم من عدم هذا المعنى حصول العلم؛ لأن الواحد منا قد يكون خاليًا عن الجهل بالشيء بمعنى أنه لا يكون عالمًا بالشيء، فثبت أن كونه تعالى عالمًا قادرًا أمر زائد على ذاته، وهو المطلوب.
And what we claim and say is that it is necessary to affirm the essence and it is necessary to affirm the relation called 'awareness' and 'knowledge'. As for affirming other matters, that is something we do not claim nor do we engage in. The decisive proof of the existence of this otherness is that when we know that contingent things end in an existent necessary of existence by itself, it does not follow from our knowledge of this extent our knowledge that He, exalted, is knowing and powerful in the meaning we mentioned. And the known is other than what is not known. Therefore His being knowing and powerful is not identical to His essence. Then this additional concept is not a negative matter. Two aspects indicate this: The first: we know necessarily that a knower's being knowing is an expression of a specific relation between the knower and the known, and this relation and this addition are not an expression of the negation of something or the absence of something else. The second: that knowledge is not an expression of the absence of anything whatsoever, but rather an expression of the absence of ignorance. Because if by 'ignorance' is meant the absence of knowledge, then knowledge would be an expression of the absence of absence, so it would be positive. And if by it is meant belief in a thing contrary to what it is, then from the absence of this meaning the occurrence of knowledge does not necessarily follow, because one of us may be free of ignorance of a thing in the sense that he is not knowing of that thing. So it is established that His being knowing and powerful is something additional to His essence, and that is what is sought.

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's own positive claim and his decisive proof. He first states what he affirms: the essence and the relation called 'knowledge' or 'awareness'. He does not engage with further entities claimed by others. Then he gives the 'decisive proof': we know that contingent things depend on a Necessary Existent; this knowledge alone does not entail knowledge that He is knowing and powerful in the specific sense. Since we can conceive of a Necessary Existent without explicitly conceiving of His knowledge, the two concepts are distinct. He then argues that this additional concept is not merely negative (i.e., not a privation). He provides two aspects: First, we intuitively know that being knowledgeable is a relation between the knower and the known, not a negation. Second, knowledge cannot be the absence of anything, because if ignorance is the absence of knowledge, then knowledge would be the absence of that absence, which is a positive attribute. Alternatively, if ignorance is defined as false belief, then the absence of false belief does not automatically give knowledge (one can be simply unaware). Thus, knowledge is a positive, relational attribute superadded to the essence. Therefore, God's being knowing and powerful is additional to His essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is simply the absence of ignorance (in the sense of false belief or unawareness).
Correction: If ignorance means lack of knowledge, knowledge would be absence of absence (positive). If ignorance means false belief, absence of false belief does not equal knowledge; one can be unaware without false belief.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first argument, what is 'being knowledgeable' (kawn al-ālim 'āliman) an expression of?
A) A negation of something.
B) A specific relation between the knower and the known.
C) An absence of ignorance.
D) A property of the known object.

Key Takeaway
Razi's two arguments prove that the superadded attribute of knowledge is a positive relational concept, not a negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge is a positive relation and not just the absence of ignorance, what does that mean for your own knowledge of Allah? Is your 'knowing' of Him a living connection, or merely the absence of doubts?
?
Razi says that being free of false belief does not automatically give you knowledge. Where in your life do you assume that because you are not wrong, you therefore know? Examine that assumption honestly.
?
Knowledge is a relation between knower and known. How actively are you cultivating that relation with Allah through study, reflection, and worship, or are you content with just not being ignorant?

Divine Attributes
beginner

Razi's Refutation of the Unknowable Attribute
Razi refutes Abu Hashim's view that the superadded attribute is unknowable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that the superadded attribute is unknowable by showing that any judgment about it requires prior conception.

Core Idea
To deny knowledge of an attribute, you must first conceive it—thus contradicting yourself.

The followers of Abu Hashim conceded that there is an additional attribute but claimed it is neither known nor unknown. Razi finds this position self-defeating and provides three arguments that any meaningful discourse about attributes presupposes their conception. This lesson walks through his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the objection and Razi's three proofs. Notice how each proof builds on the necessity of prior conception.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, then three numbered proofs.
2
Understand the opponents' claim
The followers of Abu Hashim say the attribute is 'not known nor unknown'. Ask yourself: how can something be an object of discussion if it is not known?
This is the position Razi calls extremely weak.
3
Analyze the first proof
The first proof: assent (taṣdīq) is preceded by conception (taṣawwur). To judge that the attribute is unknown, you must have conceived it. Thus the claim contradicts itself.
This is a logical refutation: the statement leads to its own negation.
4
Examine the second and third proofs
The second proof: knowing that the essence is qualified by attributes requires conceiving those attributes. The third proof: the opponents distinguish between attributes like 'being-knowing' and 'being-powerful', which requires conception.
These strengthen the argument by showing the opponents implicitly use conception in their own reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحاب أبو هاشم نحن لا ننازع في إثبات هذا الزائد إلا أنا نقول : هذا الزائد صفة والصفة لا تكون معلومة ولا مجهولة وأنتم تقولون، هذه الصفة معلومة فموضع الخلاف ههنا. إلا أنا نقول : إثبات صفة غير معلومة مذهب اختاره أبو هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار وهو في غاية الضعف، ويدل عليه وجوه. الحجة الأولى : أن التصديق مسبوق بالتصور، فلولا أنكم تصورتم هذه الصفة وإلا لامتنع منكم أن تحكموا عليها بأنها غير معلومة وهذا القول يؤدي ثبوته إلى نفيه، فيكون باطلاً. الحجة الثانية : أنا نعلم كون الذات موصوفة ولولا أنا تصورناها وعقلناها وإلا لما كان يمكننا أن نحكم كون الذات موصوفة بها. الحجة الثالثة : أنكم تميزون بين الصفة المسماة بالعالمية وبين الصفة المسماة بالقادرية، وتفرقون بأن باعتبار صفة القادرية يصح الإيجاد وباعتبار صفة العالمية الاتقان والإحكام، ولولا أنكم تصورتم هذه الصفات وعقلتموها لما قدرتم على هذا التمييز.
The followers of Abu Hashim said: We do not dispute the affirmation of this additional thing, except that we say: This additional thing is an attribute, and the attribute is neither known nor unknown, while you say this attribute is known. So the locus of disagreement is here. But we say: Affirming an attribute that is not known is a position chosen by Abu Hashim and Qadi ‘Abd al-Jabbar, and it is extremely weak. Several proofs indicate this: The first proof: That assent is preceded by conception. If you had not conceived this attribute, it would be impossible for you to judge that it is unknown. And this statement leads to its own negation, so it is invalid. The second proof: We know that the essence is qualified [by attributes]. If we had not conceived them and understood them, it would not be possible for us to judge that the essence is qualified by them. The third proof: You distinguish between the attribute called 'being-knowing' (al-‘ālimiyya) and the attribute called 'being-powerful' (al-qādiriyya), and you differentiate that by virtue of the attribute of being-powerful, bringing into existence is valid, and by virtue of the attribute of being-knowing, perfection and precision are valid. If you had not conceived these attributes and understood them, you would not be able to make this distinction.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage records an objection from the followers of Abu Hashim (a Mu‘tazili theologian) and Razi's refutation. The opponents concede that there is an additional something (zā'id), but they claim it is an attribute that is neither known (ma‘lūma) nor unknown (majhūla). Razi identifies this as the position of Abu Hashim and Qadi ‘Abd al-Jabbar, calling it extremely weak. He provides three proofs: 1) Any judgment (taṣdīq) depends on a prior conception (taṣawwur). If they had not conceived this attribute, they could not judge it to be unknown. Their statement thus undermines itself. 2) We know that the essence is qualified (mawṣūfa) by attributes. To judge that the essence is qualified by these attributes, we must first conceive them. 3) The opponents themselves distinguish between the attribute of being-knowing (al-‘ālimiyya) and being-powerful (al-qādiriyya), attributing different effects to each (bringing into existence vs. perfection). Such distinction requires prior conception of those attributes. Therefore, the attributes must be known. Thus the claim that the superadded attribute is unknowable is refuted.

تصور
صفة
عالمية
قادرية
معلومة
مجهولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is additional to the essence it could be unknowable.
Correction: Razi shows that any meaningful discourse about it implies knowledge of it, because judgment requires conception.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof (al-hujja al-ūlā) against the claim that the attribute is unknown?
A) The attribute is identical to the essence.
B) Assent is preceded by conception, so judging it unknown requires prior conception.
C) Knowledge is always about external objects.
D) The Mu‘tazila agree with the position.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that if the superadded attribute were truly unknowable, no meaningful discourse about it would be possible, yet the opponents themselves distinguish between attributes, proving they conceive them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you think about God's attribute of knowledge, do you truly conceive it as distinct from your own limited knowledge, or have you never really pondered what it means? What would it change in your life if you genuinely tried to conceive of God's perfect knowledge?
?
You often speak of God's power and knowledge. Are these mere words, or have you actually tried to understand them? Razi shows that even to deny knowledge of an attribute, you must conceive it. So, what prevents you from deepening your conception of divine attributes today?
?
If you were to describe God's attribute of power to someone else, could you do it without confusing it with His knowledge? Razi's opponents could distinguish them. Can you? Take a moment to write down one difference between God's power and His knowledge, and then ask yourself: why have I not done this before?

The Decisive Proof that Attributes are Additional to the Essence
Imam Razi gives the first part of a decisive proof that attributes are additional to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God's attributes (e.g., knowledge, power) are not identical to His essence, based on the distinction between knowing the Necessary Being and knowing His attributes.

Core Idea
Knowing that God exists doesn't automatically tell us His attributes; they must be additional.

Imam Razi begins by presenting a decisive argument for the distinction between God's essence and His attributes. He uses a simple logical observation: our knowledge that contingent things require a Necessary Being does not, by itself, give us knowledge of His specific attributes like knowledge and power. Since the known (essence) is distinct from the unknown (attributes), the attributes must be additional to the essence. This lesson unpacks that core reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage (first part) and its translation. Focus on the logical flow: from knowledge of the Necessary Being to the lack of knowledge of attributes.
Notice the phrase 'المعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم' — the known is distinct from what is not known.
2
Identify the main premise
Identify the key premise: knowing that contingent things end in a Necessary Being does not entail knowing that He is knowing and powerful in the specific sense defined earlier.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that God's being knowing and powerful is not identical to His essence. Trace how the premise leads to this conclusion.
It is a simple argument from lack of entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل القاطع على ثبوت هذه المغايرة أنا إذا علمنا انتهاء الممكنات إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته لم يلزم من علمنا بهذا القدر علمنا بكونه تعالى عالمًا قادرًا بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم، فكان كونه تعالى عالما قادرًا ليس عين ذاته، ثم هذا المفهوم الزائد ليس أمرًا سلبياً، ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول : أنا نعلم بالضرورة أن كون العالم عالما عبارة عن نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم والمعلوم، وليست هذه النسبة وهذه الإضافة عبارة عن سلب شيء أو عن عدم شيء آخر. والثاني : وهو أن العلم لا يكون عبارة عن عدم أي شيء كان، بل عبارة عن عدم الجهل؛ لأن الجهل إن أريد به عدم العلم كان العلم عبارة عن عدم العدم، فيكون ثابتا، وإن أريد به اعتقاد الشيء على خلاف ما هو عليه لم يلزم من عدم هذا المعنى حصول العلم؛ لأن الواحد منا قد يكون خاليًا عن الجهل بالشيء بهذا أنه لا يكون عالما بالشيء، فثبت ان كونه تعالى عالمًا قادرًا أمر زائد على ذاته، وهو المطلوب.
The decisive proof of the establishment of this distinction is that if we know the termination of contingent things to an existent necessary of existence in itself, it does not necessarily follow from our knowledge of this extent our knowledge of His being, exalted, knowing and powerful in the sense we mentioned; and the known is distinct from what is not known. So His being, exalted, knowing and powerful is not identical to His essence. Then this additional concept is not a negative matter. Two proofs indicate it: First: we know necessarily that the being of the knowing person knowing is an expression of a specific relation between the knower and the known, and this relation and this addition are not an expression of a negation of something or absence of something else. Second: knowledge is not an expression of the absence of anything, but rather an expression of the absence of ignorance; because if by ignorance is meant absence of knowledge, then knowledge would be absence of absence, so it would be established; and if by it is meant belief in a thing contrary to what it is, then absence of this meaning does not entail acquisition of knowledge, because one of us may be free from ignorance of a thing without being knowledgeable of it. So it is established that His being, exalted, knowing and powerful is something additional to His essence. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a decisive proof for the reality of attributes. It begins by stating the argument: knowing that contingent things terminate in a Necessary Being does not automatically give specific knowledge of His attributes like knowledge and power. Since the known (essence) is distinct from the unknown (attributes), the attributes must be additional. Then it denies that the added concept is negative (salbī), and provides two proofs. The first proof uses the intuitive understanding that knowledge is a relation, not a negation. The second proof analyzes the definition of knowledge: it is not simply absence of ignorance, because absence of ignorance can occur without actual knowledge. Thus, the positive attribute is established.

Key Terms
مغايرة
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the first part of the proof?
A) Knowledge of contingent things leads directly to knowledge of attributes.
B) Knowledge of the Necessary Being does not automatically give knowledge of His specific attributes.
C) Attributes are identical to the essence because they are known together.
D) The Necessary Being cannot have attributes.

Key Takeaway
The distinctness of attributes from essence is proven because knowledge of the essence does not entail knowledge of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that Allah exists, but do you truly know His attributes beyond a mere affirmation? This lesson shows that knowing He exists doesn't automatically give you knowledge of His power or knowledge. Have you ever settled for a vague belief in 'God' without seeking to know His names and attributes? What concrete step will you take today to learn about one attribute, like His knowledge, from the Quran or authentic sources?
?
Imam Razi's proof is logical, but does it move your heart? The fact that Allah's attributes are distinct means they are real and can be known through revelation. Are you actively seeking that knowledge, or are you content with intellectual laziness? How does this lesson challenge you to study the divine attributes with more seriousness?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is powerful and knowing, how should that change your actions today? Does your daily life reflect a fear of His power and a hope in His knowledge? Spend a moment in sincere self-accounting: where have you neglected to act upon the reality of these attributes?

Two Proofs that Divine Attributes are Positive Realities
Imam Razi provides two proofs that the additional attribute (knowledge) is a positive reality, not a negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the additional attribute (e.g., knowledge) is a positive reality, not a mere negation, through two rational proofs from Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a positive relation, not just the absence of ignorance.

After establishing that attributes are additional to the essence, Imam Razi confronts a potential misconception: perhaps the added concept is negative, like the absence of ignorance. He provides two proofs to show that it is positive. The first appeals to our intuitive understanding of knowledge as a relation between knower and known, not a negation. The second analyzes the definition of knowledge itself, showing that it cannot be reduced to absence of ignorance. This lesson examines these two important confirmations.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation covering the two proofs. Note that the first proof uses the concept of 'specific relation' (نسبة مخصوصة).
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof states that knowledge is a relation between knower and known, not a negation. Reflect on why this makes it positive.
A relation is a positive attribute, not the absence of something.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof argues that knowledge is not simply absence of ignorance, because one can be free from ignorance without having knowledge. This shows knowledge is a positive concept.
The passage gives the example of not knowing something without being ignorant of it.

Source Text

Two proofs indicate it: First: we know necessarily that the being of the knowing person knowing is an expression of a specific relation between the knower and the known, and this relation and this addition are not an expression of a negation of something or absence of something else. Second: knowledge is not an expression of the absence of anything, but rather an expression of the absence of ignorance; because if by ignorance is meant absence of knowledge, then knowledge would be absence of absence, so it would be established; and if by it is meant belief in a thing contrary to what it is, then absence of this meaning does not entail acquisition of knowledge, because one of us may be free from ignorance of a thing without being knowledgeable of it. So it is established that His being, exalted, knowing and powerful is something additional to His essence. That is what is sought.

Commentary

Since the known (essence) is distinct from the unknown (attributes), the attributes must be additional. Then it denies that the added concept is negative (salbī), and provides two proofs. Refutation of Abu Hashim's Claim That Attributes Are Unknown
Razi presents three refutations against the claim that attributes are unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that an attribute is unknown leads to a self-defeating position, based on Razi's three proofs.

Core Idea
Judging an attribute as unknown requires conceptualizing it, so the claim self-destructs.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi engages with the Mu'tazilite Abu Hashim's problematic claim that attributes of God are neither known nor unknown. Razi shows that this position is logically unsustainable. He presents three compelling arguments grounded in the basic epistemology of conceptualization and judgment. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening statement by the followers of Abu Hashim. They affirm the attribute but claim it is unknown. Notice their claim that the attribute is neither known nor unknown.
This is the claim that Razi will refute.
2
First proof: judgment requires conceptualization
Razi argues that to judge something (e.g., it is unknown) you must first conceptualize it. So claiming an attribute is unknown entails that you have already conceptualized it, contradicting the claim.
This is a classic self-refutation argument.
3
Second proof: knowing the essence is qualified
We know that the divine essence is qualified by attributes. This knowledge requires prior conceptualization of those attributes. So attributes are known.
4
Third proof: distinguishing between attributes
If you can distinguish between the attribute of knowingness and powerfulness, you must have conceptualized each. This shows they are known.
The ability to differentiate proves conceptualization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحاب أبي هاشم نحن لا ننازع في إثبات هذا الزائد إلا أنا نقول : هذا الزائد صفة والصفة لا تكون معلومة ولا مجهولة وأنتم تقولون، هذه الصفة معلومة فموضع الخلاف ههنا . إلا أنا نقول : إثبات صفة غير معلومة مذهب اختاره أبو هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار وهو في غاية الضعف، ويدل عليه وجوه. الحجة الأولى : أن التصديق مسبوق بالتصور، فلولا أنكم تصورتم هذه الصفة وإلا لامتنع منكم أن تحكموا عليها بأنها غير معلومة وهذا قول يؤدي ثبوته إلى نفيه، فيكون باطلا. الحجة الثانية : أنا نعلم كون الذات موصوفة ولولا أنا تصورناها وعقلناها وإلا لما كان يمكننا أن نحكم يكون الذات موصوفة بها. الحجة الثالثة : أنكم تميزون بين الصفة المسماة بالعالمية وبين الصفة المسماة بالقادرية، وتفرقون أن باعتبار صفة القادرية يصح الإيجاد وباعتبار صفة العالمية الاتقان والإحكام، ولولا أنكم تصورتم هذه الصفات وعقلتموها لما قدرتم على هذا التمييز.
The followers of Abu Hashim said: We do not dispute the establishment of this addition; but we say: this addition is an attribute, and the attribute is neither known nor unknown, while you say that this attribute is known. So the point of disagreement is here. But we say: the establishment of an unknown attribute is a doctrine chosen by Abu Hashim and Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, and it is extremely weak. Proofs indicate it. The first proof: affirmation (tasdiq) is preceded by conceptualization (tasawwur). So if you had not conceptualized this attribute, it would be impossible for you to judge that it is unknown. This is a statement whose affirmation leads to its negation, so it is invalid. The second proof: we know that the essence is qualified (by attributes), and if we had not conceptualized them and understood them, we would not be able to judge that the essence is qualified by them. The third proof: you distinguish between the attribute called 'knowingness' and the attribute called 'powerfulness', and you differentiate that by virtue of the attribute of powerfulness, bringing into existence is valid, and by virtue of the attribute of knowingness, precision and perfection. And if you had not conceptualized these attributes and understood them, you would not be capable of this distinction.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the followers of Abu Hashim who admit the reality of the attribute but claim it is unknown (non-cognizable). Razi then refutes with three arguments. The first argument uses the principle that judgment (tasdiq) requires prior conceptualization (tasawwur); if they judge that the attribute is unknown, they have already conceptualized it, so their position self-destructs. The second argument says that we know the essence is qualified, which requires conceptualizing the qualities. The third argument shows that they distinguish between different attributes (knowingness and powerfulness), which requires conceptualizing each. Thus, the position is untenable.

تصديق
تصور
عالمية
قادرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think it is possible to affirm an attribute without having any concept of it.
Correction: The refutation shows that judgment requires conceptualization. To claim an attribute is unknown, you must have already conceptualized it, making the claim self-defeating.

Checkpoint
What is the first refutation based on?
A) The impossibility of infinite regress
B) That judgment requires prior conceptualization
C) That attributes are not real
D) That God's essence is simple

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that claiming an attribute is unknown is self-defeating because any judgment about it, including its unknownness, requires prior conceptualization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever claimed to believe something about God—like His mercy or justice—without having genuinely conceptualized what that attribute means? How does Razi's argument expose that as self-contradictory?
?
If even the opponents who deny knowledge of attributes still distinguish between them, what does that say about your own tendency to speak of God's attributes without deep understanding? Will you commit to studying each attribute until you can truly conceptualize it?
?
The first proof argues that any judgment requires prior conceptualization. When you judge your own faith or deeds, are you operating on genuine knowledge or mere assumptions? What concrete step will you take today to turn an assumption into true conceptualization?

The Philosophers' Argument against Divine Attributes
Razi presents the philosophers' dilemma about attribute modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' dilemma against divine attributes and why they claim attributes are impossible.

Core Idea
Attributes imply either a second necessary existent or a self-causing cause, both impossible.

Imam Razi presents the philosophers' core objection to real divine attributes: a dilemma about the attribute's mode of existence. The argument attempts to show that both possibilities lead to unacceptable conclusions. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step, before noting that Razi claims to have already answered it elsewhere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the flow of the argument. Notice the logical structure.
2
Identify the dilemma
The argument presents two possibilities for the attribute's existence: necessary or possible. Note how each leads to a contradiction.
3
Examine the first horn
Why can't the attribute be necessary? Two reasons: it would imply multiple necessary existents, and the attribute depends on the essence, so it cannot be necessary.
4
Examine the second horn
Why can't the attribute be possible? It would need a cause, which is the essence itself, creating a paradox of the essence being both receiver and agent.
5
Consider the broader context
Razi says the answer has been given before. Think about why this argument is significant for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة : فقد عولوا على حرف واحد وهو أنه ثبت أن ذاته سبحانه وتعالى واجب الوجود لذاته، فلو اتصفت تلك الذات بصفة لكانت تلك الصفة إما أن تكون واجبة لذاتها أو ممكنة لذاتها، والأول باطل بوجهين: أحدهما : ما ثبت أن واجب الوجود لذاته يمتنع أن يكون أكثر من واحد. وثانيهما: أن الصفة مفتقرة إلى الذات والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، لا واجب لذاته، وأما إن كانت الصفة ممكنة لذاتها افتقرت إلى مؤثر، ولا مؤثر إلا تلك الذات، فيلزم كون الذات البسيطة قابلة وفاعلة معًا، وذلك محال، وهذه الشبهة قد ذكرنا جوابها فيما قبل.
As for the philosophers, they relied on a single point: it is established that His essence, glorified and exalted, is necessary of existence in itself. So if that essence were qualified by an attribute, that attribute would either be necessary of existence in itself or possible in itself. The first is invalid by two aspects: one: it is established that the necessary of existence in itself is impossible to be more than one. Second: the attribute is dependent on the essence, and what depends on another is possible in itself, not necessary in itself. And if the attribute is possible in itself, it would need an effecter, and there is no effecter except that essence, so it would necessitate that the simple essence is both receiver and agent together, and that is impossible. This doubt we have already mentioned its answer previously.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument against real attributes. It reduces to a dilemma: if the attribute were necessary of existence, it would be a second necessary existent, which is impossible because the Necessary exists only one; moreover, the attribute depends on the essence, so it cannot be necessary. If the attribute were possible, it would need a cause (mu'aththir), which is the essence itself, making the essence both receiver (qabil) and agent (fa'il) simultaneously, which is impossible. Razi notes that the answer was given earlier.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن
مفتقر
مؤثر
بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument against attributes is conclusive and unanswerable.
Correction: Imam Razi indicates that the answer has already been given elsewhere, implying the argument is not decisive.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' argument, if an attribute were necessary, what would be impossible?
A) It would need a cause
B) It would imply multiple necessary existents
C) It would be dependent
D) It would be temporal

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' dilemma against attributes attempts to show that both necessity and possibility of attributes lead to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the philosophers' argument, did you sense a doubt about God's attributes? If yes, what does that tell you about the strength of your conviction? Will you seek out Razi's answer to eliminate that doubt, or will you let it linger?
?
If the philosophers' argument were correct, God would have no attributes like knowledge or power. How would that affect your personal relationship with Him? Can you genuinely accept that, or does your heart demand a God with attributes?
?
Your faith in God's attributes may rest on revelation, but does it also rely on rational arguments? This lesson presents a rational challenge. If reason alone could shake your belief, what does that reveal about the true foundation of your faith? What will you do to strengthen that foundation?
