The First Argument: Divine Knowingness Must Be Uncaused
First argument: necessity of divine knowingness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazili argument that God's attribute of knowingness, being necessary, cannot have a cause.

Core Idea
Necessary attributes cannot depend on a cause.

The Mu'tazila begin their attack on divine attributes with a logical argument about necessity. They claim that if God's knowingness were a real attribute, it would be either necessary or contingent. But if contingent, it would need a cause, which is impossible for a necessary being. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first shubha. Notice how the Mu'tazila define 'necessary' and 'contingent' and use them to argue that attributes cannot be real.
The argument hinges on the principle that the necessary cannot be caused.
2
Trace the logical steps
Break down the argument: 1) God's knowingness is necessary. 2) A necessary thing has no cause. 3) Therefore, it cannot be a contingent attribute requiring a cause.
3
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: If the attribute itself is necessary, then it is identical to God's essence. But the Ash'aris say attributes are distinct yet necessary.
This shows the deep disagreement over the meaning of necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة : فقد تمسكوا في نفي الصفات بوجوه : أولها : عالمية الإله صفة واجبة والواجب لا يعلل، وإنما قلنا : إن عالميته صفة واجبة، والواجب لا يعلل، لأنها لو كانت جائزة لافتقرت إلى الموجد والمخصص، وإنما قلنا : إن الواجب لا يعلل ؛ لأن الافتقار إلى العلة إنما يكون لأجل أن يترجح وجوده على عدمه لا لأجل تلك العلة وإذا كان الرجحان على سبيل الوجوب حاصلا وجب كونه غنيا عن العلة. الشبهة الثانية : لو كان علم الله تعالى أمرًا زائدا على ذاته لكان الله تعالى محتاجًا في أن يعلم الأشياء ويقدر عليها إلى تلك الصفة والحاجة على الله تعالى محال. الشبهة الثالثة : المعقول من قيام العرض بالجوهر كون العرض حاصلا في الحيز المعين تبعًا لحصول محله فيه والحال إنما يتميز عن المحل بسبب أن الحال حاصل في ذلك الحيز على سبيل التبعية والمحل حاصل فيه على سبيل الأصالة، أما في حق الله تعالى فذاته غير مختصة بالمكان والجهة أصلا، فلم يحصل التفاوت بين الصفة والموصوف من هذا الوجه، وحينئذ لم يكن جعل أحدهما موصوفا والآخر صفة أولى من القلب والعكس فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما موصوفا بالآخر وصفة له، وذلك محال، ولما كان القول بقيام الصفة بذاته يفضي إلى هذا المحال وجب أن يكون قيام الصفة بذاته محالا . الشبهة الرابعة : لو قامت الصفات القديمة بالذات القديمة لكانت الصفات والذات متشاركين في القدم والقدم وصف ثبوتي؛ لأنه عبارة عن نفي العدم
As for the Mu'tazila, they adhered in negating attributes to proofs: First: The knowingness of God is a necessary attribute, and the necessary is not caused. We only said that His knowingness is a necessary attribute, and the necessary is not caused, because if it were contingent it would need a bringer into existence and a specifier. And we only said that the necessary is not caused because the need for a cause is only so that its existence may be preponderated over its non-existence, not because of that cause. And if the preponderance is obtained in the way of necessity, it must be independent of cause. The second doubt: If God's knowledge were something additional to His essence, then God would be in need, in order to know things and have power over them, of that attribute, and need is impossible for God. The third doubt: What is rationally understood from the subsistence of an accident in a substance is that the accident occurs in a specific spatial location following the occurrence of its locus in it; and the accident is distinguished from the locus by the fact that the accident occurs in that location dependently while the locus occurs in it independently. But concerning God, His essence is not specific to place or direction at all, so the distinction between attribute and qualified does not obtain from this aspect. Then making one of them qualified and the other attribute is not more appropriate than reversal and swapping, so it would necessitate that each of them be qualified by the other and an attribute for it, and that is impossible. And since the statement that attributes subsist in His essence leads to this impossibility, the subsistence of attributes in His essence must be impossible. The fourth doubt: If the eternal attributes subsisted in the eternal essence, the attributes and the essence would share in eternity, and eternity is a positive attribute because it is the negation of non-existence...
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage enumerates four arguments of the Mu'tazila against real attributes. The first argues that if God's knowingness were contingent, it would need a cause, but it is necessary and thus uncaused. The second argues that if knowledge were additional, God would be needful of it. The third uses an analogy with accidents in substances: in created beings, an accident is in a locus, but God has no locus, so the distinction between attribute and qualified collapses, leading to either reversal or impossibility. The fourth, which is cut off, argues that if attributes were eternal, they would share eternity with God, and eternity is positive, implying multiplicity.

Key Terms
واجب
جائز
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila only deny attributes based on tawhid without logical argument.
Correction: This argument shows they use sophisticated logical reasoning about necessity and contingency.

Checkpoint
According to the first Mu'tazili argument, why can't a necessary attribute have a cause?
A) Because it would lead to an infinite regress.
B) Because the need for a cause only arises when existence is not preponderant, and a necessary attribute is inherently preponderant.
C) Because causes only apply to physical objects.
D) Because the Qur'an says so.

Key Takeaway
The first argument forces a choice: either attributes are necessary and uncaused (hence identical to essence) or contingent and caused (impossible for God).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowingness is necessary and uncaused, then your existence is entirely dependent on His knowledge. Do you live as if your life is known and willed by a necessary Being, or do you act as if it is a random accident? What would change if you truly believed that every moment of your life is known by a knowledge that has no cause? This week, before each action, consciously remind yourself: 'Allah knows, and His knowledge is uncaused.'
?
The argument states that a necessary attribute cannot be caused, while your soul is contingent and depends on Allah at every instant. Do you feel that dependency, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? Reflect on one area where you rely on your own planning more than on Allah's decree. Will you consciously surrender that area to Him today in your prayer?
?
The Mu'tazila concluded that God's attributes are identical to His essence. While this is a theological position, it raises a personal question: When you call upon Allah by His names (e.g., al-ʿAlīm, al-Qadīr), do you feel that you are addressing His very self, or do you imagine separate qualities? How would your devotion change if you realized that His knowledge is not something added to Him but is Him? Will your next dua reflect that intimacy, speaking directly to the All-Knowing Essence?

The Second Argument: Additional Knowledge Implies Need
Second argument: attribute implies dependence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Mu'tazili argument that if God's knowledge were additional to His essence, He would be needful of it, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Need is impossible for God, so any additional attribute would make Him needy.

The second argument is elegantly simple: if knowledge is a real attribute distinct from God's essence, then God must rely on it to know things. But reliance (need) on something other than the self is incompatible with divine self-sufficiency. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument passage
Read the short Arabic text. Notice the direct implication: additional attribute → dependence.
The argument is a syllogism: if knowledge is extra, God needs it; but God cannot need anything; therefore knowledge is not extra.
2
Identify the key term 'haja'
The word 'haja' (need, dependence) is central. Understand why need is impossible for God: because He is the Necessary Being, self-sufficient.
This is a fundamental axiom in kalam.
3
Contrast with the Ash'ari response
Ash'aris argue that 'need' does not apply to God's attributes because they are not separate beings but modes of His essence. Yet the Mu'tazila consider any distinction as implying need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
When you consider that even God's knowledge is not separate from His essence, how does that deepen your sense of accountability before Him? Does it make you more careful about what you do in private, knowing that His knowledge is not an external record but part of His very being?

The Third Argument: Analogy of Accidents and Substance
Third argument: accident-substance analogy fails for God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Mu'tazili argument, which uses the analogy of accidents in substances to reject divine attributes due to lack of spatial distinction.

Core Idea
Without spatial location, attribute and essence become indistinguishable.

The third argument draws from physics: in created beings, an accident (like color) subsists in a substance (like a body) by occupying the same spatial location dependently. But God has no spatial location, so the distinction between attribute and qualified being collapses. This lesson analyses that analogy and its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument passage
Read the Arabic. Notice the technical terms: 'arad' (accident), 'jawhar' (substance), 'hayyiz' (spatial location).
2
Understand the analogy from creation
In the physical world, an accident is characterized by being in a location dependently, while its substance is there independently. This spatial differentiation allows us to distinguish them.
This is based on Aristotelian physics used in kalam.
3
Apply to God
God has no spatial location, so the basis for distinguishing attribute from essence disappears. If we still call one 'attribute' and the other 'essence', it is arbitrary and could be reversed (each could be attribute of the other), which is absurd.
This leads to the impossibility of any real distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة : فقد تمسكوا في نفي الصفات بوجوه : أولها : عالمية الإله صفة واجبة والواجب لا يعلل، وإنما قلنا : إن عالميته صفة واجبة، والواجب لا يعلل، لأنها لو كانت جائزة لافتقرت إلى الموجد والمخصص، وإنما قلنا : إن الواجب لا يعلل ؛ لأن الافتقار إلى العلة إنما يكون لأجل أن يترجح وجوده على عدمه لا لأجل تلك العلة وإذا كان الرجحان على سبيل الوجوب حاصلا وجب كونه غنيا عن العلة. الشبهة الثانية : لو كان علم الله تعالى أمرًا زائدا على ذاته لكان الله تعالى محتاجًا في أن يعلم الأشياء ويقدر عليها إلى تلك الصفة والحاجة على الله تعالى محال. الشبهة الثالثة : المعقول من قيام العرض بالجوهر كون العرض حاصلا في الحيز المعين تبعًا لحصول محله فيه والحال إنما يتميز عن المحل بسبب أن الحال حاصل في ذلك الحيز على سبيل التبعية والمحل حاصل فيه على سبيل الأصالة، أما في حق الله تعالى فذاته غير مختصة بالمكان والجهة أصلا، فلم يحصل التفاوت بين الصفة والموصوف من هذا الوجه، وحينئذ لم يكن جعل أحدهما موصوفا والآخر صفة أولى من القلب والعكس فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما موصوفا بالآخر وصفة له، وذلك محال، ولما كان القول بقيام الصفة بذاته يفضي إلى هذا المحال وجب أن يكون قيام الصفة بذاته محالا . الشبهة الرابعة : لو قامت الصفات القديمة بالذات القديمة لكانت الصفات والذات متشاركين في القدم والقدم وصف ثبوتي؛ لأنه عبارة عن نفي العدم

And if the preponderance is obtained in the way of necessity, it must be independent of cause. The first argues that if God's knowingness were contingent, it would need a cause, but it is necessary and thus uncaused. The second argues that if knowledge were additional, God would be needful of it. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila reject attributes because they cannot conceive of a non-spatial distinction.
Correction: They use the spatial analogy to argue that without a spatial basis, the distinction is arbitrary and leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why does the distinction between attribute and qualified fail for God?
A) Because God's essence is simple.
B) Because God has no spatial location, so the basis for distinction (dependent vs. independent mode of location) does not apply.
C) Because attributes are not accidents.
D) Because God knows everything.

Key Takeaway
The third argument uses the physical model of accidents to show that any real attribute in a non-spatial being would be indistinguishable from the essence, leading to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God is unlike anything created, yet do you still unconsciously imagine His attributes as separate ‘parts’ added to His essence? How does this argument expose the limits of your imagination, and what will you do to correct that mental picture?
?
This argument says that without spatial location, the distinction between attribute and essence collapses. When you say 'God is knowing' or 'God is powerful,' do you truly mean that His knowledge and power are not distinct from His essence, or do you secretly think of them as something added? Take a moment to examine your own theology: can you sincerely affirm that God’s attributes are not separate without relying on physical analogies?
?
The Mu'tazila used this proof to deny attributes entirely, while the Ash'ari disagreed. Where do you stand, and more importantly, can you defend your position without using spatial language or mental images borrowed from creation? If you were challenged to explain how God’s attributes are real yet not distinct, what would you say—and does your answer satisfy your own heart?

The Fourth Argument: Eternity as a Shared Positive Attribute
Fourth argument: eternity as a shared positive attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the start of the fourth Mu'tazili argument that if attributes are eternal, they share the attribute of eternity with the essence, implying multiplicity of eternal entities.

Core Idea
Sharing eternity makes attributes co-eternal, implying plurality in the divine.

The fourth argument is incomplete in the text, but it begins to argue that if both the essence and the attributes are eternal, then they share the property of eternity (qidam). Since eternity is a positive attribute, this would mean multiple eternal things, contradicting the oneness of God. This lesson covers the fragment.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument fragment
Read the Arabic: 'If the eternal attributes subsisted in the eternal essence, the attributes and essence would share in eternity.'
The passage cuts off, but the direction is clear.
2
Understand the concept of 'qidam' as positive
The text says 'qidam is a positive attribute (wasf thubuti)' meaning it is not merely negative (lack of beginning) but a real quality.
This is a key point: if eternity is positive, then sharing it means multiple possessors of that quality.
3
Consider the implication
If both essence and attributes are eternal, we have multiple eternal beings, which compromises tawhid. The Mu'tazila thus argue against real attributes.
This argument is similar to the classic 'tashbik' (interweaving) argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة : فقد تمسكوا في نفي الصفات بوجوه : أولها : عالمية الإله صفة واجبة والواجب لا يعلل، وإنما قلنا : إن عالميته صفة واجبة، والواجب لا يعلل، لأنها لو كانت جائزة لافتقرت إلى الموجد والمخصص، وإنما قلنا : إن الواجب لا يعلل ؛ لأن الافتقار إلى العلة إنما يكون لأجل أن يترجح وجوده على عدمه لا لأجل تلك العلة وإذا كان الرجحان على سبيل الوجوب حاصلا وجب كونه غنيا عن العلة. الشبهة الثانية : لو كان علم الله تعالى أمرًا زائدا على ذاته لكان الله تعالى محتاجًا في أن يعلم الأشياء ويقدر عليها إلى تلك الصفة والحاجة على الله تعالى محال. الشبهة الثالثة : المعقول من قيام العرض بالجوهر كون العرض حاصلا في الحيز المعين تبعًا لحصول محله فيه والحال إنما يتميز عن المحل بسبب أن الحال حاصل في ذلك الحيز على سبيل التبعية والمحل حاصل فيه على سبيل الأصالة، أما في حق الله تعالى فذاته غير مختصة بالمكان والجهة أصلا، فلم يحصل التفاوت بين الصفة والموصوف من هذا الوجه، وحينئذ لم يكن جعل أحدهما موصوفا والآخر صفة أولى من القلب والعكس فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما موصوفا بالآخر وصفة له، وذلك محال، ولما كان القول بقيام الصفة بذاته يفضي إلى هذا المحال وجب أن يكون قيام الصفة بذاته محالا . الشبهة الرابعة : لو قامت الصفات القديمة بالذات القديمة لكانت الصفات والذات متشاركين في القدم والقدم وصف ثبوتي؛ لأنه عبارة عن نفي العدم
As for the Mu'tazila, they adhered in negating attributes to proofs: First: The knowingness of God is a necessary attribute, and the necessary is not caused. We only said that His knowingness is a necessary attribute, and the necessary is not caused, because if it were contingent it would need a bringer into existence and a specifier. And we only said that the necessary is not caused because the need for a cause is only so that its existence may be preponderated over its non-existence, not because of that cause. And if the preponderance is obtained in the way of necessity, it must be independent of cause. The second doubt: If God's knowledge were something additional to His essence, then God would be in need, in order to know things and have power over them, of that attribute, and need is impossible for God. The third doubt: What is rationally understood from the subsistence of an accident in a substance is that the accident occurs in a specific spatial location following the occurrence of its locus in it; and the accident is distinguished from the locus by the fact that the accident occurs in that location dependently while the locus occurs in it independently. But concerning God, His essence is not specific to place or direction at all, so the distinction between attribute and qualified does not obtain from this aspect. Then making one of them qualified and the other attribute is not more appropriate than reversal and swapping, so it would necessitate that each of them be qualified by the other and an attribute for it, and that is impossible. And since the statement that attributes subsist in His essence leads to this impossibility, the subsistence of attributes in His essence must be impossible. The fourth doubt: If the eternal attributes subsisted in the eternal essence, the attributes and the essence would share in eternity, and eternity is a positive attribute because it is the negation of non-existence...
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage enumerates four arguments of the Mu'tazila against real attributes. The first argues that if God's knowingness were contingent, it would need a cause, but it is necessary and thus uncaused. The second argues that if knowledge were additional, God would be needful of it. The third uses an analogy with accidents in substances: in created beings, an accident is in a locus, but God has no locus, so the distinction between attribute and qualified collapses, leading to either reversal or impossibility. The fourth, which is cut off, argues that if attributes were eternal, they would share eternity with God, and eternity is positive, implying multiplicity.

Key Terms
قدم
ثبوتي
ذات
صفات

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument fragment, what is problematic about sharing eternity?
A) Eternity is not a real attribute.
B) Sharing a positive attribute like eternity would imply multiple eternal beings.
C) Attributes cannot be eternal.
D) The essence is not eternal.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument uses the positive nature of eternity to argue that real attributes would create multiple eternal entities, violating divine oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes, do you inadvertently picture them as separate 'things' attached to God? How does this argument expose that tendency as a violation of tawhid? What concrete step will you take today to purify your conception of God's oneness?
?
This argument claims that sharing a positive attribute implies distinct individuals. In your daily life, where do you treat God's mercy and justice as if they were competing forces? How can you instead see them as aspects of a single, simple divine will?
?
If eternity is a positive attribute, then God's timelessness is not just absence of time but a real quality. How does this change the way you relate to a God who is 'outside' time? Will you commit to spending five minutes in silence today, acknowledging His eternal presence?

Philosophers' Argument Against Divine Attributes
Philosophers argue that divine attributes entail impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' rational dilemma against divine attributes and why they conclude that attributes cannot exist in God.

Core Idea
If God is necessary and simple, attributes would force multiplicity, dependence, or reciprocity — all impossible.

This passage presents the core rationalist objection to the reality of divine attributes as argued by the philosophers. The argument is a dilemma: if attributes are necessary or possible, both lead to absurdities. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the theological debate on God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the concise style of philosophical argumentation.
2
Identify the dilemma
The argument presents two options for an attribute: necessary by itself or possible by itself. Locate these two horns.
These are the only possibilities given the premise.
3
Examine each horn
For the necessary horn, note the two reasons why it fails. For the possible horn, understand the reasoning that leads to absurdity.
Each horn is refuted using logical consequences.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that attributes are impossible. Note the author's remark that the answer has been given earlier.
This sets up the need for a rebuttal from the theologians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة : فقد عولوا على حرف واحد وهو أنه ثبت أن ذاته سبحانه وتعالى واجب الوجود لذاته، فلو اتصفت تلك الذات بصفة لكانت تلك الصفة إما أن تكون واجبة لذاتها أو ممكنة لذاتها، والأول باطل بوجهين : أحدهما : ما ثبت أن واجب الوجود لذاته يمتنع أن يكون أكثر من واحد. وثانيهما: أن الصفة مفتقرة إلى الذات والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، لا واجب لذاته، وأما إن كانت الصفة ممكنة لذاتها افتقرت إلى مؤثر، ولا مؤثر إلا تلك الذات، فيلزم كون الذات البسيطة قابلة وفاعلة معًا، وذلك محال، وهذه الشبهة قد ذكرنا جوابها فيما قبل.
As for the philosophers: they relied on a single point, namely that it is established that His essence (glorified and exalted) is necessary of existence by itself. So if that essence were described by an attribute, then that attribute would either be necessary by itself or possible by itself. The first is invalid for two reasons: first, it is established that the necessary of existence by itself cannot be more than one. Second, the attribute is dependent on the essence, and that which depends on another is possible by itself, not necessary by itself. But if the attribute is possible by itself, it would need an efficient cause, and there is no efficient cause except that essence, so it would follow that the simple essence is both receptive and active simultaneously, and that is impossible. We have already mentioned the answer to this argument before.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument against the reality of divine attributes. It is a proof by contradiction. The argument begins from the premise that God's essence is necessary of existence by itself (واجب الوجود لذاته). If God had an attribute, that attribute must be either necessary by itself or possible by itself. The first option (necessary attribute) is impossible for two reasons: (1) a necessary being cannot be more than one — if the attribute were necessary, there would be two necessaries, contradicting the uniqueness of the Necessary Being. (2) An attribute depends on its subject (the essence), and that which depends on another is possible, not necessary. The second option (possible attribute) is also impossible because a possible attribute needs an external efficient cause to bring it into existence, but the only cause is the essence itself, so the essence would have to be both the receiver (قابل) and the agent (فاعل) of that attribute, which is absurd for a simple, non-composite essence. Thus, neither option is tenable, so attributes cannot exist. The author notes that he has already answered this argument earlier.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
صفة
ذات
ممكن
بسيط
قابل وفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny all divine attributes without distinction.
Correction: The philosophers only deny attributes that imply multiplicity or dependency; essential attributes might be considered differently.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers say the attribute cannot be necessary by itself?
A) Because it would require the attribute to be self-sufficient and not need the essence.
B) Because it would imply two necessary beings and the attribute depends on the essence, which makes it possible.
C) Because the attribute would then be identical to the essence.
D) Because the essence would then be composite.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that divine attributes are impossible because they would lead to either multiplicity of necessary beings or the absurdity of a simple essence being both agent and recipient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes like His knowledge or power, do you unconsciously imagine them as separate 'things' added to His essence? How might this philosophical argument expose a subtle anthropomorphism in your own thinking? What would it cost you to let go of that mental picture?
?
This argument concludes that divine attributes are logically impossible. Yet your faith affirms them. Does this tension unsettle you, or do you see it as a reminder of the limits of human reason? If your trust in reason were shaken here, would you still hold onto the belief? Why?
?
The author says the answer has already been given—but you may not have read it. Are you content to accept a refutation secondhand, or will you seek it out to truly understand? What does your answer reveal about the seriousness of your pursuit of knowing God?

First Mu'tazila Argument: Divine Knowledge is Necessary
Mu'tazila argue that God's being knowing is necessary and thus uncaused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite argument against the existence of distinct divine attributes, specifically why they claim that God's knowledge is necessary and therefore uncaused.

Core Idea
A necessary attribute cannot have a cause — this is the key to the Mu'tazila critique.

The Mu'tazila were not denying that God knows; they were denying that His knowledge is a separate entity added to His essence. Their first argument hinges on the idea that if knowledge were a distinct attribute, it would either be necessary (and thus uncaused, but then it would be another necessary being) or contingent (requiring a cause, leading to infinite regress). This passage presents that foundational reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; take your time to understand each phrase.
2
Identify the two premises
Identify the two key premises: (a) God's being knowing is a necessary attribute, and (b) what is necessary has no cause. Notice how the passage connects them.
The first premise is stated explicitly: 'عالمية الإله صفة واجبة'.
3
Understand the justification
The passage explains why a necessary attribute cannot have a cause: because the need for a cause arises only when existence is not inherently preferred, and a necessary attribute has inherent preference. If it were contingent, it would need a creator and a particularizer.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that a necessary attribute is uncaused. Therefore, if divine knowledge were a distinct attribute, it would have to be necessary (leading to multiplicity) or contingent (leading to an infinite regress). Neither is acceptable.
Thus, the Mu'tazila conclude that knowledge is identical to God's essence.
5
Connect to the broader context
This is the first of several arguments the Mu'tazila use to negate distinct attributes. Understanding it is key to grasping their overall theology of tawhid.
The rest of the lesson may build on this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة : فقد تمسكوا في نفي الصفات بوجوه : أولها : عالمية الإله صفة واجبة والواجب لا يعلل، وإنما قلنا : إن عالميته صفة واجبة، والواجب لا يعلل، لأنها لو كانت جائزة لافتقرت إلى الموجد والمخصص، وإنما قلنا : إن الواجب لا يعلل ؛ لأن الافتقار إلى العلة إنما يكون لأجل أن يترجح وجوده على عدمه لا لأجل تلك العلة وإذا كان الرجحان على سبيل الوجوب حاصلا وجب كونه غنيا عن العلة.
As for the Mu'tazila: they adhered to arguments for negating attributes, among them: First: God's being knowing is a necessary attribute, and what is necessary is not caused. We say that His being knowing is a necessary attribute, and what is necessary is not caused, because if it were contingent it would need a creator and a particularizer. And we say that what is necessary is not caused because need for a cause is only for the sake of preferring existence over non-existence, not because of that cause. And if the preference is obtained by way of necessity, then it must be independent of the cause.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite argument against divine attributes. It is a classification and proof. The Mu'tazila hold that God's being knowing (عالمية الإله) is a necessary attribute (صفة واجبة) — meaning it is intrinsic to His essence, not an additional entity. The argument is: a necessary attribute cannot have a cause (لا يعلل). Why? Because if it were contingent (جائزة), it would need a creator (موجد) and a particularizer (مخصص). But since it is necessary, its existence is necessarily preferred over its non-existence; it does not require a cause to tip the balance. Need for a cause arises only when existence is not inherently preferred. Thus, to avoid an infinite regress of causes, the necessary attribute must be uncaused. Therefore, affirming a real, distinct attribute would require it to be either necessary (contradicting the above) or contingent (requiring a cause, which would be an attribute of the essence, leading to composition). The Mu'tazila conclude that attributes cannot be distinct entities.

Key Terms
عالمية الإله
صفة واجبة
يعلل
جائزة
موجد
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny that God knows.
Correction: The Mu'tazila affirm that God knows, but they argue that His knowledge is not a separate attribute added to His essence; it is identical to His essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the Mu'tazila say a necessary attribute cannot have a cause?
A) Because a cause would be another necessary being.
B) Because the need for a cause only exists when existence is not inherently preferred, and a necessary attribute has inherent preference.
C) Because attributes are not real.
D) Because God cannot be the cause of His own attributes.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that if divine knowledge were a distinct attribute, it would have to be necessary (uncaused) or contingent (caused), both of which contradict tawhid; thus knowledge is identical to God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, do you imagine it as something He 'has' or something He 'is'? This lesson warns against treating attributes as separate. Is there an area of your faith where you have unconsciously added 'parts' to God? What would it take for you to purify your tawhid today?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that if knowledge were contingent, it would need a cause. Reflect: In your own life, do you treat God's attributes as contingent—as if He sometimes knows and sometimes doesn't? How does the certainty of God's necessary knowledge change the way you trust Him in your struggles?
?
This argument is about logical necessity in God. Are you willing to let go of any mental image that compromises God's absolute oneness, even if it feels familiar? What concrete step will you take this week to deepen your understanding that Allah is one without parts?

The Need for Attributes Implies Need
Mu'tazila reduce the additional-attributes view to impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's reductio argument that if God's attributes are distinct from His essence, then He would be dependent on them, which contradicts His self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
If God needed attributes, He would not be God.

This short but powerful argument is a classic example of Mu'tazila reasoning. It attempts to prove that divine attributes cannot be real and distinct by showing the absurd consequence: God would need something outside Himself. Let's break down the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how concise it is: a conditional statement leading to an impossibility.
2
Identify the premises
The argument has two key premises: (1) If knowledge is additional, then God needs it. (2) Need is impossible for God. The conclusion follows directly.
3
Understand the term 'need' (حاجة)
In kalām, 'need' implies dependency and deficiency. The Mu'tazila assume that any real attribute would be a means, implying God lacks something essential.
4
Consider the hidden assumption
The argument assumes that an attribute, if real, functions as an instrument. Is that necessarily true? This will be the point of debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : لو كان علم الله تعالى أمرًا زائدا على ذاته لكان الله تعالى محتاجًا في أن يعلم الأشياء ويقدر عليها إلى تلك الصفة والحاجة على الله تعالى محال.
The second argument: If God's knowledge were something additional to His essence, then God would be in need, in order to know things and have power over them, of that attribute. And need is impossible for God (exalted).
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite argument, a concise reductio ad absurdum. It is a short proof. The argument states: If God's knowledge (or power) were a distinct entity (زائدا على ذاته), then God would need that attribute in order to know and have power. But need (حاجة) is impossible for God, because God is self-sufficient and independent. Therefore, the attributes cannot be distinct. This argument assumes that any real attribute would be a means or instrument, implying deficiency in the essence. The Mu'tazila reject that God requires any external or internal means to act.

Key Terms
زائدا على الذات
حاجة
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that God knows.
Correction: The argument does not deny that God knows; it denies that knowledge is a separate entity that He needs. God knows by His essence, not by an addition.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the second argument?
A) God does not know or have power.
B) Knowledge cannot be an attribute of God.
C) If knowledge were distinct, God would need it, but need is impossible for God.
D) Attributes are possible but not necessary.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that if God had real, distinct attributes, He would depend on them, which contradicts His absolute independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt utterly dependent on something or someone to function? How does that feeling of need compare to the absolute independence of Allah that this lesson teaches? Will you consciously remind yourself today that Allah needs nothing from you, yet He still cares for you?
?
If Allah had no need for any attribute outside Himself, then your existence and actions add nothing to His perfection. How does that truth challenge your ego? In your next prayer, will you worship Him as the Perfectly Self-Sufficient, not because He needs your worship, but because you need Him?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that any distinct attribute would imply need. Reflect on a quality you pride yourself on—could you still be that quality without depending on it? What does that reveal about the difference between created attributes and Allah's attributes? Will you let go of your pride today in light of this?

Third Mu'tazila Argument: Spatialization of Attributes
Mu'tazila analogize attributes to accidents in space, leading to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use the concept of spatial distinction to argue against the reality of divine attributes.

Core Idea
God's non-spatial nature breaks the analogy between physical accidents and divine attributes.

This lesson presents the third Mu'tazilite argument against the reality of divine attributes. The argument focuses on the notion of 'subsistence' (قيام), a relationship between an accident and its substance in the physical world. By analyzing this relationship, the Mu'tazila claim that applying it to God leads to an absurd conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand subsistence in the physical world
Begin by grasping the Mu'tazilite definition of how an accident subsists in a substance: the accident occurs in a specific location as secondary (تبعية), while the substance is primary (أصالة). This spatial distinction allows us to differentiate the attribute from its substratum.
2
Apply the concept to God
Now consider God: His essence is not confined to any place or direction. Therefore, the spatial basis for distinguishing attribute from essence is absent.
3
Recognize the resulting interchangeability
Without spatial distinction, the relationship between attribute and essence becomes reversible. Each would have to be both attribute and described, which is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Conclude the argument
Since the idea of attributes subsisting in God leads to an impossibility, the Mu'tazila conclude that divine attributes cannot be real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : المعقول من قيام العرض بالجوهر كون العرض حاصلا في الحيز المعين تبعًا لحصول محله فيه والحال إنما يتميز عن المحل بسبب أن الحال حاصل في ذلك الحيز على سبيل التبعية والمحل حاصل فيه على سبيل الأصالة، أما في حق الله تعالى فذاته غير مختصة بالمكان والجهة أصلا، فلم يحصل التفاوت بين الصفة والموصوف من هذا الوجه، وحينئذ لم يكن جعل أحدهما موصوفا والآخر صفة أولى من القلب والعكس فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما موصوفا بالآخر وصفة له، وذلك محال، ولما كان القول بقيام الصفة بذاته يفضي إلى هذا المحال وجب أن يكون قيام الصفة بذاته محالا .
The third argument: The intelligible meaning of an accident subsisting in a substance is that the accident occurs in a particular location, following its substratum. The accident is distinguished from the substratum because the accident occurs in that location by way of following, while the substratum occurs in it by way of originality. But with respect to God (exalted), His essence is not specific to place or direction at all. So no distinction between attribute and described occurs in this respect. Then, making one of them described and the other attribute is not more appropriate than reversing them. So it would follow that each of them is described by the other and an attribute of it, and that is impossible. Since the statement that an attribute subsists in His essence leads to this impossibility, it must be impossible for an attribute to subsist in His essence.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the third Mu'tazilite argument against attributes, focusing on the concept of 'subsistence' (قيام). In the physical world, an accident (عرض) subsists in a substance (جوهر) by being located in a specific place, with the accident being secondary (تبعية) and the substance primary (أصالة). This spatial distinction allows us to differentiate between the attribute and the substratum. However, God is not spatial—He is not in a place or direction. Therefore, this basis for distinguishing the attribute from the essence is absent. If there were an attribute, there would be no way to determine which is the attribute and which is the essence; they would be interchangeable. Each would be both attribute and described, which is absurd. Therefore, the very idea of attributes subsisting in God is impossible. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum based on the unique non-spatial nature of God.

Key Terms
قيام العرض بالجوهر
تبعية
أصالة
مكان
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that God has any attributes at all.
Correction: The argument does not deny that God has attributes; it argues that the concept of 'subsistence' as used for physical objects cannot apply to God.

Checkpoint
Why does the third argument say that if attributes subsisted in God, they would be interchangeable with the essence?
A) Because God is simple.
B) Because there is no spatial distinction to differentiate attribute from essence in God.
C) Because attributes are not real.
D) Because God cannot have accidents.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that divine attributes cannot be real because the concept of subsistence requires spatial distinction, which does not apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes like knowledge or power, do you unconsciously imagine them as 'things' that 'reside' in God like qualities in a person? How does the Mu'tazila argument that spatial categories don't apply to God force you to rethink that mental habit? What practical step can you take today to purify your conception of God from such hidden analogies?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that applying the physical concept of 'subsistence' to God leads to logical absurdity. Are there areas in your own understanding of God where you cling to comfortable but inadequate mental images? Would you be willing to let go of those images even if it feels unsettling, trusting that God is greater than any finite concept?
?
If you truly internalize that 'His essence is not specific to place or direction at all,' how would that change the way you turn to God in prayer right now? Do you pray as if He is 'somewhere' listening, or do you recognize that He is beyond all spatial constraints? Commit to one prayer today where you deliberately avoid imagining God in any location.

The Fourth Mu'tazila Argument: Sharing Eternity Leads to Composition or Idolatry
Mu'tazila present a dilemma on sharing qidam leading to composition or identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila reductio against eternal attributes: that sharing in qidam forces either an infinite regress of composition or the absurd conclusion of multiple gods and interchangeable attributes.

Core Idea
Sharing eternity forces composition or polytheism — that is the Mu'tazila challenge.

Imam al-Razi presents the fourth Mu'tazilite argument against eternal attributes. The argument is a sharp dilemma: if attributes share eternity with God's essence, they must either be composite (leading to infinite regress) or identical (leading to multiple gods). This passage shows how rational reasoning can be used to critique theological positions, and it is a classic example of reductio ad absurdum in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the two branches introduced by 'فإما أن يتميز... أو لا يتميز'.
The argument is a dilemma: two mutually exclusive possibilities, both leading to impossibility.
2
Identify the shared attribute and its nature
The argument begins by stating that if eternal attributes subsist in the eternal essence, they share in qidam (eternity). Qidam is described as a positive attribute (wasf thubuti) because it negates prior non-existence, which is a kind of existence. This sets up the shared basis.
The Mu'tazila consider qidam a real attribute; this is a key premise.
3
Follow the first branch: distinction leads to composition and regress
If essence and attribute are distinguished by another consideration (اعتبار), then each is composed of two aspects: what they share (qidam) and what distinguishes them. Each aspect must be eternal, leading to further composition and infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible.
This is a regress argument against the possibility of distinct eternal entities.
4
Follow the second branch: no distinction leads to identity and absurdity
If they are not distinguished, they become exactly similar (مثلين). Since the essence is God, the attribute must also be God — implying multiple gods. Moreover, if they are identical, it would be equally appropriate for the essence to subsist in the attribute, swapping roles, and making knowledge and power interchangeable. All are impossible.
This branch attacks the very distinctness of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : لو قامت الصفات القديمة بالذات القديمة لكانت الصفات والذات متشاركين في القدم والقدم وصف ثبوتي ؛ لأنه عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق، ونفي العدم ثبوت فإذن الذات والصفات تشتركان في هذا الوصف الثبوتي المسمى بالقدم، فإما أن يتميز الذات عن الصفة باعتبار آخر أو لا يتميز، فإن تميز فما به المشاركة غير ما به المباينة، فيكون كل واحد من الذات والصفات متركبا من هذين الاعتبارين - أعني ما به يشتركان وما به يتباينان - ثم إن كل واحد من ذينك الاعتبارين لا بد أن يكون أيضا قديماً ؛ لأنه جزء جزء القديم، وإذا اشترك ذانك الجزآن في القدم فلا بد وأن يتباينا باعتبار آخر، وحينئذ يتركب كل واحد من ذينك الجزأين من جزأين آخرين ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال. وأما إن قلنا بأن الذات والصفات بعد اشتراكهما في القدم لا يتميز كل واحد الآخر بماهية مخصوصة فحينئذ تكون الذات والصفة مثلين ولما كانت الذات إلها وجب أن تكون الصفة إلهاً فيكون هذا قولا بتعدد الآلهة.،، وأيضًا إذا كانت الذوات والصفات متماثلين لم يكن قيام أحدهما بالآخر أولى من قيام الآخر به، فتكون الذات صفة، والصفة ذاتا، والعلم قدرة، والقدرة علمًا، وكل ذلك محال ولما كان القول بإثبات الصفة الصفة القديمة مفضّيا إلى المحال، وجب أن يكون القول به محالا.
The fourth argument: If the eternal attributes subsisted in the eternal essence, then the attributes and the essence would share in eternity (qidam). Qidam is a positive attribute (wasf thubuti) because it is the negation of prior non-existence, and the negation of non-existence is existence. So the essence and attributes share this positive attribute called qidam. Then either the essence is distinguished from the attribute by another consideration or not. If distinguished, then that by which they share is different from that by which they differ, so each of the essence and attributes would be composed of these two considerations—i.e., that by which they share and that by which they differ. Then each of those two considerations must also be eternal, because it is a part of the eternal. And if those two parts share in qidam, they must differ by another consideration, and then each of those two parts would be composed of two other parts, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, which is impossible. But if we say that after sharing in qidam the essence and attributes are not distinguished by a specific essence (mahiyya), then the essence and attribute would be two similars. Since the essence is God, the attribute must also be God, and this is a statement of multiple gods. Also, if the essences and attributes were perfectly similar, then one subsisting in the other would be no more appropriate than the reverse; so the essence would be an attribute and the attribute an essence, knowledge would be power and power knowledge, all of which is impossible. Since the statement affirming the eternal attribute leads to these impossibilities, it must be impossible.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This is the fourth Mu'tazilite argument, a complex reductio. It begins with the premise that if eternal attributes subsist in the eternal essence, both share the attribute of qidam (eternity), which is a positive attribute (wasf thubuti) since it negates prior non-existence. The argument then splits into two possibilities: (1) The essence and attributes are distinguished by some other consideration (اعتبار) besides qidam. In that case, each is composed of two aspects: what they share (qidam) and what distinguishes them. But each of those aspects must itself be eternal, as a part of an eternal whole. Then those two aspects also share in qidam and must be distinguished by yet another aspect, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. (2) They are not distinguished at all, meaning they are identical in essence. Then they would be like two identical things (مثلين). Since the essence is God, the attribute would also be God, implying multiple gods (تعدد الآلهة), which is absurd. Furthermore, if they are perfectly similar, then it would be equally appropriate for the essence to subsist in the attribute, leading to a reversal of roles: the attribute would become the essence and vice versa, and distinct attributes like knowledge and power would become interchangeable, all absurd. Thus, affirming distinct eternal attributes leads to impossible consequences, so it must be false. This argument attacks the very notion of multiple eternal entities sharing in qidam.

Key Terms
قدم
وصف ثبوتي
تسلسل
مثلين
تعدد الآلهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God is eternal.
Correction: No, the argument does not deny God's eternity; it shows that positing multiple entities (essence and attributes) sharing eternity leads to logical impossibilities.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument conclude if the essence and attributes are not distinguished after sharing in qidam?
A) They are composite.
B) They are identical, leading to multiple gods or interchangeability.
C) They are both necessary.
D) The attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
Sharing qidam between essence and attributes forces a choice between infinite regress and polytheism, both impossible, so the premise of eternal attributes is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that attributing eternity to anything besides Allah is impossible, what in your life—perhaps a goal, a relationship, or a possession—do you treat as if it will never end? Name it now, and commit to shifting your hope entirely to the Eternal One.
?
The Mu'tazila argue that failing to distinguish essence from attributes leads to polytheism. In your daily worship, do you ever separate Allah's attributes in your mind, such that you fear His punishment without hope in His mercy, or rely on His mercy while ignoring His justice? How can you unify your understanding of His names in your heart starting today?
?
This argument shows that even subtle logical errors in theology can lead to absurdity. Is there a belief about Allah that you hold without evidence or blindly follow from culture? Ask yourself: 'Have I truly examined this belief against the Quran and sound reason?' Then make a concrete plan to study one attribute of Allah deeply this week.

The Fifth Mu'tazila Argument: Distinct Attributes Imply Multiple Eternal Beings
Mu'tazila argue that distinct attributes imply multiple eternal entities, which is kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that distinct attributes would lead to multiple eternal beings, and why it is considered kufr by consensus.

Core Idea
Distinct attributes as separate entities would mean multiple eternals, which contradicts tawhid.

The Mu'tazila presented several arguments against the Ash'ari doctrine of distinct attributes. The fifth argument is a sharp reductio ad absurdum based on the consensus of Muslims. It claims that if God's attributes are distinct entities co-eternal with His essence, then there would be multiple eternal beings, which is tantamount to polytheism. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the argument is presented as a conditional statement (if...then...).
2
Identify the logical structure
Break the argument into three parts: the premise (if God knows by knowledge, power by power), the consequence (then there would be distinct existents), and the conclusion (multiple eternal beings, which is kufr).
The consensus (ijma') is used as a decisive authority.
3
Reflect on the consensus argument
Consider why the Mu'tazila rely on consensus. In Islamic theology, consensus of the ummah is a source of proof. They argue that the doctrine of distinct attributes contradicts what Muslims have always believed about tawhid.
Think about how the Ash'ari would respond to this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشبهة الخامسة : لو كان الله تعالى عالما بالعلم قادرًا بالقدرة لكان علمه وقدرته وحياته وذاته موجودات متغايرة، فيكون هذا قولاً بقدماء متغايرة، وذلك كفر بإجماع المسلمين.
The fifth argument: If God were knowing through knowledge, powerful through power, then His knowledge, power, life, and essence would be distinct existents, and this would be a statement of multiple eternal beings (qudama' mutaghayira), and that is disbelief (kufr) by consensus of the Muslims.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This is the fifth Mu'tazilite argument, a short yet powerful one. It is a reductio based on religious consensus. The argument states: If God knows by a distinct attribute 'knowledge' and is powerful by a distinct attribute 'power', then divine attributes such as knowledge, power, life, and the essence itself would be distinct existents (موجودات متغايرة). That would mean there are multiple eternal entities (قدماء متغايرة). By the consensus (إجماع) of Muslims, affirming multiple co-eternal beings is tantamount to disbelief (كفر), as it resembles polytheism. Therefore, the doctrine of distinct attributes is rejected. This argument appeals to the theological orthodoxy that there is only one eternal being, God, and any addition would compromise divine unity (توحيد).

Key Terms
علم
قدرة
حياة
موجودات متغايرة
قدماء متغايرة
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God has any attributes.
Correction: This argument does not deny that God has attributes; it denies that they are separate entities co-eternal with the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence the fifth argument draws from distinct attributes?
A) God becomes composite.
B) There would be multiple eternal beings, which is kufr by consensus.
C) Attributes would be temporal.
D) God would not be knowing or powerful.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's fifth argument claims that distinct attributes would necessitate multiple eternal beings, rendering the belief in attributes kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that your understanding of Allah's attributes could, even unknowingly, resemble the beliefs of those who compromise tawhid? What steps will you take to ensure your theology is pure and based on the Quran and Sunnah?

?
This lesson shows that theological precision can be a matter of faith and disbelief. When you learn about Allah, do you actively verify each concept against revelation, or do you passively accept ideas that feel rational? What change will you make today to be more diligent in seeking correct knowledge?

The Sixth Mu'tazila Argument: Analogy with Christian Trinity
Mu'tazila argue that affirming attributes is worse than Christian trinitarianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's sixth argument against divine attributes, which draws an analogy with the Christian Trinity to argue that affirming attributes leads to greater multiplicity than Christian trinitarianism.

Core Idea
Affirming attributes is worse than Trinity according to Mu'tazila.

Imam al-Razi presents the sixth and final Mu'tazilite argument, which uses the Qur'an's condemnation of Christians as a reductio. The Mu'tazila argue that if Christians were condemned for affirming distinct attributes in the Trinity, then Muslims who affirm eight attributes are even more culpable. This argument aims to show that affirming attributes leads to polytheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the disjunctive structure (قسم) that sets up two alternatives.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possible reasons for God's condemnation of Christians: either for affirming three eternal self-subsisting entities, or for affirming an essence with distinct attributes. Find them.
3
Understand why the first is rejected
The first alternative is false because Christians do not affirm three independent eternal entities; they affirm a Trinity within one essence.
This is a key point in the argument.
4
See the conclusion
The argument concludes that the condemnation is for affirming an essence with distinct attributes, then extends it to Muslim attribute affirmers, claiming they affirm nine things (essence + eight attributes), making their disbelief three times greater.
5
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider whether this analogy is valid from a Sunni perspective. The common misunderstanding is that it equates all attribute affirmers with Christians, but it is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة : أن الله تعالى قد كَفَّرَ النصارى في قوله تعالى: ﴿لَقَدْ كَفَرَ الَّذِينَ قالوا إن الله ثَالِثُ ثَلَاثَةُ﴾ [المائدة : ٧٣] فلا يخلو إما أن يقال : إنه تعالى كفرهم لأنهم بسبب أثبتوا ذواتا ثلاثة قديمة قائمة بأنفسها ، أو لأنهم أثبتوا ذاتاً موصوفة بصفات متباينة. والأول باطل، لأن النصارى لا يثبتون ذواتا ثلاثة قديمة قائمة بأنفسها، ولما لم يقولوا بذلك استحال أن يكفرهم الله ذلك، و لما بطل القسم الأول ثبت القسم الثاني، وهو أنه تعالى إنما كفرهم لأنهم أثبتوا ذواتا موصوفة بصفات متباينة، ولما كفر النصارى لأجل أنهم أثبتوا صفات ثلاثة فمن أثبت الذات الصفات مع الثمانية فقد أثبت تسعة أشياء، وكان كفره أعظم من كفر النصارى بثلاث مرات. هذا مجموع شبه المعتزلة في نفي الصفات
The sixth argument: God indeed declared the Christians disbelievers in His saying: 'They have certainly disbelieved who say that Allah is the third of three' (Qur'an 5:73). So it must be considered: either He declared them disbelievers because they affirmed three eternal self-subsisting entities, or because they affirmed an essence described by distinct attributes. The first is false because the Christians do not affirm three eternal self-subsisting entities. Since they did not say that, it is impossible that God declared them disbelievers for that. And when the first alternative is false, the second is established, i.e., He declared them disbelievers because they affirmed an essence described by distinct attributes. Since the Christians were declared disbelievers because they affirmed three attributes, whoever affirms the essence and eight attributes has affirmed nine things, and his disbelief would be three times greater than that of the Christians. This is the sum total of the Mu'tazilite arguments for negating attributes.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This is the sixth and final Mu'tazilite argument, based on an analogy with Christian trinitarianism. It is a deductive argument from the Qur'anic condemnation of Christians. The argument proceeds by a disjunction (قسم): God's condemnation of Christians must be either because they believed in three eternal self-subsisting entities (ذوات ثلاثة قديمة قائمة بأنفسها) or because they believed in one essence described by distinct attributes (ذاتاً موصوفة بصفات متباينة). The first is false because Christians do not affirm three independent gods; they affirm a Trinity of persons within one essence. So the second must be the reason: God condemned them for affirming an essence with distinct attributes (three hypostases: Father, Son, Holy Spirit). Now, if Christians are condemned for affirming three attributes, then Muslims who affirm the essential attributes (knowledge, power, life, will, hearing, sight, speech, and others) would be affirming even more. The author mentions eight attributes (the classical seven plus perhaps one more, e.g., will, etc.), so if the essence plus eight attributes gives nine entities, the believer would be guilty of a greater polytheism than Christians. This is a powerful reductio appealing to the severity of shirk. The passage concludes with the phrase 'هذا مجموع شبه المعتزلة في نفي الصفات' summarizing the entire set of Mu'tazilite arguments.

Key Terms
تكفير النصارى
ذوات ثلاثة
صفات متباينة
إثبات الصفات
تعدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument equates all attribute affirmers with Christians.
Correction: It uses the Christian case as a reductio to show the danger of multiplicity, not to equate beliefs.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, why did God declare Christians disbelievers?
A) Because they believed in three independent gods.
B) Because they believed in an essence with distinct attributes.
C) Because they denied God's attributes.
D) Because they affirmed eight attributes.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's sixth argument uses the condemnation of Christians to argue that affirming multiple attributes leads to greater polytheism than the Christian Trinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that how you understand God’s attributes might lead to subtle associations? What steps can you take today to ensure your belief remains pure tawheed?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that affirming attributes multiplies entities. In your own worship, do you ever feel a distance between God's essence and His attributes? How can you unite them in your heart?
?
If someone today accused you of shirk for affirming eight attributes, would you be able to respond with clarity? What do you need to learn or unlearn to defend the truth with conviction?

The Argument Against Shared Eternity of Essence and Attributes
Imam al-Razi presents a reductio argument against distinct eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's reductio argument that if the divine essence and attributes share eternity, then either composition and infinite regress or polytheism and absurdity follow, thereby refuting the view of distinct eternal attributes.

Core Idea
Shared eternity between essence and attributes leads to impossibility.

Imam al-Razi presents a powerful logical argument against the claim that both the divine essence and attributes are eternal and distinct. Building on the premise that non-existence is real and existence is real, he shows that if they share the attribute of eternity, then either they must be distinguished by something else—leading to infinite regress—or they are not distinguished, resulting in polytheism. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of shared eternity
The argument begins with the idea that both essence and attributes share the attribute of eternity (al-qidam). Recognize that this is the starting point for the reductio.
2
Analyze the two branches of the distinction
The argument presents a dilemma: either the essence and attributes are distinguished by another consideration or they are not. Follow each branch to its contradictory conclusion.
3
See the final conclusion
Both branches lead to impossibility: infinite regress or polytheism/absurdity. Thus, the original assumption that essence and attributes are both eternal and distinct must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابق، ونفي العدم ثبوت فإذن الذات والصفات تشتركان في هذا الوصف الثبوتي المسمى بالقدم، فإما أن يتميز الذات عن الصفة باعتبار آخر أو لا يتميز، فإن تميز فما به المشاركة غير ما به المباينة، فيكون كل واحد من الذات والصفات متركبا من هذين الاعتبارين - أعني ما به يشتركان وما به يتباينان - ثم إن كل واحد من ذينك الاعتبارين لا بد أن يكون أيضًا قديماً؛ لأنه جزء جزء القديم، وإذا اشترك ذانك الجزآن في القدم فلا بد وأن يتباينا باعتبار آخر، وحينئذ يتركب كل واحد من ذينك الجزأين من جزأين آخرين ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال. وأما إن قلنا بأن الذات والصفات بعد اشتراكهما في القدم لا يتميز كل واحد الآخر بماهية مخصوصة فحينئذ تكون الذات والصفة مثلين ولما كانت الذات إلها وجب أن تكون الصفة إلهاً فيكون هذا قولا بتعدد الآلهة. وأيضًا إذا كانت الذوات والصفات متماثلين لم يكن قيام أحدهما بالآخر أولى من قيام الآخر به، فتكون الذات صفة، والصفة ذاتا، والعلم قدرة، والقدرة علمًا، وكل ذلك محال ولما كان القول بإثبات الصفة القديمة مفضيّا إلى المحال، وجب أن يكون القول به محالا.
The previous [argument] established that non-existence is [real] and existence is [real], so the essence and the attributes share in this existential description called eternity. Then either the essence is distinguished from the attribute by another consideration or it is not. If it is distinguished, then that by which they participate is other than that by which they differ; thus each of the essence and the attributes becomes composed of these two considerations—namely, that by which they share and that by which they differ. Then each of those two considerations must also be eternal, because it is a part of the eternal. And when those two parts share in eternity, they must necessarily differ by another consideration, and then each of those two parts becomes composed of two other parts, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible. And if we say that the essence and the attributes after their sharing in eternity are not distinguished each from the other by a specific quiddity, then the essence and the attribute would be like two similar things. And since the essence is God, the attribute must also be God, and this would be a statement affirming multiple gods. Also, if the essences and attributes were similar, then one's subsisting in the other would not be more appropriate than the other's subsisting in it; thus the essence would be an attribute, the attribute an essence, knowledge would be power, power would be knowledge—all of which is impossible. And since the statement affirming the eternal attribute leads to impossibility, the statement affirming it must be impossible.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the view that the divine essence and attributes are both eternal while being distinct. The author argues that if essence and attributes share the attribute of eternity (al-qidam), then either they are distinguished by something else or they are not. If they are distinguished, then each would be composed of a common factor (eternity) and a distinguishing factor, and those factors would themselves need to be eternal, leading to an infinite regress. If they are not distinguished, then they would be identical, and since the essence is God, the attribute would also be God—resulting in polytheism. Additionally, if they were similar, the relation of subsistence would be reversible, causing absurdities like knowledge being power. Thus, the claim of distinct eternal attributes is impossible.

Key Terms
ذات
صفة
قدم
تسلسل
مثلان
مشاركة
مباينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this argument denies the attributes entirely.
Correction: The argument only refutes the view that they are distinct eternal entities co-existing with the essence, not the existence of attributes per se.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what follows if the essence and attributes share in eternity but are distinguished by another consideration?
A) They would be identical
B) They would be composed of common and distinguishing factors, leading to infinite regress
C) They would be similar without distinction
D) The attributes would be created

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi's argument shows that positing distinct eternal attributes alongside the essence leads to either infinite regress or polytheism, both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This argument forces you to confront the impossibility of multiple eternal beings. How does this protect your heart from any inclination to associate partners with Allah, even in thought?
?
If you have ever entertained the idea that Allah's attributes could be separate, what does this argument show you about the danger of such thinking, and how will you guard your heart from it?

Fifth Objection: Multiplicity of Eternals
Imam al-Razi records the fifth Mu'tazili objection against eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili objection that affirming distinct eternal attributes leads to belief in multiple eternal entities, which they consider disbelief.

Core Idea
Distinct eternal attributes imply multiple eternal entities – a charge of disbelief.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God's attributes are distinct and eternal, then we end up with multiple eternal things. This, they claimed, contradicts the Islamic belief in the oneness of God. Let's read their fifth objection as recorded by Imam al-Razi.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If God were knowing by knowledge... then His knowledge, power, life, and essence would be distinct existents.'
The Mu'tazila equate 'distinct existents' with 'multiple eternal entities'.
2
Identify the conclusion
The objection concludes that this would be 'disbelief by consensus of the Muslims.' Ask yourself: what premise leads them to that conclusion?
3
Reflect on the Ash'ari response
Although not given here, Ash'aris distinguish between essential attributes and the Essence. Consider how that distinction might answer the objection.
The Ash'ari view is that attributes are neither identical to the essence nor separate existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشبهة الخامسة : لو كان الله تعالى عالما بالعلم قادرًا بالقدرة لكان علمه وقدرته وحياته وذاته موجودات متغايرة، فيكون هذا قولاً بقدماء متغايرة، وذلك كفر بإجماع المسلمين. الشبهة السادسة : أن الله تعالى قد كَفَّرَ النصارى في قوله تعالى: ﴿لَقَدْ كَفَرَ الَّذِينَ قالوا إن الله ثَالِثُ ثَلَاثَةٍ﴾ [المائدة : ٧٣] فلا يخلو إما أن يقال : إنه تعالى كفرهم لأنهم أثبتوا ذواتا ثلاثة قديمة قائمة بأنفسها ، أو لأنهم أثبتوا ذاتاً موصوفة بصفات متباينة. والأول باطل، لأن النصارى لا يثبتون ذواتا ثلاثة قديمة قائمة بأنفسها، ولما لم يقولوا بذلك استحال أن يكفرهم الله ذلك، و لما بطل القسم الأول ثبت القسم الثاني، وهو أنه تعالى إنما كفرهم لأنهم أثبتوا ذاتاً موصوفة بصفات متباينة، ولما كفر النصارى لأجل أنهم أثبتوا صفات ثلاثة فمن أثبت الذات الصفات مع الثمانية فقد أثبت تسعة أشياء، وكان كفره أعظم من كفر النصارى بثلاث مرات. هذا مجموع شبه المعتزلة في نفي الصفات
The fifth objection: If God were knowing by knowledge and powerful by power, then His knowledge, power, life, and essence would be distinct existents, and this would be a statement affirming multiple eternal entities, and that is disbelief by consensus of the Muslims. The sixth objection: God has declared the Christians disbelievers in His statement: 'Indeed, they have disbelieved who say, God is the third of three' [5:73]. It is not free from either being said that He declared them disbelievers because they affirmed three eternal, self-subsistent entities, or because they affirmed an essence described with distinct attributes. The first is false, because the Christians do not affirm three eternal, self-subsistent entities, and since they did not say that, it is impossible that God declared them disbelievers for that. And when the first division is invalidated, the second is established: that He declared them disbelievers only because they affirmed an essence described with distinct attributes. And since the Christians were declared disbelievers because they affirmed three attributes, then whoever affirms the essence and the attributes with the eight [attributes] has affirmed nine things, and his disbelief is three times greater than the disbelief of the Christians. This is the sum of the Mu'tazilite objections in negating the attributes.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage records two specific objections (shubha) from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari position of eternal attributes. The fifth objection argues that if God is knowing by a distinct knowledge, powerful by a distinct power, etc., then we have multiple eternal entities (qidamā' mutaghāyira), which constitutes disbelief. The sixth objection uses the Quranic condemnation of Christians who said God is one of three. The Mu'tazila reason that since Christians are condemned for affirming distinct attributes of a single essence (not three distinct essences), then Muslims who affirm eight attributes plus the essence are equally guilty, and worse. The passage ends by noting these are their general objections against the attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ash'aris believe in many gods or eternal entities separate from God.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that divine attributes are not separate existents; they are modes of the essence, neither identical nor distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth objection, what would be the consequence of God being knowing by knowledge and powerful by power?
A) God would be more perfect
B) Knowledge, power, life, and essence would be distinct existents
C) Muslims would agree with the Christians
D) God would be one

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that distinct eternal attributes imply multiple eternal entities, which they equate with disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If asked by a friend, 'Doesn't describing God with many attributes make Him seem like multiple beings?' could you explain the correct view based on this lesson? If not, what steps will you take to be able to defend this aspect of tawheed? Set a specific plan to learn.

Sixth Objection: The Christian Analogy
Imam al-Razi records the sixth Mu'tazili objection using the Christian analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use the Quranic condemnation of Christians to argue that affirming distinct attributes is worse than the Christian heresy.

Core Idea
The Christian analogy: affirming eight distinct attributes is three times worse than the Christian three.

The Mu'tazila cleverly invoke the Quranic verse condemning Christians who say God is one of three. They argue that the condemnation applies to anyone who affirms distinct attributes of a single essence, and that Ash'aris, with eight attributes, are even more guilty. Let's explore their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the full objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two-part logical division: either Christians are condemned for believing in three self-subsistent entities, or for believing in one essence with distinct attributes.
2
Follow the elimination
The first option is rejected because Christians do not believe in three self-subsistent gods. So the second option must be the reason.
The Mu'tazila assume the Quranic condemnation is for the latter.
3
Calculate the conclusion
If Christians are condemned for three attributes (Father, Son, Holy Spirit), then Muslims with eight attributes plus essence (nine things) are three times more deserving of condemnation.
The number eight refers to the standard Ash'ari list of attributes.
4
Reflect on the response
Ash'aris would deny that their attributes are similar to the Christian persons. Consider how they might distinguish between essential attributes and hypostases.
