Refuting the Argument from Similarity of Knowledge
Imam al-Razi presents and refutes the first Mu'tazili objection that knowledge implies similarity with human knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili objection that if God's knowledge is distinct, it would be similar to human knowledge, and how Imam al-Razi refutes it by distinguishing the mode of relation.

Core Idea
God's knowledge relates as a knower, not as an object known.

Imam al-Razi now addresses a specific objection from the Mu'tazila against the attribute of knowledge. They argue that if God had a distinct knowledge, it would be like ours, leading to absurd consequences. The response hinges on the mode of relation: God's knowledge is essential, ours accidental.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the Mu'tazili argument: if God knows by a distinct knowledge, then His knowledge relates to the same object in the same way as ours, making them similar.
Notice the logical chain: similarity leads to shared temporality/eternity, which is impossible.
2
Understand the refutation's key distinction
Imam al-Razi distinguishes between the mode of relation: God's knowledge relates in the mode of the knower (ʿālimiyya), while human knowledge relates in the mode of being known (maʿlūm). This avoids similarity.
This distinction prevents the objection from applying even to the position that God knows without a distinct attribute.
3
Apply the distinction to the objection
If God had a distinct knowledge, His knowledge would relate in the mode of the known (like ours), leading to similarity. But since the Ashʿarī view holds that God's knowledge is an essential attribute relating as a knower, the objection fails.
The refutation cleverly turns the objection into a proof for the uniqueness of divine knowledge.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Understand why the Mu'tazili argument fails: it assumes a false similarity in modes of relation, ignoring the ontological difference between essential and accidental knowledge.
This is a classic example of theological reasoning distinguishing divine and human attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما شبههم في نفي صفة العلم خاصة فمن وجوه : الشبهة الأولى : لو كان عالما بالعلم لكان علمه إذا تعلق بشيء يكون ذلك الشيء متعلق علمنا، ومتعلق علم الله تعالى من وجه واحد ومن طريقة واحدة وكل علمين كذلك، فهما مثلان فيلزم أن يكون علم الله وعلمنا مثلين، فيلزم من حدوث علمنا حدوث علم الله تعالى ومن قدم علم الله تعالى قدم علمنا، ولما بطل الوجهان علمنا أنه تعالى عالم لا بالعلم. لا يقال هذا لازم على نفاة الصفات في العالمية لأنا نقول : احترزنا عن هذا بقولنا : إن العلمين يتعلقان بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد على طريقة واحدة، وهذا غير لازم في كونه تعالى عالما؛ لأن علمه تعالى يتعلق بذالك المعلوم تعلق العالمية لا تعلق العلوم، وعلمنا لا يتعلق به تعلق العالمية بل تعلق المعلوم، فقد اختلفت الطريقة، أما إذا كان تعالى عالما بالعلم تعلق علمه بذلك المعلوم، وتعلق علمنا أيضًا تعلق المعلوم، فكان تعلق كل واحد منهما به على طريقة واحدة فيلزم التماثل، فظهر الفرق.
As for their specific objections in negating the attribute of knowledge, they are from [several] aspects: The first objection: If He were knowing by knowledge, then when His knowledge is related to a thing, that thing would be the object of our knowledge as well, and the object of God's knowledge is from one aspect and one mode, and every two [pieces of] knowledge are like that, so they would be similar. This would necessitate that God's knowledge and our knowledge are similar, and thus from the temporality of our knowledge the temporality of God's knowledge would follow, and from the eternity of God's knowledge the eternity of our knowledge would follow. Since both possibilities are invalid, we know that He is knowing without [a distinct] knowledge. It cannot be said that this [objection] is binding upon the negaters of attributes regarding the attribute of 'knowing', for we say: We avoided this by our statement: The two [pieces of] knowledge are related to one known object from one aspect and one mode. And this is not necessary in His being knowing, because His knowledge relates to that known object with the relation of the knower (al-ʿālimiyya), not the relation of being known (al-ʿulūm), while our knowledge does not relate to it with the relation of the knower but with the relation of being known. Thus the mode differs. But if He were knowing by knowledge, His knowledge would relate to that known object, and our knowledge would also relate with the relation of being known, so the relation of each to it would be in one mode, necessitating similarity. So the difference appears.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents a specific Mu'tazili objection against the attribute of knowledge (ʿilm) and its refutation. The objection argues that if God knows by a distinct knowledge, then His knowledge and our knowledge would both relate to the same object in the same way, making them similar. Since our knowledge is temporal, God's would be temporal, and since God's is eternal, ours would be eternal—both impossible, so God knows without a distinct knowledge. The refutation responds by distinguishing the mode of relation: God's knowledge relates to objects in the mode of the knower (ʿālimiyya), i.e., as an essential attribute of the knower, while our knowledge relates in the mode of being known (maʿlūm), i.e., as an acquired accident. Thus they are not similar. However, if one posits a distinct knowledge in God, that knowledge would relate in the same mode as ours, leading to similarity. Hence the argument fails.

العلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God has a distinct attribute of knowledge, it must be like human knowledge in every respect.
Correction: God's knowledge relates in the mode of the knower (essential attribute), while human knowledge relates in the mode of being known (accident). Thus they are not similar.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation distinguish God's knowledge from human knowledge in terms of relation to the known object?
A) God's knowledge relates as an accident, human knowledge as a substance
B) God's knowledge relates in the mode of the knower, human knowledge in the mode of being known
C) God's knowledge is limited, human knowledge is infinite
D) God's knowledge is created, human knowledge is eternal

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili objection fails because it ignores the fundamental difference in the mode of relation between divine and human knowledge.

?
The Mu'tazili objection assumed that if God knew like we do, then His knowledge would be temporal. But al-Razi refuted that by distinguishing the mode. Have you ever treated God's knowledge as if it were just a larger version of human knowledge? Second Mu'tazili Objection: God's Knowledge Cannot Be One
Imam al-Razi presents the second Mu'tazili objection that knowing infinite objects requires either one knowledge, finite, or infinite knowledges—all impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili trichotomy and the three reasons they give against the possibility of God knowing all objects by a single knowledge.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that one knowledge cannot encompass infinite objects—otherwise absurd consequences follow.

Imam al-Razi presents the second Mu'tazili objection against the attribute of knowledge. They argue that God's knowledge of infinite objects cannot be a single knowledge, nor finite knowledges, nor infinite knowledges. The passage focuses on refuting the first possibility with three distinct arguments from analogy and logical consequence. Understanding these objections is crucial for grasping the dialectical context of the Ash'ari position on divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the trichotomy
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection begins by stating a trichotomy: God's knowledge of infinite objects must be either one knowledge, finite knowledges, or infinite knowledges.
The passage argues all three are impossible; only the first is fully argued here.
2
Examine each of the three reasons against one knowledge
The passage gives three distinct reasons why one knowledge cannot encompass all objects. For each reason, identify the core analogy or logical move.
Reason 1: epistemic asymmetry. Reason 2: analogy with witnessed knowledge of colors. Reason 3: slippery slope to infinite relations.
3
Note the incomplete argument against finite knowledges
The passage begins to argue against finite knowledges but cuts off. Consider what the missing part might likely say based on the pattern of reasoning.
Finite knowledges cannot encompass infinite objects—that is the obvious completion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية: لا شك أنه تعالى عالم بالمعلومات التي لا نهاية لها، فإما أن يعلم كل تلك المعلومات بعلم واحد أو بعلوم متناهية أو بعلوم غير متناهية؟ والكل باطل فبطل القول بكونه تعالى عالما بالعلم. إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يعلم الكل بعلم واحد من وجوه : الأول : أنه يصح ان يعلم كونه عالما بأحد المعلومين، مع الشك في كونه تعالى عالما بالمعلوم الآخر، والمعلوم غير ما هو غير معلوم. الثاني: أن العلم المتعلق بالسواد مخالف للعلم المتعلق بالبياض في الشاهد، فلو لا جاز تعلق العلم في الغائب بالمعلومات الكثيرة لكان ذلك العلم قائماً مقام العلوم في الشاهد، وإذا جاز كون الشيء الواحد قائما مقام الأشياء المختلفة فلم يجوز قيام الصفة الواحدة مقام الصفات المختلفة، حتى تثبت لله تعالى صفة واحدة تكون علما وقدرة وحياة ؟ بل لم لا يجوز أن تكون ذاته قائمة مقام الذات، ومقام جملة الصفات، وحينئذ يلزمكم نفي الصفات ؟ الثالث : أنه لو جاز تعلق العلم الواحد بالمعلومين لم يكن تعلقه بمعلومين أولى تعلقه بثلاثة أو أربعة فيفضي ذلك على تعلقه بمعلومات لا نهاية لها في من وكل ذلك محال. فثبت بهذه الوجوه أنه تعالى يمتنع كونه عالما بكل المعلومات بعلم واحد. وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يعلم هذه المعلومات بعلوم متناهية لأن المتناهي إذا الشاهد،
The second objection: There is no doubt that He is knowing of the infinite objects of knowledge. So either He knows all those objects of knowledge by one knowledge, or by finite knowledges, or by infinite knowledges? All are invalid, so the statement that He is knowing by knowledge is invalid. We say: It is not permissible that He knows all by one knowledge, from [several] aspects: First: It is valid that He knows that He is knowing of one of the two objects while being doubtful about His being knowing of the other object, and the known object is other than what is not known. Second: Knowledge related to blackness is different from knowledge related to whiteness in the witnessed [realm]. So if it were not permissible for knowledge in the unseen to relate to many objects, that knowledge would stand in place of the [multiple] knowledges in the witnessed realm. And if it is permissible for one thing to stand in place of different things, why is it not permissible for one attribute to stand in place of different attributes, so that you affirm for God one attribute that is knowledge, power, and life? Rather, why is it not permissible that His essence stands in place of the essence and the place of the entire attributes? Then you would be compelled to negate the attributes. Third: If it were permissible for one knowledge to be related to two objects, its relation to two would not be more appropriate than its relation to three or four, and that would lead to its relation to infinite objects, and all that is impossible. So by these aspects it is established that it is impossible for Him to be knowing of all objects of knowledge by one knowledge. And we say: It is not permissible that He knows these objects by finite knowledges, because the finite when... the witnessed [realm].
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents the second specific Mu'tazili objection against the attribute of knowledge. It poses a trichotomy: God knows infinite objects either by one knowledge, by finite knowledges, or by infinite knowledges. All three are deemed impossible. The refutation of the first possibility (one knowledge) is given in three reasons: (1) if one knowledge suffices, then knowing one object would entail knowing all, which is false; (2) in the witnessed world, knowledge of black differs from knowledge of white, so one knowledge cannot replace many; if it could, then one attribute could replace all attributes, leading to the very position the Mu'tazila reject; (3) if one knowledge could relate to two, there is no reason to stop at two, leading to infinite objects, which is impossible. The refutation of the second possibility (finite knowledges) is cut off, but the argument would likely be that finite knowledge cannot encompass the infinite. The text breaks off, leaving the objection incomplete.

Key Terms
معلومات
علم واحد
علوم متناهية
علوم غير متناهية
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three reasons prove that God cannot have a single omniscient knowledge.
Correction: These are Mu'tazili objections; Ash'aris affirm a single omniscient knowledge that is not bound by human analogies. The objections are presented as part of the dialectical context, not as proofs of impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, what is the third reason against one knowledge encompassing all objects?
A) It would make God doubtful about some objects
B) It would lead to an infinite regress of knowledges
C) If one knowledge can relate to two objects, it could relate to infinite objects, which is impossible
D) It would contradict the witnessed world where knowledge of black differs from knowledge of white

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili objection claims that one knowledge cannot cover infinite objects, using three analogies from the witnessed world and a slippery slope argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Mu'tazili argument that one knowledge cannot encompass infinite objects, do you feel a flicker of doubt about the simplicity and perfection of God's omniscience? This lesson exposes that temptation. Will you consciously affirm that God's knowledge is not like human knowledge, even when your mind struggles to conceive it?
?
The passage argues from analogies in the witnessed world—like knowledge of black being different from knowledge of white. Have you, in your own life, limited God's knowledge by projecting human limitations onto Him? Today, in what specific situation might you be guilty of thinking God cannot fully know your struggles because they are too complex?
?
The third argument warns that if one knowledge can know two objects, there is no stopping at two—leading to infinite. This is a logical trap. Yet, do you sometimes doubt that God can truly know every detail of your life simultaneously? In prayer, do you act as if you need to remind God of things, as if His knowledge is limited? This lesson calls you to repent of such underestimation.

Response to the Objection on Necessary Likeness
Razi responds to an objection about necessary likeness between God's knowledge and ours.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the distinction between divine and human knowledge by differentiating the mode of relation ('ālimiyya vs ma'lūm), and thus preserves God's knowledge as unique without anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
God's knowledge relates to things as the Knower; ours relates as the known. No likeness follows.

In the previous lesson, Razi argued that God knows without a knowledge (عالم لا بالعلم). Now he faces an objection: if God's knowledge and our knowledge both relate to the same object, they must be similar, leading to absurdities. Razi responds by showing that the mode of relation is fundamentally different: God's knowledge relates as the attribute of 'being knowing' (تعلق العالمية), while ours relates as the relation of 'being known' (تعلق المعلوم). This distinction prevents any necessary likeness.

Guided Path
1
Restate the objection
Understand the objection: if two knowledges relate to the same object in the same way, they are similar. Why would this be problematic for Razi's position?
The objection targets the earlier proof that God knows without a knowledge.
2
Identify Razi's key distinction
Notice that Razi introduces two modes: تعلق العالمية (relation of being knowing) and تعلق المعلوم (relation of being known). How does this block the similarity argument?
3
See the consequence if God had a knowledge
Consider the hypothetical: if God were knowing through a knowledge (بالعلم), then both relations would be the same (تعلق المعلوم), forcing similarity. Razi uses this to reinforce his original claim.
This shows that the objector's scenario would only apply if God had a knowledge, which Razi denies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيء متعلق علمنا، ومتعلق علم الله تعالى من وجه واحد ومن طريقة واحدة وكل علمين كذلك، فهما مثلان فيلزم أن يكون علم الله وعلمنا مثلين، فيلزم من حدوث علمنا حدوث علم الله تعالى ومن قدم علم الله تعالى قدم علمنا، ولما بطل الوجهان علمنا أنه تعالى عالم لا بالعلم. لا يقال هذا لازم على نفاة الصفات في العالمية لأنا نقول : احترزنا عن هذا بقولنا : إن العلمين يتعلقان بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد على طريقة واحدة، وهذا غير لازم في كونه تعالى عالماً؛ لأن علمه تعالى يتعلق بذلك المعلوم تعلق العالمية لا تعلق العلوم، وعلمنا لا يتعلق به تعلق العالمية بل تعلق المعلوم، فقد اختلفت الطريقة، أما إذا كان تعالى عالماً بالعلم تعلق علمه بذلك المعلوم، وتعلق علمنا أيضاً تعلق المعلوم، فكان تعلق كل واحد منهما به على طريقة واحدة فيلزم التماثل، فظهر الفرق.
The thing is the object of our knowledge, and the object of the knowledge of God Most High from one aspect and from one mode, and every two knowledges are like that, so they are similar. Then it would follow that the knowledge of God and our knowledge are similar, and then it would follow from the temporality of our knowledge the temporality of God's knowledge, and from the pre-eternity of God's knowledge the pre-eternity of our knowledge. And since both implications are false, we know that He Most High is knowing without a knowledge. It cannot be said: This is incumbent upon the negators of attributes in the case of being knowing, for we say: We guarded against this by our statement: The two knowledges relate to one known thing from one aspect and in one mode. And this is not necessary in His being Most High knowing, because His knowledge Most High relates to that known thing with the relation of being knowing (tā'alluq al-'ālimiyya), not the relation of knowledge (tā'alluq al-'ulūm), and our knowledge does not relate to it with the relation of being knowing but rather with the relation of being known (tā'alluq al-ma'lūm). So the mode has differed. But if He Most High were knowing through a knowledge, His knowledge would relate to that known thing, and our knowledge would also relate with the relation of being known. Then the relation of each of them to it would be in one mode, and similarity would follow. So the difference appears.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection that the earlier proof (that God knows without a knowledge) entails a necessary likeness between God's knowledge and created knowledge, leading to absurdities. Razi explains that the objection fails because the mode of attribution is different: God's knowledge relates to the known as the knowledge of the knower (تعلق العالمية), while our knowledge relates to the known as the knowledge of the known (تعلق المعلوم). Since the modes differ, no likeness follows. However, if God were knowing through a knowledge, both would relate in the same mode (تعلق المعلوم), and then likeness would be necessary. Thus the distinction is preserved.

Key Terms
تعلق العالمية
تعلق المعلوم
تماثل
عالم لا بالعلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two knowledges that relate to the same object must be similar, regardless of the mode of relation.
Correction: Razi shows that similarity depends on the mode of relation: God's knowledge relates as 'being knowing' (تعلق العالمية), while ours relates as 'being known' (تعلق المعلوم). Since these differ, no likeness follows.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes God's knowledge from human knowledge in Razi's response?
A) God's knowledge is eternal while ours is temporal.
B) God's knowledge relates as 'being knowing' while ours relates as 'being known'.
C) God knows all things while we know only some.
D) God's knowledge is simple while ours is complex.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends God's knowledge as unique by distinguishing the mode of relation ('ālimiyya) from that of created knowledge (ma'lūm), preventing necessary likeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If God's knowledge is not like yours—if He does not 'process' information or 'observe' as a creature does—what does that mean for your accountability? Are you tempted to think that because His knowledge is different, He might 'overlook' or 'forget'? Confront that assumption now.
?
Razi argues that if God's knowledge were like ours, it would entail absurdities. Yet in your daily life, you often treat God as if He thinks and reacts as you do. What specific habit or thought pattern can you commit to changing today to honor the unique reality of His knowledge?

The Trilemma Against Divine Knowledge
Razi presents a trilemma against God knowing through knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's trilemma argument against the view that God knows through a knowledge, and why all three possibilities are impossible.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot be through a knowledge — infinite objects cannot be covered by one, finite, or infinite knowledges.

Razi presents a devastating objection to those who say God knows through a knowledge. He sets up a trilemma: if God knows infinite things, it must be by one knowledge, finite knowledges, or infinite knowledges. Each leads to absurdity. This passage dismantles the first two options thoroughly, then shows the third is metaphysically impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the trilemma introduction
Start by reading the opening lines where Razi lays out the three possibilities. Notice the structure: if God knows infinite things, then one of three cases must hold.
This sets up the logical framework for the entire argument.
2
Examine the three reasons against one knowledge
Razi gives three reasons why one knowledge cannot cover all infinite objects. Read each reason carefully and note how each shows a contradiction or absurd consequence.
The first reason uses doubt, the second uses analogy from the witnessed realm, the third uses slippery slope.
3
Understand the problem with finite knowledges
Razi argues that finite knowledges would require each to know multiple objects, but he has already proven one knowledge can know only one object. This makes finite knowledges impossible.
This step relies on the earlier conclusion that one knowledge cannot know more than one object.
4
Grasp why infinite knowledges are impossible
Razi argues that an infinite number of knowledges is metaphysically impossible because every existing number is finite (subject to increase and decrease). Therefore, infinite knowledges cannot exist.
This is a metaphysical argument about the nature of numbers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية: لا شك أنه تعالى عالم بالمعلومات التي لا نهاية لها، فإما أن يعلم كل تلك المعلومات بعلم واحد أو بعلوم متناهية أو بعلوم غير متناهية؟ والكل باطل فبطل القول بكونه تعالى عالماً بالعلم. إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يعلم الكل بعلم واحد من وجوه : الأول : أنه يصح أن يعلم كونه عالماً بأحد المعلومين، مع الشك في كونه تعالى عالماً بالمعلوم الآخر، والمعلوم غير ما هو غير معلوم. الثاني: أن العلم المتعلق بالسواد مخالف للعلم المتعلق بالبياض في الشاهد، فلو جاز تعلق العلم في الغائب بالمعلومات الكثيرة لكان ذلك العلم قائماً مقام العلوم في الشاهد، وإذا جاز كون الشيء الواحد قائماً مقام الأشياء المختلفة فلم يجوز قيام الصفة الواحدة مقام الصفات المختلفة، حتى تثبت لله تعالى صفة واحدة تكون علماً وقدرة وحياة ؟ بل لم لا يجوز أن تكون ذاته قائمة مقام الذات، ومقام جملة الصفات، وحينئذ يلزمكم نفي الصفات ؟ الثالث : أنه لو جاز تعلق العلم الواحد بالمعلومين لم يكن تعلقه بمعلومين أولى من تعلقه بثلاثة أو أربعة فيفضي ذلك إلى تعلقه بمعلومات لا نهاية لها وكل ذلك محال. فثبت بهذه الوجوه أنه تعالى يمتنع كونه عالماً بكل المعلومات بعلم واحد. وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يعلم هذه المعلومات بعلوم متناهية لأن المتناهي إذا وزع على غير المتناهي لزم أن يكون المعلوم بكل علم أشياء كثيرة، وقد بينا أنه لا يجوز أن يعلم بالعلم الواحد أكثر من معلوم واحد، وإنما قلنا إنه لا يجوز أن يعلم المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها بعلوم لا نهاية لها ; لأن وجود عدد لا نهاية له محال، وذلك لأن كل عدد موجود فهو قابل للزيادة والنقصان وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه، فكل عدد موجود فهو متناه والعدد الذي لا نهاية له يمتنع وجوده، ولما كان كونه تعالى عالماً بالعلم لا بد وأن يكون على أحد هذه الأقسام، ولما ثبت أنها بأسرها باطلة ثبت أن كونه تعالى عالماً بالعلم محال.
The second objection: There is no doubt that He Most High is knowing of the infinite known things. So either He knows all those known things by one knowledge, or by finite knowledges, or by infinite knowledges? All are false, so the statement that He is knowing through a knowledge is false. We said: It is not permissible that He knows all by one knowledge, due to aspects: First: It is correct that He knows that He is knowing one of the two known things, along with doubt about His being Most High knowing the other known thing, and the known is other than what is unknown. Second: The knowledge related to blackness is different from the knowledge related to whiteness in the witnessed realm. So if knowledge in the unseen were allowed to relate to many known things, that knowledge would stand in the place of [many] knowledges in the witnessed realm. And if it is allowed for one thing to stand in the place of different things, why is it not allowed for one attribute to stand in the place of different attributes, such that you affirm for God Most High one attribute that is knowledge, power, and life? Rather, why is it not allowed that His essence stand in the place of the essence and in the place of the sum of attributes? Then it would be incumbent upon you to negate attributes. Third: If it were allowed for one knowledge to relate to two known things, then its relation to two would not be more appropriate than its relation to three or four, and that would lead to its relation to infinite known things, and all of that is impossible. So by these aspects it is established that He Most High cannot be knowing all known things by one knowledge. And we said: It is not permissible that He knows these known things by finite knowledges because when the finite is distributed upon the infinite, it would follow that what is known by each knowledge is many things, and we have already shown that it is not permissible to know by one knowledge more than one known thing. And we said: It is not permissible that He knows the infinite known things by infinite knowledges, because the existence of a number without end is impossible, and that is because every existing number is subject to increase and decrease, and everything that is such is finite. So every existing number is finite, and the number without end cannot exist. And since His being Most High knowing through knowledge must be in one of these divisions, and since they have all been shown to be false, it is established that His being Most High knowing through knowledge is impossible.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents a trilemma against the view that God knows through a knowledge. If God knows infinite things, it must be either (1) by one knowledge, (2) by finite knowledges, or (3) by infinite knowledges. All three are impossible. (1) One knowledge cannot know multiple objects because: (a) one could know one object while doubting another, which is absurd for God; (b) in the witnessed realm, knowledge of blackness differs from knowledge of whiteness, so if one knowledge could cover both, it would replace many knowledges, leading to the absurd consequence that a single attribute could replace all attributes, even God's essence; (c) if one knowledge could know two, it could know three or more ad infinitum, which is impossible. (2) Finite knowledges cannot cover infinite objects because each would have to know many things, but we already proved one knowledge cannot know more than one. (3) An infinite number of knowledges is impossible because any existing number is finite (being subject to increase and decrease). Since all options fail, the premise that God knows through knowledge is false.

Key Terms
علم واحد
علوم متناهية
علوم غير متناهية
شاهد
غائب
عدد لا نهاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's knowledge could be simple yet cover all details.
Correction: Razi argues that simplicity cannot encompass multiplicity in the same mode; a single knowledge cannot cover multiple distinct objects.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with finite knowledges covering infinite objects?
A) Finite knowledges are impossible to exist.
B) Each finite knowledge would have to know many objects, but one knowledge cannot know more than one.
C) Finite knowledges would be temporal.
D) Finite knowledges would require a chain.

Key Takeaway
Razi's trilemma shows that the notion of God knowing through a knowledge leads to contradiction; therefore, God's knowledge must be different from human knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is not like ours, how should that change the way you approach your doubts about His plans? Are you treating His knowledge as limited like yours? What specific doubt will you surrender to Him today?
?
Razi argues that finite knowledge cannot encompass infinite. Do you sometimes act as if you can fully understand God's wisdom? Where in your life are you demanding explanations from Him as if He owes you an account? Will you stop?
?
The trilemma shows that our human categories fail to describe God. What does that mean for your prayer life? Are you praying to a God you think you understand, or to the One who transcends your categories? How will you adjust your worship to acknowledge His absolute transcendence?

The Third Objection: Knowledge Cannot Know Itself
Razi refutes the idea that knowledge can know itself, either directly or by another knowledge, leading to regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that if God knows through a knowledge, that knowledge cannot know itself and cannot be known by another knowledge without leading to infinite regress.

Core Idea
A relation requires two distinct relata; thus knowledge cannot be its own object.

Razi presents a logical objection against the Ash'ari position that God knows through a knowledge existing in Him. He argues that if God knows through a knowledge, then that knowledge must be known by either itself or another knowledge. Both options lead to absurdity. In this lesson, we unpack his reasoning and see why a self-reflexive knowledge is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text of the third objection twice — once for overall sense, then focus on the two alternatives.
Notice the structure: two options, each refuted.
2
Understand knowledge as a relation
Razi defines 'knowing' as a relation between knower and known. Ask yourself why a relation cannot be reflexive.
This is key to the first refutation.
3
Trace the infinite regress argument
The second alternative leads to an infinite chain of knowledges. Why is this impossible in kalam?
Regress is a standard argument form.
4
Connect to the main debate
Reflect on why this objection targets the idea that God's knowledge is an additional attribute.
This prepares for the fourth objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : لو كان عالماً بالعلم لكان إما أن يعلم ذلك العلم بنفس ذلك العلم أو بعلم آخر، والأول باطل ; لأن كون الشيء عالماً بالشيء نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم والمعلوم، والنسبة لا تتحقق إلا بين الشيئين فثبت أن العلم الواحد يمتنع أن يكون علماً بنفسه، والثاني باطل أيضاً ; لأنه لو افتقر في معرفة العلم الأول إلى علم ثان لافتقر في معرفة العلم الثاني إلى علم ثالث ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال. الشبهة الرابعة : لو كان تعالى عالماً بالعلم لكان ذا علم، ولو كان ذا علم لحصل فوقه عليم لقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَفَوْقَ كُلِ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٌ ﴾ [يوسف: 76] وهذا محال فهذا محال، فوجب أن لا يكون عالماً بالعلم.
The third objection: If He were knowing through a knowledge, then either He knows that knowledge by that same knowledge or by another knowledge. The first is false because a thing's being knowing of something is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and the relation is only realized between two things. So it is established that one knowledge cannot be knowledge of itself. The second is also false because if it depends for knowing the first knowledge on a second knowledge, then it would depend for knowing the second knowledge on a third knowledge, and an infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. The fourth objection: If He Most High were knowing through a knowledge, He would possess knowledge; and if He possessed knowledge, there would be above Him a Knower, due to His saying Most High: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is a Knower' [Yusuf 12:76]. This is impossible; so this is impossible. Thus it follows that He should not be knowing through a knowledge.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage contains two objections against the position that God knows through a knowledge. The third objection argues that if God knows through a knowledge, then that knowledge must be known either by itself or by another knowledge. It cannot be known by itself because knowledge is a relation between a knower and a known, and a relation requires two distinct relata. It cannot be known by another knowledge because that would lead to an infinite regress. The fourth objection invokes the Quranic verse 'And above every possessor of knowledge is a Knower' to argue that if God possessed knowledge (as an attribute), then there would be a Knower above Him, which is impossible. Therefore, God cannot be knowing through a knowledge.

Key Terms
نسبة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge can know itself, like self-awareness.
Correction: Razi clarifies that knowing is a relational concept requiring two distinct things; self-knowledge would collapse the distinction.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject that knowledge can know itself?
A) Because knowledge is simple and cannot be an object.
B) Because a relation requires two distinct things, making self-knowledge impossible.
C) Because God's knowledge is infinite.
D) Because the Quran forbids it.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third objection shows that if God knows through a knowledge, that knowledge cannot be known without leading to either a relational impossibility or an infinite regression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that knowledge cannot be self-reflexive because a relation requires two distinct things. When you think about your own knowledge of God, do you ever treat that knowledge as if it were self-sufficient—something you possess independently of the One Known? How can you break that illusion today and restore your knowledge as a humble relation to the Unseen?
?
The infinite regress argument forces you to stop at a first cause. In your spiritual life, do you ever justify your actions with endless rationalizations instead of resting in the sufficiency of God's knowledge? What would it take for you to end that regress and simply submit to the One who knows all things?

The Fourth Objection: The Quranic Verse Against a Knowledge Attribute
Razi uses a Quranic verse to argue that if God had knowledge, He would not be the highest knower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the Quranic verse 'And above every possessor of knowledge is a Knower' to argue that God cannot be a possessor of a knowledge attribute.

Core Idea
If God possessed knowledge, there would be a greater knower above Him — an impossibility.

Razi presents his fourth objection, drawing on a Quranic verse to attack the Ash'ari position. He argues that if God had a knowledge attribute (i.e., was a 'possessor of knowledge'), then according to the verse, there would be a Knower above Him. Since that is impossible, the premise that God knows through a knowledge must be false. This lesson examines this scriptural argument and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic of the fourth objection and its translation. Notice how Razi uses a Quranic verse as a premise.
The verse is from Surah Yusuf.
2
Understand the logical structure
The argument is a conditional: If God has knowledge, then He is a possessor of knowledge; then the verse applies; then there would be a Knower above Him; but that is impossible.
Identify each step.
3
Examine the interpretation of the verse
Razi interprets 'above every possessor of knowledge' to mean a superior knower. Consider how an opponent might respond.
This is a contested interpretation.
4
Connect to the broader debate
This objection challenges the necessity of a knowledge attribute separate from God's essence.
It complements the third objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : لو كان عالماً بالعلم لكان إما أن يعلم ذلك العلم بنفس ذلك العلم أو بعلم آخر، والأول باطل ; لأن كون الشيء عالماً بالشيء نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم والمعلوم، والنسبة لا تتحقق إلا بين الشيئين فثبت أن العلم الواحد يمتنع أن يكون علماً بنفسه، والثاني باطل أيضاً ; لأنه لو افتقر في معرفة العلم الأول إلى علم ثان لافتقر في معرفة العلم الثاني إلى علم ثالث ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال. الشبهة الرابعة : لو كان تعالى عالماً بالعلم لكان ذا علم، ولو كان ذا علم لحصل فوقه عليم لقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَفَوْقَ كُلِ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٌ ﴾ [يوسف: 76] وهذا محال فهذا محال، فوجب أن لا يكون عالماً بالعلم.
The third objection: If He were knowing through a knowledge, then either He knows that knowledge by that same knowledge or by another knowledge. The first is false because a thing's being knowing of something is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and the relation is only realized between two things. So it is established that one knowledge cannot be knowledge of itself. The second is also false because if it depends for knowing the first knowledge on a second knowledge, then it would depend for knowing the second knowledge on a third knowledge, and an infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. The fourth objection: If He Most High were knowing through a knowledge, He would possess knowledge; and if He possessed knowledge, there would be above Him a Knower, due to His saying Most High: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is a Knower' [Yusuf 12:76]. This is impossible; so this is impossible. Thus it follows that He should not be knowing through a knowledge.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage contains two objections against the position that God knows through a knowledge. The third objection argues that if God knows through a knowledge, then that knowledge must be known either by itself or by another knowledge. It cannot be known by itself because knowledge is a relation between a knower and a known, and a relation requires two distinct relata. It cannot be known by another knowledge because that would lead to an infinite regress. The fourth objection invokes the Quranic verse 'And above every possessor of knowledge is a Knower' to argue that if God possessed knowledge (as an attribute), then there would be a Knower above Him, which is impossible. Therefore, God cannot be knowing through a knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the verse 'above every possessor of knowledge is a Knower' applies universally, then even your own knowledge is limited by a higher Knower. Are you content to remain a mere 'possessor' or are you actively seeking to know the Ultimate Knower through submission and learning? What concrete step will you take today to humble your intellect before Him?
?
Razi argues that attributing a separate knowledge to God would imply a greater Knower — a logical impossibility. Examine your own conception of God: have you ever unconsciously limited His attributes to fit your human understanding? Repent of that, and commit to affirming His perfection as He describes Himself.
?
This argument forces you to consider that God's knowledge is identical with His essence — He does not 'have' knowledge as an added thing. How does this truth affect the sincerity of your worship? Do you pray to a God who merely 'has' knowledge, or to the One who is Knowledge Itself? Let this reshape how you seek His guidance today.

The Objection That Divine Power Cannot Create Bodies
Razi presents and rebuts an objection against divine power using analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the divine attribute of power and how Razi frames it using analogical reasoning from the witnessed realm.

Core Idea
Analogy from created powers would force God to be unable to create bodies—an absurdity.

This lesson examines the first objection raised by those who deny the divine attribute of power. The objectors draw an analogy from the witnessed realm, where powers share the inability to create bodies. They argue that if God's power is a power, it would also lack this ability. Razi presents this objection and begins to engage with it, noting its implications for Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objectors first state a common judgment about powers in the witnessed realm, then argue that this common judgment must have a common cause, which is 'being a power'. Then they apply this to God.
The text cuts off at the end.
2
Identify the analogical reasoning
The objection uses an analogy from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (ghayb). Identify the shared description (being a power) and the common judgment (inability to create bodies). How does the objector conclude that God would be unable to create bodies?
This is a classic kalam argument form.
3
Consider the Ash'ari counterpoint
Razi mentions that the objection is 'incumbent upon the foundations of the Ash'aris'. Recall the Ash'ari argument for the vision of God (ru'ya) using a similar analogy. Compare the two arguments.
This shows the dialectical context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما شبههم في نفي صفة القدرة فمن وجهين : الشبهة الأولى : أن القدرة في الشاهد مختلفة وهي مع اختلافها مشتركة في أنها لا تصلح لخلق الجسم فهذا الحكم المشترك لا بد من تعليله بوصف مشترك، ولا مشترك بينهما إلا كونها قُدَراً، وإذا كان كونها قُدَراً علة لأن لا تكون صالحة لخلق الجسم وجب في كل ما كان قدرة أن لا تكون صالحة لخلق الجسم، فلو كان الله تعالى قادراً بالقدرة لوجب أن لا يقدر على خلق الجسم، واللازم محال فالملزوم مثله. قالوا هذا الكلام لازم على أصول الأشعرية فإنهم قالوا : العرض والجوهر مشتركان في صحة الرؤية، فلا بد من استناد هذا الحكم المشترك إلى وصف مشترك، ولا مشترك يعقل بين الجوهر وبين العرض إلا الوجود أو الحدوث والحدوث لا يصلح لهذه العلة، فبقي الوجود، والله تعالى موجود فوجب أن تصح رؤيته، فكذا نقول : القدرة في الشاهد مشتركة في أنها غير صالحة لخلق الجسم، وهذا الحكم المشترك لا بد من تعليله بوصف مشترك، والمشترك ههنا هو كونها قُدَراً ، فوجب أن
And as for their objections in negating the attribute of power, they are from two aspects: The first objection: That power in the witnessed realm is diverse, and despite its diversity it is common in that it is not suitable for creating a body. So this common judgment must be caused by a common description, and there is no common description between them except their being powers. And if their being powers is the cause for not being suitable to create a body, then it would be necessary that every power is not suitable for creating a body. So if God Most High were powerful through a power, it would be necessary that He cannot create a body, and the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. They said: This speech is incumbent upon the foundations of the Ash'aris, for they said: The accident and the substance share in the validity of being seen. So it is necessary that this common judgment be based on a common description, and there is no common description intelligible between substance and accident except existence or temporality, and temporality is not suitable for this cause, so existence remains, and God Most High exists, so it would be necessary that His vision is valid. So likewise we say: Power in the witnessed realm is common in that it is not suitable for creating a body, and this common judgment must be caused by a common description, and the common here is their being powers, so it is necessary that...
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the attribute of power (قدرة). The argument is that in the witnessed realm (the world of creation), different powers share the property of being unable to create a body. This common property must have a cause in a common description, and the only common description is that they are 'powers'. Therefore, if God's power is a power, it would also be unable to create a body—a clear absurdity, since God is the creator of bodies. The objection is then met with a counter-argument from the Ash'ari perspective: the Ash'aris use a similar analogy to prove the vision of God (ru'ya) based on the common property of existence between substance and accident. The objectors argue that the Ash'ari principle leads to the conclusion that God's power, being a power, cannot create bodies, just as their own proof for vision leads to God being seen. The text cuts off at 'so it is necessary that', likely continuing the argument. This objection and rebuttal illustrate the dialectical method of kalam.

Key Terms
قدرة
شاهد
خلق الجسم
علة
وصف مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power in the witnessed realm is analogous to divine power in every respect, so God's power must share the limitations of created powers.
Correction: The objection itself shows that such analogical reasoning leads to absurdity; the Ash'ari response would deny that the common description 'power' is univocal between creator and creation.

Checkpoint
What common judgment about power in the witnessed realm does the first objection use?
A) Powers are diverse.
B) Powers are temporally created.
C) Powers are not suitable for creating a body.
D) Powers are limited.

Key Takeaway
The first objection against the attribute of power uses analogical reasoning from the witnessed realm to argue that if God were powerful through a power, He would be unable to create bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face intellectual objections to God's power, do you trust that your own reasoning is sufficient, or do you recognize the limits of analogical reasoning from the created world? How does this lesson's exposure of the objectors' flawed analogy challenge your reliance on your own intellect in matters of faith?
?
If every power in the witnessed realm cannot create a body, and yet you affirm God's power to create, what does that teach you about the nature of God's attributes being incomparable? Does your heart truly accept that God's power is unlike any power you know, or do you still measure Him by your own experiences?
?
The objectors use the Ash'ari's own method against them. When you use theological arguments, are you consistent in applying them to God? Or do you selectively apply reasoning to fit your conclusions? How does this lesson call you to intellectual honesty in your faith?

Why God's Knowledge Cannot Be Through an Attribute
Razi argues that infinite number is impossible, so God cannot have knowledge attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that God's knowledge cannot be through a distinct attribute because it would entail an infinite number of attributes, which is impossible.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot be through an attribute because infinite knowledges are impossible.

Razi has already established that a single knowledge can know only one known. Now he applies this to God: if God knew through a distinct attribute of knowledge, then given the infinite objects of His knowledge, He would need infinite attributes. But an infinite number of attributes is impossible because every actual number is finite. Thus, the very concept of God knowing through an attribute is rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to get a sense of Razi's concise argumentative style.
2
Identify the key claim and the contradiction
Notice that Razi's conclusion is that God cannot be knowing through an attribute of knowledge. The contradiction arises from the impossibility of an infinite number.
3
Unpack the argument step by step
Examine each premise: (1) one knowledge knows only one known; (2) God's objects of knowledge are infinite; (3) if He knew through an attribute, He would need infinite attributes; (4) an infinite number is impossible because every existing number is finite.
Use the guided breakdown to follow the logical flow.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this argument leads to the conclusion that God's knowledge is identical with His essence, not a separate attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجوز وزع على غير المتناهي لزم أن يكون المعلوم بكل علم أشياء كثيرة، وقد بينا أنه لا أن يعلم بالعلم الواحد أكثر من معلوم واحد، وإنما قلنا إنه لا يجوز أن يعلم المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها بعلوم لا نهاية لها ؛ لأن وجود عدد لا نهاية له محال، وذلك لأن كل عدد موجود فهو قابل للزيادة والنقصان وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه، فكل عدد موجود فهو متناه والعدد الذي لا نهاية له يمتنع وجوده، ولما كان كونه تعالى عالمًا بالعلم لا بد وأن يكون على أحد هذه الأقسام، ولما ثبت أنها بأسرها باطلة ثبت أن كونه تعالى عالما بالعلم محال.
It is not permissible that if it is divided into the infinite, there would be many known things for every knowledge, and we have shown that one knowledge cannot know more than one known. And we say that it is not permissible that He knows infinite known objects with infinite knowledges, because the existence of an infinite number is impossible. This is because every existing number is subject to increase and decrease, and everything that is so is finite. So every existing number is finite, and an infinite number is impossible to exist. And since His being knowing through knowledge must be on one of these divisions, and since it is established that all of them are false, it is established that His being knowing through knowledge is impossible.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a proof by impossibility. Razi argues that if God were knowing through an attribute of knowledge (علم), then He would either know each known object by one knowledge or multiple knowns by one knowledge. He has already established that one knowledge cannot know more than one known. So if there are infinite knowns (which there are), then infinite knowledges would be required. But an infinite number of knowledges is impossible because every number that exists is finite (it can be increased or decreased). Therefore, the assumption that God knows through knowledge leads to contradiction, so God cannot have such an attribute. This is a standard argument in kalam for the simplicity of God's knowledge being identical with His essence.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
عدد لا نهاية له
متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this argument denies that God knows anything at all.
Correction: The argument only denies that God's knowledge is through a separate attribute. It affirms that God knows, but His knowledge is identical with His essence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, why is it impossible for God to know through an attribute of knowledge?
A) Because infinite number is impossible, and God would need infinite knowledges.
B) Because knowledge is a created attribute.
C) Because God cannot be described by any attribute.
D) Because the knowns are finite.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the concept of God knowing through an attribute leads to an impossible infinite regress, so God's knowledge must be identical with His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's argument shows that God's knowledge is not like human knowledge—it is not through a separate attribute that processes information. Do you truly live as if God's knowledge of you is immediate, complete, and not dependent on any process? When you commit a sin in private, do you act as if God knows it in a 'delayed' or 'partial' way? This week, identify one area where you tend to forget God's immediate knowledge, and set a concrete reminder (like a phone notification) to repeat: 'Allah knows me perfectly and directly.'
?
The passage says that if God's knowledge were through an attribute, it would require infinite attributes, which is impossible. This means God's knowledge is one and perfect—nothing is outside it. Is there a secret you are hiding that you hope God does not see clearly? Write it down on a piece of paper, then burn it as a symbol of your willingness to submit to the fact that Allah knows it fully, and ask for His forgiveness with certainty that He knows your repentance.
?
Razi's argument relies on the principle that infinite number is impossible. In your own life, do you ever feel that your sins are 'infinite'—too many for God's mercy? This lesson teaches that God's knowledge is not limited by number; He knows every sin individually. But His mercy is also not limited. How will you change your view of God's mercy knowing that His knowledge is perfect and your sins are fully known yet He still forgives? Commit to thanking Allah for His perfect knowledge and mercy today.

Third Objection: Self-Knowledge or Infinite Regress
Razi presents the opponent's objection that if God knows through knowledge, it leads to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the objection that God's knowledge cannot be through an attribute, due to the impossibility of self-knowledge and the threat of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Knowledge as a relation cannot know itself, and any second knowledge leads to endless regression.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a powerful objection against the Ash'ari claim that God knows through an eternal attribute of knowledge. The opponent argues that if God knows by knowledge, that knowledge must either know itself or be known by another knowledge—both of which lead to rational impossibility. We will trace the logical steps and see why infinite regress (tasalsul) is invoked as a decisive refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that it is a conditional argument with two alternatives, each refuted.
The structure: if (knowledge), then either (self-knowledge) or (another knowledge). Both are impossible.
2
Identify the first argument: self-knowledge is impossible
The opponent states that knowledge is a relation between two distinct entities (knower and known). Therefore, one knowledge cannot be knowledge of itself because that would require it to be both relation and relatum.
A relation requires two relata; a single thing cannot relate to itself in this way.
3
Identify the second argument: regress if another knowledge is needed
If knowledge requires another knowledge to be known, that second knowledge would need a third, ad infinitum. This infinite regress is rationally impossible (muhal).
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: the chain cannot terminate.
4
Reflect on the implication
If both alternatives fail, the initial premise (knowing through knowledge) is false. The opponent concludes that God's knowledge cannot be through an attribute.
This objection challenges the Ash'ari solution to the ontology of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : لو كان عالماً بالعلم لكان إما أن يعلم ذلك العلم بنفس ذلك العلم أو بعلم آخر، والأول باطل ؛ لأن كون الشيء عالما بالشيء نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم والمعلوم، والنسبة لا تتحقق إلا بين الشيئين فثبت أن العلم الواحد يمتنع يكون علما بنفسه، والثاني باطل أيضًا ؛ لأنه لو افتقر في معرفة العلم الأول إلى علم ثان لافتقر في معرفة العلم الثاني إلى علم ثالث ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال.
The third objection: If He were knowing through knowledge, then He would either know that knowledge by itself or by another knowledge. The first is false because a thing's being knowing of a thing is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and a relation is only realized between two things, so it is established that one knowledge cannot be knowledge of itself. The second is also false because if it were in need of a second knowledge to know the first knowledge, it would be in need of a third knowledge to know the second knowledge, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This is an objection (shubha) against the Ash'ari claim that God knows through an attribute of knowledge. The opponent argues: if God knows through knowledge, then either He knows that knowledge by itself or by another knowledge. It cannot know itself because knowledge is a relation between knower and known, requiring two distinct entities. Also, if a second knowledge were required to know the first, that would lead to an infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible. Hence, God cannot know through an attribute of knowledge.

Key Terms
علم
نسبة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's knowledge could be self-referential like self-awareness, but the objection defines knowledge as a relation between two distinct entities.
Correction: In this framework, knowledge is a relation between two things; a single entity cannot be both the subject and object of the same relation.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given for rejecting self-knowledge?
A) Knowledge is eternal.
B) Knowledge requires two distinct entities.
C) God does not have attributes.
D) Knowledge is created.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses the impossibility of self-knowledge and infinite regress to argue that God's knowledge cannot be through an attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own moments of self-reflection. When you think about your thoughts, is that thought itself being known? How does this passage's argument that knowledge is a relation between two things make you reconsider whether you can truly 'know yourself'? Are you comfortable with that limitation, or does it drive you to seek a knower beyond yourself?
?
The objection uses infinite regress to show that a chain of justifications cannot go on forever. In your own life, when you search for ultimate reasons for your belief in God, do you find yourself relying on a chain that must end? Where does that chain end for you personally, and how does this passage's rejection of infinite regress challenge or affirm your stopping point?
?
This passage questions the very possibility of God knowing through a separate attribute. Reflect on whether you have ever tried to understand God's attributes as you understand human qualities, with clear distinctions. How does the idea that God's knowledge is His essence (as Ash'aris might say) affect how you relate to Him? Are you more drawn to a God whose attributes are distinct or identical? Which feels more intimate, and why?

Fourth Objection: The Verse About Possessors of Knowledge
Razi presents a logical objection using a Qur'anic verse to deny that Allah knows through an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an opponent uses Qur'an 12:76 to argue that Allah cannot have an attribute of knowledge.

Core Idea
A literal reading of a verse can challenge divine attributes, but it must be interpreted correctly.

In this objection, the opponent cites a verse that seems to imply a hierarchy of knowers. They argue that if Allah were knowing through an attribute, He would be a possessor of knowledge, and then there would be a greater knower above Him. This would contradict His supremacy. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: if-then statements leading to an impossibility.
This is a condensed argument typical of kalam.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument into premises: (1) If Allah is knowing through knowledge, then He has knowledge. (2) If He has knowledge, then He is a possessor of knowledge. (3) The Qur'an says above every possessor of knowledge is a knower. (4) Therefore, there would be a knower above Allah, which is impossible.
The conclusion is that the initial assumption must be false.
3
Understand the key verse
Focus on the verse from Surah Yusuf: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is a knower.' The opponent takes this as a universal statement covering all beings, including Allah.
The verse is the crucial premise for the objection.
4
Consider the Ash'ari response
The common Ash'ari refutation is that the verse applies only to creatures, not to Allah, since Allah's knowledge is not relative. He is not a 'possessor of knowledge' in the same sense.
This shows the importance of interpreting verses in light of established theological principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : لو كان تعالى عالمًا بالعلم لكان ذا علم، ولو كان ذا علم لحصل فوقه عليم لقوله تعالى : ﴿وَفَوْقَ كُلِ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٌ ﴾ [يوسف: ٧٦] وهذا محال فهذا محال، فوجب أن لا يكون عالما بالعلم.
The fourth objection: If the Exalted were knowing through knowledge, He would possess knowledge. And if He possessed knowledge, there would be above Him a knower, because of His saying: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is a knower' (Yusuf 76). This is impossible, so that is impossible. Therefore, it is necessary that He is not knowing through knowledge.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This is another objection from the opponent. They cite the Qur'anic verse (Yusuf 12:76) to argue that if God had knowledge (as an attribute), He would be one of those 'possessors of knowledge,' and then there would be a knower above Him, which is impossible for God. Hence God cannot have an attribute of knowledge. The argument relies on the literal reading of the verse as a universal statement.

Key Terms
ذو علم
عليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Above every possessor of knowledge is a knower' applies universally to all beings, including Allah.
Correction: The verse speaks of relative knowledge among creatures; Allah's knowledge is incomparable, and He is not under any knower.

Checkpoint

A) ﴿وَفَوْقَ كُلِ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾
B) ﴿لا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ﴾
C) ﴿اللَّهُ لا إِلَهَ إِلاَّ هُوَ﴾
D) ﴿وَهُوَ الْقَاهِرُ فَوْقَ عِبَادِهِ﴾

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because the Qur'anic verse is interpreted as relative, not absolute, and does not imply a hierarchy above Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Does your heart tremble or do you trust that the correct understanding resolves the difficulty? What steps can you take to ensure your faith is built on sound knowledge rather than fragile reasoning?

The First Objection: Analogy from Created Powers
First objection against divine power, with Ash'ari parallel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that opponents use to deny God's attribute of power, and the Ash'ari counter-argument from ru'yat Allah.

Core Idea
Created powers cannot create bodies, so divine power cannot either—unless the analogy fails.

This lesson presents the first objection against the Ash'ari view that God is powerful through an attribute of power (qudrah). The opponents argue that created powers share the property of being unable to create bodies, and this must be due to their being 'powers.' Therefore, if God had a power attribute, He would also be unable to create bodies—a conclusion the Ash'aris reject. They also note that the Ash'ari's own principle of shared attributes, used to argue for the visibility of God, would support their objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first objection
Read the Arabic passage from 'الشبهة الأولى' to the end of that section. Note how the objection is structured: it identifies a shared property among created powers, then argues this property must be attributed to a shared attribute (their being powers), and then applies the same reasoning to God.
Pay attention to the analogy with the Ash'ari argument for ru'yat Allah.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the objection into its key steps: (1) Created powers vary but share being unsuitable for creating bodies. (2) This shared judgment needs a shared cause. (3) The only shared attribute is their being powers. (4) Therefore, being a power causes unsuitability for creating bodies. (5) If God has a power attribute, He too would be unable to create bodies.
3
Understand the Ash'ari counter-analogy
The opponents note that the Ash'aris argue that substances and accidents share the property of visibility because they share existence. The opponents use the same logic: created powers share unsuitability for creating bodies because they share being powers. Thus, the Ash'ari principle would imply God's power cannot create bodies.
This is a polemical move: it turns the Ash'ari's own reasoning against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما شبههم في نفي صفة القدرة فمن وجهين : الشبهة الأولى : أن القدرة في الشاهد مختلفة وهي مع اختلافها مشتركة في أنها لا تصلح لخلق الجسم فهذا الحكم المشترك لا بد من تعليله بوصف مشترك، ولا مشترك بينهما إلا كونها قُدَرًا، وإذا كان كونها قُدَرًا علة لأن لا تكون صالحة لخلق الجسم وجب في كل ما كان قدرة أن لا تكون صالحة لخلق الجسم، فلو كان الله تعالى قادرًا بالقدرة لوجب أن لا يقدر على خلق الجسم، واللازم محال فالملزوم مثله. :قالوا هذا الكلام لازم على أصول الأشعرية فإنهم قالوا : العرض والجوهر مشتركان في صحة الرؤية، فلا بد من استناد هذا الحكم المشترك إلى وصف مشترك، ولا مشترك يعقل بين الجوهر وبين العرض إلا الوجود أو الحدوث والحدوث لا يصلح لهذه العلة، فبقي الوجود، والله تعالى موجود فوجب أن تصح رؤيته، فكذا نقول : القدرة في الشاهد مشتركة في أنها غير صالحة لخلق الجسم، وهذا الحكم المشترك لا بد من تعليله بوصف مشترك والمشترك ههنا هو كونها قُدَرًا فوجب أن يكون كونها قدرًا علة لهذا الامتناع، فوجب أن يحصل هذا الامتناع في كل ما كان قدرة. الشبهة الثانية وهي أن القدرة في الشاهد مع اختلافها لا يصلح شيء منها لخلق الجسم، فلو فرضنا قدرة في الغائب لكانت تلك القدرة إما أن تكون مثلا لهذه القدرة الموجودة في الشاهد أو مخالفة لها، فإن كانت تلك القدرة مثلا لهذه القدرة في الشاهد، ثم إن هذه القدرة لا تصلح لخلق الجسم، فكذا تلك القدرة لا تصلح لخلق الجسم، فإذا كانت تلك القدرة مخالفة بعض هذه القدرة لبعض، ولما كانت هذه القدرة مع ما بينا من المخالفة لا يصلح شيء منها لخلق الجسم، فكذلك تلك القدرة الغائبة وجب أن لا تصلح لخلق الجسم. فهذه جملة شبه المخالفين في هذه المسألة.
And as for their objections in negating the attribute of power, they are of two aspects: The first objection: Power in the witnessed world is varied, and despite its variation it is shared in that it is not suitable for creating the body. This shared judgment necessarily requires attribution to a shared attribute, and there is no shared attribute between them except their being powers. And if their being powers is a cause for them not being suitable for creating the body, it is necessary in everything that is a power that it not be suitable for creating the body. So if God were powerful through power, He would necessarily not be able to create the body. The consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. They said: This statement is necessary upon the principles of the Ash'aris, for they said: The accident and the substance share in the validity of vision, so this shared judgment must be attributed to a shared attribute. No shared attribute between substance and accident is conceivable except existence or origination. Origination is not suitable for this cause, so existence remains. And God exists, so His vision must be valid. So likewise we say: Power in the witnessed world is shared in its being unsuitable for creating the body, and this shared judgment must be attributed to a shared attribute, and the shared here is their being powers. So their being a power must be the cause of this impossibility, so this impossibility must occur in everything that is a power. The second objection: Power in the witnessed world, despite its variation, no part of it is suitable for creating the body. So if we suppose a power in the unseen, that power must either be similar to this power existing in the witnessed world or different from it. If it is similar, and this power is not suitable for creating the body, then that power is likewise not suitable. If it is different from some of this power to others, and since this power with the variation we described no part of it is suitable for creating the body, then likewise that unseen power must not be suitable for creating the body. So this is the sum of the opponents' objections in this issue.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents two objections from the opponents (likely Mu'tazila) against the Ash'ari view that God is powerful through an attribute of power (qudrah). 

The first objection argues: Created powers (in the witnessed world) are varied but share the property that they cannot create bodies. This shared property must be caused by a shared attribute, which is their being 'powers'. So if God had a power attribute, He would also be unable to create bodies. The opponents also note that this reasoning parallels the Ash'ari argument for the visibility of God based on shared existence.

The second objection: If we supposed a power in the unseen, it would either be like or unlike created powers. If like, it cannot create bodies. If unlike, still no part of created powers can create bodies, so the unseen power also cannot create bodies. Therefore, God cannot have a power attribute.

Key Terms
القدرة
الجسم
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine power must behave exactly like created powers because both are called 'power'.
Correction: Ash'aris argue that God's power is not analogous to created powers; the term 'power' is predicated analogically (tashkīk), not univocally. Thus, the properties of created powers do not necessarily apply to God.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what is the shared cause that makes created powers unable to create bodies?
A) Their createdness (ḥudūth)
B) Their being powers (kawnuhā qudaran)
C) Their existence (wujūd)
D) Their diversity

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that if created powers cannot create bodies because they are powers, then any power attribute in God would also prevent Him from creating bodies—so God cannot have a power attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about God's attributes, do you unconsciously assume that His qualities must be like the best human qualities? How does the objection in this lesson expose that assumption, and what would it cost you to let go of it?
?
The objectors use a logical argument that seems airtight, yet it leads to a conclusion that contradicts what you know about God's power. Have you ever trusted your own logic over a clear teaching of revelation? What would it take for you to submit your reasoning to the authority of the Qur'an and Sunnah in such a case?
?
You have heard the Ash'ari response that divine power is unlike created power. But is that just a convenient escape from the argument? Honestly, does your heart accept that God's attributes are utterly beyond analogy, or do you still measure Him by what you know? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized that?

The Second Objection: Dilemma of Similarity or Difference
Second objection using dilemma of similarity or difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that uses a dilemma to argue that divine power, if it existed, would be either like or unlike created powers, and in both cases unable to create bodies.

Core Idea
Divine power, if it exists, must be either similar or dissimilar to created powers—both options lead to impossibility.

The opponents present a second argument: if God had a power attribute, that power would be either similar to created powers or different from them. If similar, it would share the inability to create bodies. If different, it would still be unlike every specific created power, but since all created powers—despite their differences—cannot create bodies, any power different from them would also be unable to create bodies. Thus, the conclusion is that God cannot have a power attribute at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second objection
Read from 'الشبهة الثانية' to the end of the chunk. Notice how the objection sets up a dilemma with two branches: similarity or difference.
The passage ends with 'فهذه جملة شبه المخالفين في هذه المسألة', summarizing both objections.
2
Analyze the first branch (similarity)
If the supposed divine power is similar to created powers, then it will share their property: inability to create bodies. Thus, God would not be able to create bodies—an absurd consequence.
3
Analyze the second branch (difference)
If it is different, then it differs from each specific created power. But all created powers, despite being different from each other, are still unable to create bodies. So difference does not remove the inability; it is a property of all powers. Hence, even a different power would be unable to create bodies.
The argument assumes that the inability to create bodies is not tied to any particular type of power but is a universal feature of power as such.
4
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Ask: Does the argument assume that any power must be either identical to or different from created powers? And does it assume that 'different from X' still falls under the same general category of power? The Ash'ari would question whether divine power belongs to the same genus as created power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for their objections in negating the attribute of power, they are of two aspects: The first objection: Power in the witnessed world is varied, and despite its variation it is shared in that it is not suitable for creating the body. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's power is different from created powers, it might be able to create bodies.
Correction: The objection argues that even created powers differ from each other but still cannot create bodies, so difference alone does not enable creation. Therefore, even a different divine power would still be unable to create bodies.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, what happens if the unseen power is dissimilar to created powers?
A) It might be able to create bodies because it is different.
B) It still cannot create bodies because all created powers share that inability regardless of differences.
C) It becomes a substance rather than a power.
D) The dilemma collapses.

Key Takeaway
The second objection argues by dilemma that any supposed divine power, whether similar or dissimilar to created powers, would be unable to create bodies, contradicting God's creative acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that something is impossible for God because you've never seen it happen in the world? When you do that, you are repeating the logic of this objection—limiting God to your experience. Will you repent from that today and ask Him to do what only He can do?
?
This objection treats divine power as either identical to or merely different from created powers. But God's power is not just 'different' in degree; it is categorically other. In your prayer life, do you expect God to act within the bounds of human possibility, or do you truly call upon Him as the One whose power has no analogue? Name one situation where you need to trust His unique power beyond analogy.
?
The opponents assume that if divine power is 'different,' it still can't create bodies because even different created powers can't. But God's power is not a type or species like created powers. Are you measuring God's actions by human standards in any area of your life—forgiveness, provision, guidance? Commit today to letting God be God, not a bigger version of a human.
