Razi's Response to the First Objection on Power
Razi responds to the first objection about power by questioning the necessity of the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the possibility of an attribute being contingent in itself yet necessary due to the divine essence, and how he handles the ensuing objection about the essence being both receptive and agent.

Core Idea
Divine attributes can be contingent in themselves but necessary by another.

In this passage, Razi tackles the first objection against divine power. He proposes a nuanced view: an attribute like power could be contingent in its own nature, but necessary because of the essence that possesses it. When the opponent objects that this makes the essence both receptive and agent—a contradiction—Razi questions why that is impossible, revealing a deeper philosophical principle. He then promises a fuller response later, showing his systematic approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure: Razi proposes, the opponent objects, Razi questions, the opponent cites a principle, Razi defers. This is a dialectical argument.
The Arabic uses technical terms: صفة (attribute), ممكن (contingent), واجب (necessary), ذات (essence), قابل (receptive), فاعل (agent).
2
Identify Razi's initial proposal
Razi asks: 'Why is it not permissible that the attribute be possible in itself and necessary due to the necessity of the essence?' This is the core idea—an attribute can have two aspects: its own contingency and its necessity from God.
Razi is not claiming this is definitely the case, but offering it as a valid possibility.
3
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent says this would imply the essence is both receptive (قابل) and agent (فاعل) simultaneously, which he considers impossible. Razi challenges this by asking 'And why do you say that is impossible?'
Receptive here means 'that which receives an attribute' and agent means 'that which produces effects'.
4
Note the principle cited and Razi's deferral
The opponent cites the principle 'from one thing only one effect proceeds' (الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا أثر واحد). Razi says he will answer this objection later, indicating a structured argument.
This principle is a philosophical axiom that Razi will later refute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : لِمَ لا يجوز أن تكون الصفة ممكنة لذاتها واجبة لوجوب الذات؟ قوله : يلزم كون الذات قابلة وفاعلة معًا قلنا : ولم قلتم إن ذلك محال ؟ قوله : لأن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا أثر واحد قلنا سنجيب عن شبهتك في هذه المسألة إن شاء الله تعالى.
And the answer to the first objection: Why is it not permissible that the attribute be possible in itself and necessary due to the necessity of the essence? He said: It would necessitate the essence being both receptive and agent simultaneously. We said: And why do you say that is impossible? He said: Because the one (the essence) only produces a single effect. We said: We will answer your objection on this issue, God willing.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a response to the first objection against divine power. Razi proposes that the attribute of power could be contingent in itself but necessary due to the divine essence. The opponent then raises a problem: if the essence has both receptive (قابل) and agent (فاعل) properties simultaneously, that is impossible. Razi questions that impossibility, and the opponent cites the principle that from one thing only one effect proceeds (الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا أثر واحد). Razi postpones the full answer to this specific point, indicating that he will address it later.

Key Terms
الصفة
ممكن
واجب
الذات
فاعل
قابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is conceding defeat when he says 'We will answer your objection'—he is postponing his full refutation.
Correction: Razi is not giving up; he is systematically deferring a detailed response to a later section. This is a common scholarly technique in theological works.

Checkpoint
According to the opponent, why cannot the essence be both receptive and agent?
A) Because it would require two attributes.
B) Because one thing can only produce one effect.
C) Because God is not receptive.
D) Because power is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Razi's defense of divine power hinges on the idea that an attribute can be contingent in itself yet necessary by another—a key move in Islamic theology to maintain both divine simplicity and the reality of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi postpones the answer to the opponent's objection. Have you ever shelved a doubt about God's power because you couldn't resolve it? Are you willing to trust that the answer exists, even if you don't have it now, and actively seek it instead of ignoring it? What step will you take today to confront that doubt?
?
Razi argues that an attribute can be necessary because of the essence, not by itself. In your life, what are you relying on that is only strong because of your connection to God? Are you trying to make yourself 'necessary in yourself' instead of depending on Him? How will you shift that dependence today?

Refuting the Mu'tazila's First Objection on Knowledge
Razi refutes the Mu'tazila objection using ambiguity and contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God's being knowing cannot be caused by an attribute because it is necessary, using logical analysis of ambiguity and self-contradiction.

Core Idea
Necessity does not preclude causation; ambiguity and self-contradiction undermine the objection.

Razi addresses a key objection from the Mu'tazila: if God's being knowing is necessary, it cannot depend on an attribute of knowledge. Razi shows that the objection rests on an ambiguity and is self-defeating. Let's examine his two-pronged refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument come through before interpreting.
Notice the structure: Razi gives two aspects (ثانيها).
2
Understand the two aspects
Identify the two parts of the response: the first deals with the ambiguity of 'necessary', the second with the self-contradiction in using necessity as a cause.
Razi uses logical analysis, not scriptural argument.
3
Analyze the logical moves
Examine how Razi breaks down the Mu'tazila premise. The first response distinguishes between necessity by itself and necessity by another. The second shows the objection is self-refuting.
This is a classic kalam refutation technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى للمعتزلة في العلم . وهي قولهم: عالمية الله تعالى صفة واجبة والواجب لا يعلل - فنقول : الجواب عنه من وجوه : أحدها أن قولكم عالمية الله تعالى واجبة قدر مشترك بين أن تكون عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لذاته وبين أن تكون عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لوجوب اتصافه بالعلم، وإذا كان كذلك فبتقدير أن تكون عالمية الله تعالى لأجل وجوب اتصافه بالعلم لم يلزم من كون عالميته واجبة بهذا التفسير استغناؤه عن العلم إلا أن وجوب الشيء بالشيء لا يقتضي استغناءه عنه، وإن ادعيتم أصل وجوب العالمية بهذا القدر لا يقتضي امتناع تعليل العالمية بالعلم فإن ادعيتم أصل وجوب العالمية فهذا القدر لا يقتضي امتناع تعليل العالمية بالعلم وإن ادعيتم أن عالمية الله واجبة لنفس الذات فمسلم أنه لو ثبت لكم هذه المقدمة وجب القطع بامتناع تعليل العالمية بالعلم إلا أن قولكم: عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لنفس الذات هو ادعاء نفس المطلوب، فلا يمكن جعله مقدمة في إثبات المطلوب، فثبت أن هذه الشبهة مغالطة. وثانيها : أن هذه الشبهة متناقضة، وذلك لأنكم جعلتم كون العالمية واجبة علة لأن لا تكون معللة، وكونها غير معللة أمر واجب عندكم، فإذا عللتم كونها غير معللة بكونها واجبة فقد عللتم الحكم الواجب، وذلك مناقض لكلامكم.
And the answer to the first objection of the Mu'tazila concerning knowledge: It is their statement: 'God's being knowing is a necessary attribute, and the necessary is not caused.' We say: The answer to it is from several aspects: One of them is that your statement 'God's being knowing is necessary' is a common term between it being necessary by itself and it being necessary due to the necessity of His being described by knowledge. If that is so, then if it is assumed that God's being knowing is due to the necessity of His being described by knowledge, it does not follow from His being knowing being necessary in this interpretation that He is independent of knowledge, because a thing's being necessary due to another does not imply its independence from it. And if you claim that the necessity of being knowing in this sense does not preclude causing being knowing by knowledge, then if you claim the original necessity of being knowing, this sense does not preclude causing being knowing by knowledge. And if you claim that God's being knowing is necessary by itself, then it is granted that if this premise were established for you, then it would be necessary to decide the impossibility of causing being knowing by knowledge, except that your statement 'God's being knowing is necessary by itself' is a claim of the very thing sought, so it cannot be taken as a premise to prove the sought. Thus it is established that this objection is a fallacy. The second aspect: That this objection is contradictory, because you have made being knowing necessary a cause for it not being caused, and its being uncaused is a necessary thing according to you. So if you cause its being uncaused by its being necessary, then you have caused the necessary judgment, and that contradicts your statement.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This is a detailed refutation of the Mu'tazila objection that God's attribute of being knowing (العالمية) is necessary (واجبة) and what is necessary cannot be caused (لا يعلل), so it cannot depend on an attribute of knowledge.

Razi gives two responses:
1. The term 'necessary' is ambiguous: it could mean necessary by itself (واجبة لذاتها) or necessary due to something else (واجبة لوجوب اتصافه بالعلم). If it means the latter, it does not preclude being caused by knowledge. If they claim it means necessity by itself, that is exactly the point in dispute and cannot be taken as a premise.
2. The objection is self-contradictory because they use 'necessity' as a cause to prove that it is uncaused, which means they are causally explaining the very thing they claim cannot be explained.

Key Terms
عالمية
واجب
معلل
مغالطة
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is necessary, it cannot have a cause.
Correction: Razi shows that necessity may be derived from something else, so it does not automatically preclude a cause. The objection conflates two meanings of 'necessary'.

Checkpoint
What is the first response's main point?
A) The necessity of God's knowledge prevents causation.
B) The term 'necessary' is ambiguous and may imply being caused.
C) The Mu'tazila are mistaken about specific verses.
D) Knowledge is a created attribute.

Key Takeaway
Razi dismantles the Mu'tazila objection by showing the ambiguity of 'necessary' and the self-contradiction in using necessity as a cause for uncausedness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is necessary (like an attribute of God) it cannot be explained or have a cause? How does Razi's distinction between self-necessity and derived necessity challenge your assumption? What would it mean for you to hold that God's attributes can be both necessary and caused by His essence?
?
Think of a time when you used a concept without defining it clearly, like the Mu'tazila used 'necessary'. How might ambiguity in your own understanding have led to an invalid conclusion? Are you willing to re-examine a belief you hold to check if it is based on an unexamined premise?
?
Razi shows that the Mu'tazila's argument is self-contradictory because they use necessity to prove uncausedness. Is there an area in your faith or life where you hold two contradictory ideas? Would you be ready to resolve that contradiction even if it requires changing your stance?

Al-Razi's Three Refutations of the Mu'tazilite Doubt on Divine Knowledge
Al-Razi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God's knowledge needs no attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's three refutations of the Mu'tazilite claim that God's omniscience is uncaused, and how each refutation exposes a logical flaw in their argument.

Core Idea
Divine omniscience is necessary but not uncaused.

The Mu'tazilites argued that because God's omniscience is a necessary attribute, it cannot have a cause, thus God does not need the attribute of knowledge. Al-Razi masterfully dismantles this argument in three ways, exposing ambiguity, self-contradiction, and inconsistency with their own doctrines. This passage is a cornerstone of Islamic theological dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite doubt
Begin by reading the Mu'tazilite claim as presented at the start of the passage: 'God's omniscience is a necessary attribute, and what is necessary cannot be caused.' Understand their reasoning.
This is the view Al-Razi intends to refute.
2
Analyze the first refutation (ambiguity)
Al-Razi points out that the term 'necessary' is ambiguous. Distinguish between necessary in itself and necessary by another. Consider how this ambiguity undermines their argument.
3
Examine the second refutation (self-contradiction)
Al-Razi shows that if necessity is the cause of uncausedness, then they are causing a necessary judgment, which contradicts their own claim.
This is a clever reductio ad absurdum.
4
Study the third refutation (inconsistency with their own doctrine)
The Mu'tazilites themselves use causation for necessary states (ahwal). Al-Razi uses their own theory against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى للمعتزلة في العلم . وهي قولهم: عالمية الله تعالى صفة واجبة والواجب لا يعلل - فنقول : الجواب عنه من وجوه : أحدها أن قولكم عالمية الله تعالى واجبة قدر مشترك بين أن تكون عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لذاته وبين أن تكون عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لوجوب اتصافه بالعلم، وإذا كان كذلك فبتقدير أن تكون عالمية الله تعالى لأجل وجوب اتصافه بالعلم لم يلزم من كون عالميته واجبة بهذا التفسير استغناؤه عن العلم إلا أن وجوب الشيء بالشيء لا يقتضي استغناءه عنه، وإن ادعيتم أصل وجوب العالمية بهذا القدر لا يقتضي امتناع تعليل العالمية بالعلم فإن ادعيتم أصل وجوب العالمية فهذا القدر لا يقتضي امتناع تعليل العالمية بالعلم وإن ادعيتم أن عالمية الله واجبة لنفس الذات فمسلم أنه لو ثبت لكم هذه المقدمة وجب القطع بامتناع تعليل العالمية بالعلم إلا أن قولكم: عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لنفس الذات هو ادعاء نفس المطلوب، فلا يمكن جعله مقدمة في إثبات المطلوب، فثبت أن هذه الشبهة مغالطة. وثانيها : أن هذه الشبهة متناقضة، وذلك لأنكم جعلتم كون العالمية واجبة علة لأن لا تكون معللة، وكونها غير معللة أمر واجب عندكم، فإذا عللتم كونها غير معللة بكونها واجبة فقد عللتم الحكم الواجب، وذلك مناقض لكلامكم. وثالثها: أنكم تقولون : إنه تعالى ممتاز عن خلقه بحالة توجب أحوالا أربعة وهي: الموجودية والعالمية والحيية والقادرية وهذه الأحوال الأربعة واجبة الثبوت لله تعالى، ثم إنكم عللتموها بالحالة الخامسة، فقد بطل قولكم : إن الواجب لا يجوز تعليله.
And the answer to the first doubt of the Mu'tazila concerning knowledge, which is their statement: 'God's omniscience is a necessary attribute, and what is necessary cannot be caused.' We say: The answer to it has several aspects. First: Your statement that God's omniscience is necessary is ambiguous between [the claim] that God's omniscience is necessary in itself, and [the claim] that God's omniscience is necessary due to the necessity of His being described by knowledge. If so, then if we suppose that God's omniscience is due to the necessity of His being described by knowledge, then from the fact that His omniscience is necessary in this interpretation, it does not follow that He is independent of knowledge, because the necessity of a thing by another does not entail independence from it. And if you claim the absolute necessity of omniscience, this [absolute] does not preclude the possibility of making omniscience caused by knowledge. And if you claim that God's omniscience is necessary in itself, then we concede that if this premise were established for you, then the impossibility of making omniscience caused by knowledge would necessarily follow. However, your statement that God's omniscience is necessary in itself is itself the very claim at issue; it cannot be taken as a premise to prove the desired conclusion. Thus it is established that this doubt is a fallacy. Second: This doubt is self-contradictory; that is because you have made the necessity of omniscience a cause for its not being caused, and its not being caused is, according to you, necessary. So when you caused its not being caused by its being necessary, you have caused a necessary judgment, and that contradicts your own statement. Third: You say that God Most High is distinguished from His creation by a state that necessitates four states: existence, omniscience, livingness, and power; and these four states are necessary of affirmation for God Most High. Then you caused them by a fifth state. So your statement that what is necessary cannot be caused is refuted.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first Mu'tazilite doubt concerning God's knowledge. The Mu'tazilites argue that God's omniscience is a necessary attribute and that what is necessary cannot be caused, so omniscience does not need a cause like knowledge. Al-Razi provides three answers. First, he points out an ambiguity: the term 'necessary' can mean necessary in itself or necessary due to something else. If they mean necessary due to something else, then it does not preclude causation. If they mean necessary in itself, that is just the claim they need to prove, so it is a petitio principii. Second, he shows the argument is self-contradictory: if they say necessity is the cause of uncausedness, then they are causing a necessary judgment (uncausedness) which itself is necessary, thus causing the necessary, which they claim is impossible. Third, he uses the Mu'tazilites' own doctrine of 'states' (ahwal): they say God has a state that necessitates four other states (existence, omniscience, livingness, power), yet they claim these states are caused by the fifth state, so they are accepting causation of necessary things, contradicting their own principle.

Key Terms
عالمية
واجب
تعليل
مغالطة
متناقضة
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi denies that God's omniscience is necessary.
Correction: Al-Razi does not deny the necessity; he shows that the Mu'tazilite argument fails to establish that necessity implies uncausedness. He accepts that God's attributes are necessary, but they are caused by the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the first error Al-Razi points out in the Mu'tazilite argument that God's omniscience is uncaused?
A) They confuse necessary in itself with necessary by another.
B) They ignore the possibility of eternal causation.
C) They assume God's knowledge is an accident.
D) They deny the existence of attributes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses logical analysis, reductio ad absurdum, and internal critique to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that God's omniscience is uncaused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that seemed 'necessary' to you, but you never examined whether it was truly necessary in itself or only due to something else? How might this lesson change how you defend your beliefs about God?
?
Al-Razi shows that even a seemingly strong argument can be self-contradictory. Which of your own intellectual commitments might you need to re-examine for hidden contradictions, even if they feel obvious and necessary?
?
When you recite or affirm that Allah is ‘All-Knowing,’ do you treat this as a mere fact or as an attribute that demands a deeper, logically grounded understanding? What step will you take today to move beyond superficial acceptance?

Al-Razi's Refutation of the Second Mu'tazilite Doubt
Al-Razi responds to the second Mu'tazilite doubt on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's response to the claim that God's knowledge would imply need, and how he distinguishes between different interpretations of 'need'.

Core Idea
God's essence itself necessitates knowledge; no external attribute is needed.

The Mu'tazila argue that if God knows by knowledge, He depends on that knowledge. Al-Razi shows that this objection rests on a misunderstanding. He explains that on his view, the essence itself causes the relations of knowledge, so no need arises. Even on the view that distinguishes essence, attribute, and relations, the causal chain does not imply objectionable need.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite doubt
Read the doubt as presented: if God knows by knowledge, He would need that knowledge to know things. This is the accusation of dependence.
The Mu'tazila object to the attribute of knowledge as distinct from essence.
2
Al-Razi's first response
Al-Razi says if 'need' means the relations of knowledge depend on something external, he does not hold that. Instead, the essence itself necessitates those relations.
This reading avoids any implication of need.
3
Addressing the three-entity view
For those who affirm essence, attribute, and relations, Al-Razi asks: why can't the essence cause the attribute and the attribute cause the relations? That is not necessarily problematic need.
He challenges them to define what they mean by 'need'.
4
Conclusion and challenge
Al-Razi concludes that the dispute is about whether such causal chains are possible or impossible. The Mu'tazila must clarify their definition of need.
The burden is on them to show impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب من الشبهة الثانية للمعتزلة . وهي قولهم : لو كان عالما بالعلم لاحتاج في معرفة الأشياء إلى تلك الصفة - فنقول : الاحتياج لو افتقر في حصول تلك التعلقات - التي هي المسماة بالشعور والعلم - إلى شيء آخر فنحن لا نقول ذلك، بل نقول الموجب لتلك التعلقات هو نفس الذات، وعلى هذا التفسير لا يلزم تحقق الحاجة، وأما على قول من أثبت أمورًا ثلاثة الذات والمعنى والتعلقات فجوابه : لم لا يجوز أن تكون الذات موجبة للمعنى، المعنى موجبًا للتعلقات ؟ فإن أردتم بالحاجة هذا المعنى فلم قلتم إن ذلك محال؟ فإن النزاع ليس إلا فيه، وإن أردتم بالحاجة شيئًا آخر فبينوه.
As for the answer to the second doubt of the Mu'tazila, which is their statement: 'If He were knowing by knowledge, He would need that knowledge in order to know things.' We say: Need—if [you mean] that the obtaining of those relations—which are called awareness and knowledge—depends on something else, then we do not say that. Rather we say that what necessitates those relations is the essence itself. On this interpretation, need does not necessarily obtain. As for the view of one who affirms three things: the essence, the meaning (attribute), and the relations, the answer is: Why is it not permissible that the essence necessitates the meaning, and the meaning necessitates the relations? If by 'need' you mean this, why do you say that is impossible? For the dispute concerns nothing else. And if by 'need' you mean something else, then make it clear.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a response to the second Mu'tazilite doubt: if God knows by knowledge, then He would be dependent on that knowledge. Al-Razi distinguishes two interpretations. First, if they mean that the relations of knowledge require an external cause, Al-Razi says he does not hold that; rather, the essence itself produces the relations directly, so no dependence occurs. Second, if they posit three entities—essence, attribute (meaning), and relations—then Al-Razi asks: why can't the essence cause the attribute and the attribute cause the relations? That is not necessarily a 'need' in an objectionable sense. He challenges them to define what they mean by 'need'; the debate is precisely about whether such causal chains are possible or not.

Key Terms
تعلقات
شعور
حاجة
ذات
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Al-Razi denies that God has an attribute of knowledge.
Correction: Al-Razi affirms the attribute of knowledge; he only rejects that it implies need. He holds that the essence itself is the cause of the relations of knowing.

Checkpoint
How does Al-Razi respond to the accusation that if God knows by knowledge, He would need that knowledge?
A) He denies that God has knowledge.
B) He says the essence itself is the cause of the relations of knowledge, so no need arises.
C) He says knowledge is identical with essence.
D) He says need only applies to created beings.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defends the attribute of knowledge by showing that the essence itself causes the relations of knowing, so no dependence on an external attribute occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you face trials, do you ever feel that God must be reminded of your situation? How does Al-Razi's assertion that God's essence itself necessitates all knowledge—even without any external 'need'—expose that feeling as a subtle form of shirk in reliance?
?
In what specific area of your life have you been acting as if God's knowledge of you is incomplete unless you verbally inform Him? What will you do today to trust that He knows without your prompting?
?
Al-Razi distinguishes between an objectionable need and a permissible causal order. Have you been treating some of your own dependencies on Allah as 'needs' that lower your reliance? Contess one dependency you will reframe as a gift from His essence, not a weakness in His provision.

Refuting Inherence: The Infinite Regress Argument
Al-Razi presents the first of two proofs against the Mu'tazilite definition of inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the concept of spatiality to show that the Mu'tazilite definition of inherence leads to an infinite regress, proving it false.

Core Idea
A bad definition forces an absurd infinite chain of occurrences.

Al-Razi begins his response by dissecting the Mu'tazilite definition of inherence. He shows that their definition, if taken literally, would require an endless series of spatial occurrences for any attribute like 'being in a location,' which is impossible. This first proof uses logical reasoning to expose the definition's flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: the doubt is stated, then rejected, then a proof is given.
The key term 'ka'iniyya' (spatiality) appears here.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite definition
Find the definition of 'inherence' (hulul) as presented by the Mu'tazila. It says: 'the occurrence of a state in a location, following the occurrence of its locus therein.'
3
Follow the infinite regress
Trace Al-Razi's reasoning: if a body is in a location, its being-in-location (ka'iniyya) is an attribute. If inherence requires a prior occurrence of the locus in the location, then ka'iniyya would need a prior occurrence, leading to infinite regress.
The regress happens 'simultaneously' – the body would need infinite occurrences at once.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since infinite simultaneous occurrences are impossible, the definition is false. The attribute actually subsists in the body directly.
This establishes that inherence does not require the Mu'tazilite definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : حلول الشيء في الشيء عبارة عن حصول الحال في الحيز، تبعا لحصول محله فيه - فنقول : هذا التفسير باطل، ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : أن كون الجسم حاصلا في الحيز هو المسمى بالكائنية، وهي صفة في الجسم قائمة به، فلو كان الحلول عبارة عما ذكرتم لزم أن تكون هذه الكائنية حاصلة في الحيز تبعا لحصول ذلك الجسم فيه، فيكون كون الجسم موصوفا بكونه في هذا الحيز مشروطًا بحصول آخر لهذا الجسم في هذا الحيز، ثم الكلام في ذلك الحصول كالكلام في الأول . فيلزم أن يكون الجسم حاصلا في ذلك الحيز مرارا لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة، وذلك محال، فثبت أن كونه حاصلا في ذلك الحيز صفة قائمة به وحالة فيه وثبت أن ما ذكرتم من تفسير الحلول والقيام غير حاصل فيه، فثبت أن تفسير الحلول والقيام بما ذكرتم باطل. الثاني : وهو أنكم لا تنكرون أن ذات الله تعالى موصوفة بالأحوال وبالأحكام، فقد حصل كون الشيء موصوفًا وصفة بدون ما ذكرتم من التفسير، فكان ما ذكرتموه باطلا.
And the answer to the third doubt, which is their statement: 'The inherence of a thing in a thing is the occurrence of a state in a location, following the occurrence of its locus therein.' We say: This interpretation is false; two proofs indicate this. First: That a body's being located in a location is what is called spatiality (al-ka'iniyya), and it is an attribute subsisting in the body. If inherence were as you described, then it would follow that this spatiality would be occurring in the location following the occurrence of that body in it, so that the body's being described as being in this location would be conditional upon another occurrence of this body in this location. Then the discussion about that occurrence would be like the discussion about the first. This would entail that the body occurs in that location infinite times simultaneously, which is impossible. So it is established that the body's being in that location is an attribute subsisting in it and a state in it, and it is established that your interpretation of inherence and subsistence (al-qiyam) is not realized in it, so it is established that the interpretation of inherence and subsistence as you mentioned is false. Second: You do not deny that the essence of God Most High is described by states (ahwal) and by judgments (ahkam). So the fact that a thing is described and is a description occurs without your interpretation. Therefore what you mentioned is false.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage answers the third Mu'tazilite doubt, which defines inherence as the occurrence of a state in a location following the occurrence of its locus. Al-Razi gives two proofs. The first proof uses the concept of spatiality (ka'iniyya). If a body is in a location, its being in that location is a quality (ka'iniyya) that subsists in the body. If inherence requires that the state occurs in the location because the locus occurs there, then the body's spatiality would require that the body occur in the location FIRST, causing infinite regress (the body would have to be in the location an infinite number of times simultaneously). This is absurd, so the definition fails; spatiality is a self-subsisting attribute in the body, not requiring that the body be in the location in a different sense. The second proof is simpler: you (Mu'tazila) admit that God's essence is described by states and judgments without your definition of inherence, so the definition is not necessary.

Key Terms
حلول
حيز
كائنية
قيام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite definition of inherence is necessary to explain how attributes relate to a substance.
Correction: Al-Razi shows the definition leads to absurdity and is not necessary, as spatiality demonstrates self-subsistence.

Checkpoint
What concept does Al-Razi use in his first proof to refute the Mu'tazilite definition of inherence?
A) Divine essence
B) Accidents
C) Spatiality (ka'iniyya)
D) Substance

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first proof shows that the Mu'tazilite definition of inherence leads to an impossible infinite regress, so the definition is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a theological term like 'inherence', do you tend to accept definitions without thinking through their implications? Al-Razi shows that a seemingly reasonable definition leads to absurdity. Where in your own understanding of faith are you holding onto explanations you haven't examined for hidden contradictions? Will you take time this week to test one of your assumptions with rigorous reasoning?
?
The Mu'tazila believed their definition was correct, yet Al-Razi exposed its flaw. Are you willing to have your deeply held beliefs challenged by careful argument, even if it leads to uncomfortable conclusions? What belief about Allah's attributes are you most afraid to subject to logical scrutiny? Will you ask Allah to guide you to the truth, whatever it may be?
?
Al-Razi's argument uses the concept of infinite regress to show impossibility. In your personal life, are there patterns of thought or behavior that lead to an endless cycle—like making excuses or overthinking—without resolution? What concrete step can you take today to break that cycle and ground your actions on a firm foundation, just as Al-Razi grounds his proof on a clear impossibility?

Refuting Inherence: The Divine Attribute Counterexample
Al-Razi delivers the second proof against the Mu'tazilite definition of inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's second proof: the Mu'tazila themselves accept that God's essence is described by states and judgments without requiring their definition of inherence, so the definition is unnecessary.

Core Idea
Divine attributes exist without the flawed definition.

After the first proof, Al-Razi offers a simpler refutation. He points out that the Mu'tazila do not deny that God's essence is qualified by states and judgments. Yet these divine attributes do not require the problematic definition of inherence. This shows the definition is superfluous and false.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic and translation of the second proof. Notice it is very short and direct.
It is a single sentence containing a powerful counterexample.
2
Identify the key elements
Look for the terms 'ahwal' (states) and 'ahkam' (judgments). The Mu'tazila accept that God's essence is described by these.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Razi argues: Since you (Mu'tazila) affirm that God has attributes ('being described' = having attributes) without using your definition of inherence, that definition is not necessary. Hence it is false.
The definition is not needed for God; if it were true, it would be a universal condition.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this proof complements the first: one shows the definition leads to absurdity, the other shows it is unnecessary.
Together they form a decisive refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : حلول الشيء في الشيء عبارة عن حصول الحال في الحيز، تبعا لحصول محله فيه - فنقول : هذا التفسير باطل، ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : أن كون الجسم حاصلا في الحيز هو المسمى بالكائنية، وهي صفة في الجسم قائمة به، فلو كان الحلول عبارة عما ذكرتم لزم أن تكون هذه الكائنية حاصلة في الحيز تبعا لحصول ذلك الجسم فيه، فيكون كون الجسم موصوفا بكونه في هذا الحيز مشروطًا بحصول آخر لهذا الجسم في هذا الحيز، ثم الكلام في ذلك الحصول كالكلام في الأول . فيلزم أن يكون الجسم حاصلا في ذلك الحيز مرارا لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة، وذلك محال، فثبت أن كونه حاصلا في ذلك الحيز صفة قائمة به وحالة فيه وثبت أن ما ذكرتم من تفسير الحلول والقيام غير حاصل فيه، فثبت أن تفسير الحلول والقيام بما ذكرتم باطل. الثاني : وهو أنكم لا تنكرون أن ذات الله تعالى موصوفة بالأحوال وبالأحكام، فقد حصل كون الشيء موصوفًا وصفة بدون ما ذكرتم من التفسير، فكان ما ذكرتموه باطلا.
And the answer to the third doubt, which is their statement: 'The inherence of a thing in a thing is the occurrence of a state in a location, following the occurrence of its locus therein.' We say: This interpretation is false; two proofs indicate this. First: That a body's being located in a location is what is called spatiality (al-ka'iniyya), and it is an attribute subsisting in the body. If inherence were as you described, then it would follow that this spatiality would be occurring in the location following the occurrence of that body in it, so that the body's being described as being in this location would be conditional upon another occurrence of this body in this location. Then the discussion about that occurrence would be like the discussion about the first. This would entail that the body occurs in that location infinite times simultaneously, which is impossible. So it is established that the body's being in that location is an attribute subsisting in it and a state in it, and it is established that your interpretation of inherence and subsistence (al-qiyam) is not realized in it, so it is established that the interpretation of inherence and subsistence as you mentioned is false. Second: You do not deny that the essence of God Most High is described by states (ahwal) and by judgments (ahkam). So the fact that a thing is described and is a description occurs without your interpretation. Therefore what you mentioned is false.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage answers the third Mu'tazilite doubt, which defines inherence as the occurrence of a state in a location following the occurrence of its locus. Al-Razi gives two proofs. The first proof uses the concept of spatiality (ka'iniyya). If a body is in a location, its being in that location is a quality (ka'iniyya) that subsists in the body. If inherence requires that the state occurs in the location because the locus occurs there, then the body's spatiality would require that the body occur in the location FIRST, causing infinite regress (the body would have to be in the location an infinite number of times simultaneously). This is absurd, so the definition fails; spatiality is a self-subsisting attribute in the body, not requiring that the body be in the location in a different sense. The second proof is simpler: you (Mu'tazila) admit that God's essence is described by states and judgments without your definition of inherence, so the definition is not necessary.

أحكام
ذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite definition of inherence is necessary for all instances where an attribute is predicated of a subject.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that divine attributes exist without that definition, so it is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What do the Mu'tazila accept about God's essence according to Al-Razi's second proof?
A) It is subject to inherence in a locus.
B) It is described by states and judgments.
C) It has no attributes.
D) It is located in a place.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's second proof shows that the Mu'tazilite definition is unnecessary since divine attributes exist without it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila were willing to admit that their definition of inherence failed for God. Are you willing to abandon any comfortable belief about Allah when it conflicts with clear revelation? Examine your heart: is there a cherished idea that you cling to even though it might undermine Allah's majesty? Commit to surrendering it today.
?
In this proof, Al-Razi uses the Mu'tazila's own acceptance of God's attributes to refute them. Do you accept that Allah's attributes are not separate from His essence but also not identical? Or do you find yourself thinking of them as 'add-ons'? What does that imply about your real belief? Pray for sincerity and seek the truth from the sources.
?
The second proof shows that a definition can be false without being contradictory—it simply fails to cover an obvious case. What beliefs do you hold that might be similarly incomplete or inaccurate because you never tested them against the full scope of divine revelation? Resolve to identify one such belief this week and investigate it thoroughly.

Answer to the Second Mu'tazilite Objection
Razi answers the Mu'tazila's second objection on divine attributes and need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that divine attributes imply need, and see how he defends the Ash'ari position that the essence itself is sufficient.

Core Idea
Attributes do not imply need; the essence itself necessitates knowledge.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God knows through an attribute, He would need that attribute to know, implying dependence. Razi responds by clarifying that the Ash'ari view does not posit such need. Instead, the essence directly necessitates the relations called knowledge. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the Mu'tazilite objection: if God knows by an attribute, He needs that attribute to know, implying dependence.
This is the second objection mentioned by Razi.
2
Examine Razi's first answer
Razi says the essence itself necessitates the relations of knowledge, so no need for an external attribute.
This applies to those who posess only essence and attributes, not three things.
3
Analyze the second answer
For those who accept three entities (essence, attribute, relations), Razi asks why it is impermissible that essence necessitates attribute and attribute necessitates relations.
This shifts the burden of proof to the Mu'tazila to show impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب من الشبهة الثانية للمعتزلة . وهي قولهم : لو كان عالما بالعلم لاحتاج في معرفة الأشياء إلى تلك الصفة - فنقول : الاحتياج لو افتقر في حصول تلك التعلقات - التي هي المسماة بالشعور والعلم - إلى شيء آخر فنحن لا نقول ذلك، بل نقول الموجب لتلك التعلقات هو نفس الذات، وعلى هذا التفسير لا يلزم تحقق الحاجة، وأما على قول من أثبت أمورًا ثلاثة الذات والمعنى والتعلقات فجوابه : لم لا يجوز أن تكون الذات موجبة للمعنى، المعنى موجبًا للتعلقات ؟ فإن أردتم بالحاجة هذا المعنى فلم قلتم إن ذلك محال؟ فإن النزاع ليس إلا فيه، وإن أردتم بالحاجة شيئًا آخر فبينوه.
As for the answer to the second objection of the Mu'tazila: their saying, 'If He were knowing by knowledge, He would need that attribute in order to know things' — we say: need, if it were that the attainment of those relations, which are called perception and knowledge, depends on something else, then we do not say that. Rather, we say that the one who necessitates those relations is the very essence. On this interpretation, the actualization of need does not follow. As for the view of those who posit three things: the essence, the attribute, and the relations, the answer is: why is it not permissible that the essence necessitates the attribute, and the attribute necessitates the relations? If by 'need' you mean this meaning, then why do you say that is impossible? The dispute is only about this. And if by 'need' you mean something else, then clarify it.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's answer to the second objection of the Mu'tazila. The objection is that if God knows through an attribute called 'knowledge,' then He would need that attribute to know, implying dependence. Razi responds by first clarifying that the Ash'ari position does not claim that the essence depends on the attribute to produce the relations called knowledge; rather, the essence itself directly necessitates those relations. For those who accept a threefold distinction (essence, attribute, relations), the answer is that the essence can necessitate the attribute, and the attribute then necessitates the relations, and this chain does not imply that God needed something external. The concept of 'need' is thus redefined, and the dispute is shown to be semantic.

Key Terms
شبهة
علم
ذات
تعلقات
حاجة
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes imply that God needs them to act or know.
Correction: In the Ash'ari view, the essence directly necessitates the relations of knowledge; attributes are not external needs but are inherent to the essence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's answer, what directly necessitates the relations of knowledge?
A) The attribute of knowledge
B) The essence itself
C) An external cause
D) The attribute and essence together

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the divine essence is sufficient for knowledge, removing the charge of need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila saw attributes as implying lack. Examine your own heart: when you think of God's attributes, do you ever subconsciously feel He is 'missing' something? How does Razi's reminder that essence itself necessitates knowledge free you from that error?
?
Razi insists that God's knowledge does not come from an external source. In your life, where do you look for knowledge and security? Are you relying on something other than God's direct essence? What would it take to shift that trust?
?
The objection of 'need' is deflected by clarifying that God's essence is the source. When you feel needy or lacking, do you turn to God as the ultimate source, or do you seek other crutches? How can this lesson transform your dependence on Him from panic to peace?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Definition of Inherence
Razi gives two arguments against the Mu'tazilite definition of ḥulūl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the Mu'tazilite spatial definition of inherence using two arguments.

Core Idea
Inherence is not spatial; spatializing it leads to infinite regress.

The Mu'tazilites argued that attributes 'inhere' in God spatially, like a state occurring in a location. Razi rejects this definition with two decisive arguments. The first shows an infinite regress, the second points to the Mu'tazilites' own admission that God is described by states without spatiality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's response
Carefully read the Mu'tazilite definition of ḥulūl and Razi's initial rejection. Notice the two arguments he introduces.
The first argument is longer and involves logical consequences.
2
Analyze the first argument's infinite regress
Follow the reasoning: If inherence requires an attribute to occur in a location following its locus, then the attribute 'being-in-a-place' would need another occurrence, leading to an infinite chain. Identify why this is impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the second argument
Razi notes that the Mu'tazilites themselves admit God's essence is described by states without such spatial inherence. Reflect on why this makes their definition self-defeating.
This argument is more direct and relies on their own admission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : حلول الشيء في الشيء عبارة عن حصول الحال في الحيز، تبعا لحصول محله فيه - فنقول : هذا التفسير باطل، ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : أن كون الجسم حاصلا في الحيز هو المسمى بالكائنية، وهي صفة في الجسم قائمة به، فلو كان الحلول عبارة عما ذكرتم لزم أن تكون هذه الكائنية حاصلة في الحيز تبعا لحصول ذلك الجسم فيه، فيكون كون الجسم موصوفا بكونه في هذا الحيز مشروطًا بحصول آخر لهذا الجسم في هذا الحيز، ثم الكلام في ذلك الحصول كالكلام في الأول . فيلزم أن يكون الجسم حاصلا في ذلك الحيز مرارا لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة، وذلك محال، فثبت أن كونه حاصلا في ذلك الحيز صفة قائمة به وحالة فيه وثبت أن ما ذكرتم من تفسير الحلول والقيام غير حاصل فيه، فثبت أن تفسير الحلول والقيام بما ذكرتم باطل. الثاني : وهو أنكم لا تنكرون أن ذات الله تعالى موصوفة بالأحوال وبالأحكام، فقد حصل كون الشيء موصوفًا وصفة بدون ما ذكرتم من التفسير، فكان ما ذكرتموه باطلا. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الذات والصفة حقيقتان مختلفتان لذاتيهما، فقول القائل: ليس أحدهما أن يكون موصوفًا والآخر صفة أولى من العكس، إن كان المراد به أنا لا نعرف ما به حصلت تلك الأولوية فهذا مسلم، ولكن لا يلزم من عدم به عدمه في نفسه، وإن أردتم به عدم الأولوية في نفس الأمر فهذا غير مسلم؛ لأن الذات والصفة لما كانتا مختلفتين في الماهية فلعله حصلت هذه الأولوية لنفس تلك الماهية ، وإن كنا لا نعرف ما لأجله حصلت هذه الأولوية فسقط هذا الكلام بالكلية.
And the answer to the third objection: their saying, 'The indwelling (ḥulūl) of a thing in a thing is defined as the occurrence of the state in the location, following the occurrence of its locus in it' — we say: this interpretation is false, and two aspects indicate that. The first: that the body's being in the location is called 'being-in-a-place' (kāʾiniyya), which is an attribute subsisting in the body. So if ḥulūl were defined as you mentioned, it would necessarily follow that this kāʾiniyya occurs in the location following the occurrence of that body in it. Then the body's being described as being in this location would be conditioned by another occurrence of this body in this location. Then the discussion about that occurrence is like the discussion about the first. This would necessitate that the body occurs in that location an infinite number of times at once, which is impossible. So it is established that its being in that location is an attribute subsisting in it and a state in it, and it is established that your interpretation of ḥulūl and qiyām (subsistence) does not apply to it. So it is established that the interpretation of ḥulūl and qiyām as you mentioned is false. The second: you do not deny that the essence of God Almighty is described by states and rulings. So the thing's being described and an attribute have been realized without that interpretation you mentioned. Therefore, what you mentioned is false. And when you understand this, we say: the essence and the attribute are two distinct realities (ḥaqīqatān) by their very essences. So the saying of one who says, 'It is not more appropriate for one of them to be described and the other to be an attribute than the opposite' — if what is meant is that we do not know by what that priority occurred, this is conceded, but the ignorance of it does not entail its non-existence in itself. And if you mean the non-existence of priority in reality, this is not conceded, because since the essence and the attribute differ in quiddity, it is possible that this priority occurred due to that very quiddity, even if we do not know the reason for that priority. So this argument collapses entirely.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage provides Razi's response to the third Mu'tazilite objection, which defines 'inherence' (ḥulūl) of an attribute in an essence as the occurrence of a state in a spatial location following its locus. Razi refutes this definition with two arguments. First, if inherence required the attribute to occur in a location following its locus, then the attribute of 'being-in-a-place' (kāʾiniyya) would itself need another occurrence in the same location to subsist, leading to an infinite regress of occurrences occurring simultaneously—an impossibility. This shows that the attribute does not 'inhere' in the sense defined; rather, it is a state subsisting in the essence directly. Second, the Mu'tazila themselves admit that God's essence is described by states and rulings without needing such a spatial inherence, so their definition is unnecessary. Razi then clarifies that essence and attribute are two distinct realities. The opponent's claim that neither has priority in being the subject or attribute is refuted: ignorance of the cause of priority does not disprove priority, and ontological priority can be rooted in the very quiddities of essence and attribute.

Key Terms
حلول
كائنية
حيز
حالة
قيام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes inhere in God like a body in a place.
Correction: Razi demonstrates this leads to infinite regress and is self-defeating; attributes subsist directly in the essence without spatiality.

Checkpoint
What does the first argument against the Mu'tazilite definition of ḥulūl demonstrate?
A) That attributes are spatial
B) That the definition leads to an infinite regress
C) That attributes are identical to the essence
D) That God is not in a location

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite definition of inherence is false because it either leads to an infinite regress or is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining Allah's attributes (like His Power or Knowledge) as if they are 'inside' Him in a spatial sense? Where did that image come from, and what can you do today to remove it from your mind, knowing that He is beyond all location and that such spatialization is a limited human projection?
?
Razi points out that even his opponents, the Mu'tazilites, describe Allah with attributes without requiring spatial inherence. Are there any beliefs or practices you hold that are contradicted by your own daily actions? What area of your life hides such a contradiction, and what is one step you will take to align your practice with the truth you now see?
?
The infinite regress argument shows that trying to explain inherence spatially leads to absurdity. Have you ever held onto an explanation about Allah that, if you followed it logically, would collapse into nonsense? What familiar but flawed concept about Allah do you need to abandon, even if it feels comfortable, so that you can worship Him as He truly is?

Divine Attributes

5 min
Essence and Attribute as Distinct Realities
Razi clarifies the ontological priority of essence over attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that essence and attribute are distinct realities and that priority of one over the other is rooted in their quiddities.

Core Idea
Priority between essence and attribute is real, even if its cause is unknown.

After refuting the spatial definition, Razi addresses a deeper point: the Mu'tazilite claim that there is no inherent priority between essence and attribute. Razi argues that they are distinct quiddities, and priority can be rooted in that very distinction, even if we don't know the reason.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The Mu'tazilite says: 'It is not more appropriate for one to be described and the other to be an attribute than the opposite.' Recognize that this challenges the asymmetry between essence and attribute.
This claim aims to undermine the real distinction.
2
Razi's distinction between ignorance and non-existence
Razi splits the claim into two interpretations: (1) 'we do not know the cause of priority' and (2) 'priority does not exist in reality'. He concedes the first but rejects the second.
The key move: ignorance of cause ≠ absence of cause.
3
The possibility of priority in the quiddity
Razi argues that since essence and attribute are different in quiddity (māhiyya), priority could be rooted in that very difference, even if we cannot specify the precise reason.
This is a modal argument: it is possible that priority is intrinsic.
4
Conclusion: the objection collapses
Because the opponent cannot prove that priority does not exist in reality, and Razi has shown it is possible, the objection fails.
This completes Razi's response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

D) He says priority is impossible

Key Takeaway
The ontological priority of essence over attribute is real and grounded in their distinct quiddities, even if its specific cause is unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you cannot fully understand why something is true in your faith — like the priority of Allah's essence over His attributes — do you ever find yourself tempted to downplay or deny its reality? How does Razi's distinction between ignorance of the cause and non-existence challenge that tendency in your own heart? What will you do the next time you face a divine truth that surpasses your comprehension?
?
You claim to believe that Allah's essence and attributes are real and distinct, but does your daily worship reflect that priority? When you call upon Allah by His names and attributes, do you truly sense that you are addressing the very essence behind them, or do you treat the attributes as mere concepts? Today, choose one attribute of Allah and meditate specifically on how it flows from His essence — then act on that reflection in your next prayer.
?
Razi argues that priority can be rooted in quiddity even without our knowing the reason. What areas of your life — your relationships, your struggles, your doubts — have you abandoned because you couldn't find a satisfactory reason? Will you humbly accept that some realities are grounded in truths beyond your grasp, and commit to acting on what you know rather than waiting for full clarity?

Eternity as a Positive Attribute: Razi's Defense
Razi argues that eternity (qidam) is a positive concept and defends divine attribute reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi argues that eternity (qidam) is a positive attribute and how he defends the reality of divine attributes against Mu'tazilite objections.

Core Idea
Eternity is not just 'no beginning'—it includes a positive reality distinguishable from nothingness.

This lesson examines Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's defense of divine attribute reality in response to the Mutazilite claim that eternity is merely a negative concept. Razi redefines eternity as the negation of being preceded by non-existence, arguing that the state of 'being preceded by non-existence' is itself a positive existential attribute. Through careful reasoning, he shows that this positive aspect cannot be reduced to pure nothingness, and that different quiddities can share such attributes without compromising their distinctness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazila define eternity as 'negation of prior non-existence', a purely negative concept. Recognize why this would undermine positive attributes.
2
Follow Razi's redefinition
Razi redefines eternity as 'negation of being preceded by non-existence', shifting focus to the state of being preceded, which he argues is an existence-related matter.
3
Understand the positive aspect
Razi argues that 'being preceded by non-existence' is distinguishable from pure nothingness, so it must be a positive attribute. This counters the Mu'tazilite reduction.
He also addresses the infinite regress objection by appealing to intuitive distinction.
4
Examine the analogy of shared concomitants
Even if eternity is positive, the Mu'tazila may object that sharing eternity would make essence and attribute identical. Razi replies that different quiddities can share a common concomitant, just as originated things share 'being originated'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن شبهتهم الرابعة : أنا لا نسلم أن القدم مفهوم ثبوتي. قوله : القدم عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق قلنا لا نسلم بل هو عبارة عن نفي كون الشيء مسبوقاً بذلك العدم، وكونه مسبوقا بالعدم أمر وجودي. لا يقال لو كان كونه مسبوقا بالعدم أمرًا وجوديًا لكان ذلك الزائد إما أن يكون قديما فيلزم أن تكون الذات محدثة والصفة قديمة وهو محال، أو يكون محدثاً فيكون مسبوقاً بالعدم فتكون المسبوقية زائدة عليه، ولزم التسلسل، لأنا نقول: هذا معارض من وجه آخر وهو أن مسبوقية وجوده بالعدم صفة من صفات وجوده، ونحن ندرك بالضرورة تفرقة بين هذه الصفة وبين نفس العدم المحض، فوجب أن تكون هذه المسبوقية صفة ثبوتية. سلمنا أن القدم أمر ثبوتي فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : الذات والصفة حقيقتان مختلفتان لذاتيهما إلا أنهما مشتركتان في هذا القدم؟ والأشياء المختلفة لا يبعد في العقل اشتراكها في لازم واحد والذي يحقق ما ذكرناه أن الحوادث مختلفة في الماهيات ومشتركة في كونها حادثة ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك في القدم؟
And the answer to their fourth objection: we do not concede that eternity (qidam) is a positive concept. Their statement: 'Eternity is the negation of prior non-existence' — we say: we do not concede. Rather, it is the negation of the thing's being preceded by that non-existence, and its being preceded by non-existence is an existence-related matter (amr wujūdī). It cannot be said: if its being preceded by non-existence were an existence-related matter, then that additional thing would either be eternal, necessitating that the essence is originated and the attribute is eternal—which is impossible—or it would be originated, then it would be preceded by non-existence, so the precedence would be additional to it, and an infinite regress would follow. We say: this is countered from another angle: the precedence of its existence by non-existence is an attribute of its existence, and we necessarily perceive a distinction between this attribute and pure non-existence itself. Therefore, this precedence must be a positive attribute. And if we concede that eternity is a positive matter, then why is it not permissible to say: the essence and the attribute are two distinct realities by their essences, but they share in this eternity? Different things are not intellectually impossible to share in one concomitant (lāzim). What verifies what we have mentioned is that originated things are different in quiddities and share in their being originated. So why is it not permissible that the case is likewise with eternity?
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth Mu'tazilite objection regarding the concept of eternity (qidam). The Mu'tazila claim that 'eternity' is a purely negative concept (the negation of prior non-existence), implying that attributes cannot be positive. Razi counters by redefining eternity as 'the negation of being preceded by non-existence', and argues that 'being preceded by non-existence' is a positive attribute (amr wujūdī) of existence itself, distinguishable from pure nothingness. He then dismisses the opponent's potential infinite regress argument by insisting on the intuitive distinction between the attribute of precedence and non-existence. Even if one concedes that eternity is positive, the Mu'tazila further object that if both essence and attribute share eternity, they would be identical. Razi replies that different quiddities can share a common concomitant, just as different originated things share the attribute of being originated. Thus, the objection fails.

ثبوتي
وجودي
مسبوقية
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that eternity (qidam) is merely a negative concept, i.e., the simple absence of a beginning, and therefore not a real positive attribute.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that 'being preceded by non-existence' is an existence-related attribute that we distinguish from pure non-existence, making it a positive attribute.

Checkpoint

A) A purely negative concept
B) A positive attribute of existence
C) An illusion
D) Identical to non-existence

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that eternity involves a positive aspect—'being preceded by non-existence'—which is a real attribute distinguishable from nothingness, thereby defending the reality of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi insists that eternity is not merely a 'no beginning' but a positive reality. Razi uses logical argument to defend a truth about Allah. Are you content to accept core beliefs about Allah without ever wrestling with doubts or objections? What is one attribute of Allah that you struggle to feel is 'real' rather than just a concept? Commit to studying it with the same depth Razi gives to eternity.

The Ash'ari Formula as a Verbal Dispute
Razi distinguishes two meanings of 'other' to resolve the verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi resolves the fifth Mu'tazilite objection by distinguishing two meanings of 'other' and showing the dispute is verbal.

Core Idea
Razi shows the disagreement is about the meaning of 'other', not about the attributes themselves.

This passage tackles the fifth Mu'tazilite objection to the Ash'ari claim that the divine attributes are neither identical to the essence nor entirely separate. Opponents see this as contradictory. Razi argues that the dispute is purely verbal (lafẓī) and depends on what 'other' (ghayr) means. By distinguishing two senses of 'other', he shows the Ash'ari position is consistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and the English translation carefully to get a sense of Razi's argument. Notice the structure: objection, response, two conditional distinctions.
Pay attention to the phrase 'هذا بحث لفظي' – this is the core claim.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'other'
Razi presents two possible interpretations of 'other' (غَيْر). List them: one requiring independent existence, the other simply denoting distinctness in description.
The word 'غَيْر' is used in different senses in kalam.
3
Understand why the dispute is verbal
Reflect on how the disagreement dissolves once each side clarifies which meaning of 'other' they intend. The Mu'tazila assume one meaning; the Ash'aris use another.
This is a classic example of a lafẓī (verbal) dispute.
4
Connect to the broader theology
Consider why this resolution matters for the doctrine of divine attributes. It upholds the real distinction between essence and attributes without compromising tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن شبهتهم الخامسة : أن أصحابنا قالوا : الصفة مع الذات لا هو ولا غيره واستبعد المخالفون ذلك، وهذا البحث لفظي، فإنا نقول : إن عنيتم بالغيرين أشياء مستقلة بالذات والحقيقة فلا نسلم أن الذات والصفة غيران على هذا التفسير، وإن عنيتم بالغيرين كل شيئين سواء كانا مستقلين أو كان أحدهما صفة للآخر، والآخر موصوفًا به فلم قلتم : إن لإثبات شيئين متغايرين قديمين بهذا التفسير باطل، فإن النزاع ما وقع إلا فيه ؟
And the answer to their fifth objection: that our companions (Ash'aris) said: 'The attribute together with the essence is neither It nor other than It,' and the opponents deemed that improbable. This inquiry is verbal. For we say: if by 'two others' you mean things independent in essence and reality, then we do not concede that the essence and attribute are two others on this interpretation. And if by 'two others' you mean any two things, whether they are independent or one is an attribute of the other and the other is described by it, then why do you say that affirming two distinct eternal things on this interpretation is false? The dispute only revolves around this.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage addresses the fifth Mu'tazilite objection, which targets the famous Ash'ari formula: 'The attribute is neither the essence nor other than it.' The opponents find this paradoxical. Razi argues that the dispute is merely verbal (lafẓī). He distinguishes two meanings of 'other' (ghayr). If 'other' means two independently existing entities, then indeed the attribute is not 'other' in that sense. But if 'other' simply means two distinct things in the sense that one is the attribute and the other is the described subject (without implying independent existence), then there is no problem in affirming two distinct eternal things in this sense. The real dispute is only about this second meaning, and the Ash'ari position is consistent.

Key Terms
غير
صفة
ذات
لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think that 'other' necessarily means independently existing, so the Ash'ari formula appears contradictory.
Correction: Razi explains that 'other' can also mean distinct in description without independent existence, thus the Ash'ari position is valid.

Checkpoint

A) By claiming they are identical
B) By saying attributes are entirely separate
C) By clarifying that 'other' can mean distinct but not independent
D) By rejecting the distinction altogether

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that resolving the meaning of 'other' removes the apparent contradiction in the Ash'ari view on attributes.

Answering the Christian Hypostasis Analogy
Razi contrasts Ash'ari attributes with Christian hypostases to answer the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi rejects the Mu'tazilite analogy between Ash'ari attributes and Christian hypostases.

Core Idea
Christian hypostases are independent essences; Ash'ari attributes are not.

The Mu'tazila argued that Ash'ari attributes resemble the Christian Trinity. Razi refutes this by highlighting a crucial difference: Christians treat their 'attributes' (hypostases) as independent essences capable of transfer, whereas Ash'ari attributes have no independence. This passage clarifies the defining line between Islamic orthodoxy and Christian doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice that the passage is cut off mid-sentence, but the main argument is clear.
The text may continue on the next page, but we have enough to understand Razi's point.
2
Identify the key distinction
Razi states that God disbelieved the Christians because they affirmed attributes that are in reality essences (dhawāt). Find this claim in the text.
The Mu'tazila conflate Ash'ari attributes with Christian hypostases.
3
Understand the evidence of transfer
Razi points to the Christian doctrine of the transfer (intiqāl) of the hypostasis of the Word from God to Jesus. Why does this show independence?
A thing that can transfer independently must be self-subsistent and independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So even though they call them attributes, they are in reality saying that they are
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's answer to the sixth Mu'tazilite objection, which draws an analogy between Ash'ari attributes and the Christian doctrine of hypostases (aqānīm). The Mu'tazila argue that if attributes are real, they would be like the Christian Trinity. Razi responds by noting that God disbelieved the Christians because they held that their attributes (like the Word) are actually independent essences capable of transfer (e.g., the incarnation of the Word into Jesus). This shows that Christians treat their 'attributes' as independent entities, whereas Ash'ari attributes are not independent, transferable, or self-subsistent. The text is cut off, but the point is clear: the analogy fails because Christian doctrine affirms independence and transferability, which Ash'aris deny.

Qidam as a Positive Attribute: Al-Razi's Defense
Al-Razi refutes the definition of qidam as purely negative and defends it as a positive attribute, addressing regress and using analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi defends the view that qidam (eternity) is a positive attribute (amr wujudi) and how he counters objections about infinite regress using a key distinction.

Core Idea
Qidam is not mere negation but a positive attribute of not being preceded by non-existence.

In this passage, al-Razi tackles a major objection against the reality of divine attributes: that qidam is merely a negative concept. He transforms the definition to show it entails a positive state, then defends this view against a regress argument, and finally uses an analogy from created things to reinforce his point. This lesson will walk you through his multi-layered reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how al-Razi first rejects the opponent's definition, then anticipates a counter-argument, responds, and finally concedes a point to strengthen his case.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا نسلم' — it signals a rejection of a premise.
2
Identify the opponent's initial claim
The opponent says qidam is an expression for the negation of prior non-existence. Al-Razi redefines it as the negation of being preceded by non-existence. Why is this shift important?
The key is that 'being preceded' is a positive state, not a pure absence.
3
Trace the infinite regress objection and al-Razi's reply
The opponent argues: if 'being preceded' is a positive attribute, then it either is eternal (making the essence originated) or originated (leading to regress). Al-Razi counters by pointing out that such precedence is a real distinguishable attribute of existence, so the regress argument fails.
Al-Razi uses a 'tanaqud' (contradiction) approach: the opponent's own logic would lead to absurdity.
4
Understand the analogy with temporal events
Al-Razi concedes that qidam is positive (for argument) and then gives an example: temporal events differ in essence but share the attribute of being originated. Similarly, the divine essence and attribute may share qidam while being distinct.
This analogy undermines the opponent's claim that attributes cannot be real if they share a common attribute.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Review how al-Razi uses logical steps to defend the reality of attributes. The key is that qidam involves a positive relational attribute (precedence) and that sharing a common attribute does not erase distinction.
This passage is a good example of kalam dialectic: definition, objection, counter-objection, and analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن شبهتهم الرابعة : أنا لا نسلم أن القدم مفهوم ثبوتي. قوله : القدم عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق قلنا لا نسلم بل هو عبارة عن نفي كون الشيء مسبوقاً بذلك العدم، وكونه مسبوقا بالعدم أمر وجودي. لا يقال لو كان كونه مسبوقا بالعدم أمرًا وجوديًا لكان ذلك الزائد إما أن يكون قديما فيلزم أن تكون الذات محدثة والصفة قديمة وهو محال، أو يكون محدثاً فيكون مسبوقاً بالعدم فتكون المسبوقية زائدة عليه، ولزم التسلسل، لأنا نقول: هذا معارض من وجه آخر وهو أن مسبوقية وجوده بالعدم صفة من صفات وجوده، ونحن ندرك بالضرورة تفرقة بين هذه الصفة وبين نفس العدم المحض، فوجب أن تكون هذه المسبوقية صفة ثبوتية. سلمنا أن القدم الأمر ثبوتي فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : الذات والصفة حقيقتان مختلفتان لذاتيهما إلا أنهما مشتركتان في هذا القدم؟ والأشياء المختلفة لا يبعد في العقل اشتراكها في لازم واحد والذي يحقق ما ذكرناه أن الحوادث مختلفة في الماهيات ومشتركة في كونها حادثة ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك في القدم؟
And the answer to their fourth doubt: We do not accept that qidam is a positive concept. He said: 'Qidam is an expression for the negation of prior non-existence.' We say: We do not accept; rather it is an expression for the negation of the thing being preceded by that non-existence, and its being preceded by non-existence is an existent matter (amr wujudi). It cannot be said: If its being preceded by non-existence were an existent matter, then that extra (attribute) would either be eternal, necessitating that the essence is originated and the attribute eternal, which is impossible; or it would be originated, thus being preceded by non-existence, so the precedence would be additional to it, and infinite regress would follow. For we say: This is countered from another angle: that the precedence of its existence by non-existence is an attribute of its existence, and we perceive by necessity a distinction between this attribute and pure non-existence itself. So this precedence must be a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya). We concede that qidam is a positive thing. Then why can it not be said that the essence and the attribute are two realities distinct in themselves except that they share in this qidam? Different things are not rationally prevented from sharing a single necessary concomitant (lazim). What confirms our point is that temporal events differ in essences and share in being originated (haditha). So why can it not be the case with qidam?
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. Al-Razi responds to the fourth objection against the reality of divine attributes. The opponent argues that qidam (eternity) is a negative concept, i.e., merely the absence of prior non-existence. Al-Razi first rejects that premise: qidam is not a negation of non-existence; rather it is the negation of being preceded by non-existence, which is a positive state ('being preceded' is an existent attribute). He then anticipates an infinite regress objection: if this 'precedence' is a positive attribute, then either it is eternal (making the essence originated) or originated (leading to regress). Al-Razi counters by pointing out that precedence is a real attribute of existence, distinguishable from pure non-existence, so it must be positive. The regress argument is thus neutralized. Finally, he concedes for argument's sake that qidam is positive and uses it to show that distinct essences can share a common attribute, like temporal events sharing originatedness. This undermines the opponent's claim that attributes cannot be real.

Key Terms
القدم (qidam)
ثبوتي (thubuti)
مسبوقية (precedence)
صفة ثبوتية (positive attribute)
لازم (lazim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qidam (eternity) is a purely negative concept, equivalent to absence of beginning or non-existence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that qidam is a positive attribute because it entails the negation of being preceded by non-existence, and 'being preceded' is an existent matter (amr wujudi).

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's response, what does qidam (eternity) truly express?
A) The negation of non-existence altogether
B) The negation of being preceded by non-existence, which is a positive state
C) The absence of beginning
D) A pure negation

Key Takeaway
Qidam is a positive attribute because it involves the real state of not being preceded by non-existence, and distinct realities can share such an attribute, as seen in temporal events sharing originatedness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is eternal,' do you actually feel that as a positive reality of His being, or is it just the absence of a beginning in your mind? How does al-Razi's insistence that qidam is thubūtī challenge you to deepen your understanding of God's attributes beyond mere negations?
?
Al-Razi uses an analogy: temporal events share 'originatedness' despite different essences. If you truly internalize that even created things share attributes, how should that affect your humility before God when you affirm that His attributes are real and distinct from His essence? Are you willing to accept that your mind cannot fully grasp this, yet you must affirm it as true?
?
This argument shows that we perceive a distinction between precedence and non-existence by necessity. Is there an area of your faith where you have reduced a positive truth about Allah to a mere negation to avoid intellectual discomfort? What would it mean for your worship to treat each of His attributes as a positive, real quality that demands a response from you?
