Response to the First Objection on Divine Power
Razi answers the first objection on divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's dialectical response to the first objection that uses the equal rulings of created powers to deny God's power.

Core Idea
Equality of rulings does not require a single cause; possibility suffices to refute.

The first objection argued that since created powers cannot create bodies, God's power, being similar in concept, also cannot. Razi counters by showing that the ruling can be explained by the specific essences or by a common description that excludes God's power. He also turns the argument against the opponent by linking it to the permissibility of seeing God. This lesson examines the structure of his response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that if created powers cannot create bodies, then God's power, being similarly described as power, also cannot.
2
First answer: specific essences
Razi suggests that the inability might be due to the specific essence of each power, not a common factor.
This entails different causes for the same ruling, which is not impossible.
3
Second answer: common description with exclusion
If one insists on a common description, Razi proposes that the common description (being power) might include a restriction that applies only to contingent powers, excluding God.
We may not know that description, but possibility is enough.
4
Turning the argument
Razi notes that the same reasoning is used by Ash'arites to prove the permissibility of seeing God, which the opponents deny.
This reveals inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبه التي تمسكتموا بها في نفي قدرة الله تعالى فنقول : أما الجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : فهو أنه لا نزاع في أن القدرة التي فيها الشاهد لا تصلح لخلق الأجسام ولا نزاع في أنه لا بد من تعليل هذا الحكم، لكن لم لا يجوز تعليل هذا الحكم بخصوصية ماهية كل واحد من تلك القُدَر؟ أقصى ما في الباب أنه يلزم تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة، إلا ان هذا عندكم غير ممتنع، وهذا هو الحق لما بينا أن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهية لا يمتنع اشتراكها في بعض اللوازم. سلمنا أنه لا بد من تعليل هذه الأحكام المتساوية بوصف واحد مشترك بينها ولا مشترك بينها إلا كونها قدرًا، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذه القدر مشتركة في وصف، لأجل ذلك الوصف يمتنع خلق الجسم بها إلا أن ذلك الوصف لا تندرج فيه القدرة القديمة، وتندرج فيه جميع هذه القدر الحادثة؟ أقصى ما في الباب أنا لا تعرف ذلك الوصف ولكن السائل يكفيه مجرد الاحتمال وأيضًا فهذا هو عين الدليل الذي يتمسك به أصحابنا في جواز الرؤية، فإن صح هذا الدليل لزمكم القطع بجواز الرؤية على الله تعالى، وأنتم لا تقولون به. والجواب عن شبهتهم الثانية : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: تلك القدرة القديمة مخالفة لهذه القدرة التي في الشاهد؟ قوله : "ليست مخالفة تلك القدرة لهذه القدرة أعظم من مخالفة بعضها لبعض" قلنا : هذا في غاية الركاكة، لاحتمال أن تكون تلك القدرة القديمة لها خصوصية، ولا توجد تلك الخصوصية في شيء من القدر الموجودة في الشاهد فلا جرم كانت تلك القدرة صالحة لخلق الأجسام ولم تكن غيرها صالحة لهذا المعنى، ومع قيام الاحتمال بطل ما ذكرتم .
As for the objections you have clung to in negating the power of Allah, we say: As for the answer to the first objection: It is that there is no dispute that the power in the witnessed realm is not fit for creating bodies, and there is no dispute that this ruling must be explained. But why is it not permissible to explain this ruling by the specificity of the essence of each one of those powers? The most that can be said is that it entails explaining equal rulings by different causes, but this is not impossible according to you, and this is the truth because we have shown that different things in essence are not prevented from sharing some properties. We concede that these equal rulings must be explained by a single description common between them, and there is nothing common between them except being power (qudra). So why is it not permissible to say: These powers share a description, due to which description the creation of a body is impossible with them, except that that description does not include the eternal power, but includes all these contingent powers? The most that can be said is that we do not know that description, but the questioner is satisfied by mere possibility. Also, this is exactly the argument that our companions use for the permissibility of vision. So if this argument is valid, it necessitates for you a definitive ruling on the permissibility of seeing Allah, yet you do not say that. And the answer to their second objection: Why is it not permissible to say: That eternal power is different from this power in the witnessed realm? His statement: 'The difference of that power from this power is not greater than the difference of some of them from each other' — we say: This is extremely weak, because it is possible that that eternal power has a particularity, and that particularity is not found in any of the powers existing in the witnessed realm. Consequently, that power is fit for creating bodies while others are not fit for this meaning. And with the existence of possibility, what you mentioned becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's response to two objections concerning divine power. The first objection argues that since created powers (qudar) cannot create bodies, God's power, being similar in concept, also cannot create bodies. Razi answers by first noting that the ruling that witnessed powers cannot create bodies must have a cause. It could be the specific essence (māhiyya) of each power, even if different causes lead to the same ruling—this is not impossible. Alternatively, if one insists on a single common description (being power), Razi suggests that the commonality might include a specific restriction that applies only to contingent powers and not to God's eternal power. This is a possible explanation, and mere possibility suffices to refute the objection. Razi further turns the argument against the opponents: the same reasoning is used by Ash'arites to prove the permissibility of seeing God, which the opponents deny. The second objection argues that if God's power were different from created powers, it would be no more different than created powers differ from each other, so the difference is insufficient to grant creative ability. Razi dismisses this as extremely weak (ghāyat al-rakāka), because it is possible that God's power has a unique particularity (khuṣūṣiyya) absent in all created powers, which explains its ability to create bodies. Per possibility, the objection fails.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra, power)
ماهية (māhiyya, essence)
وصف (waṣf, description/attribute)
أحكام متساوية (aḥkām mutasāwiya, equal rulings)
خصوصية (khuṣūṣiyya, particularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi admits that we do not know the description, so his argument is weak.
Correction: Razi's point is that mere possibility suffices; we do not need to know the description to refute the objection.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is it not necessary to know the specific description that prevents created powers from creating bodies?
A) Because the opponent concedes the possibility.
B) Because mere possibility is enough to refute the objection.
C) Because the description is known through revelation.
D) Because all powers are identical.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the first objection by showing that equal rulings can have different causes or a common description that excludes God, and by turning the argument against the opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doctrine of Allah that you cannot fully grasp, do you demand a complete explanation before you submit, or does the mere possibility of a sound reason suffice to silence your doubt? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
You have just seen that our limited reasoning can be turned against the very arguments we use. Are you willing to let go of the comfort of complete intellectual certainty and rely on the sufficiency of possibility in matters of faith? What is holding you back?
?
The argument for the permissibility of seeing Allah uses the same logic as the response to the objection on power. If you accept one, you must accept the other. Are you consistent in your theological positions, or do you pick and choose based on comfort? Ask yourself honestly and commit to alignment with sound reasoning.

Response to the Second Objection on Divine Power
Razi answers the second objection on divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's rejection of the second objection through the concept of a unique particularity in God's power.

Core Idea
A unique particularity in God's power explains its exclusive ability to create bodies.

The second objection argues that if God's power differs from created powers, the difference is no greater than differences among created powers, so it cannot grant unique creative ability. Razi dismisses this as extremely weak, because it is possible that God's power possesses a unique particularity absent in all created powers. This lesson examines that response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The opponent claims that the difference between God's power and created powers is not greater than differences among created powers, so it cannot explain God's unique creative ability.
2
Razi's counter: possibility of unique particularity
Razi says the objection is extremely weak because it is possible that God's power has a unique particularity not found in any created power.
This particularity would render it capable of creating bodies.
3
The principle of possibility
Razi emphasizes that with the existence of this possibility, the opponent's argument becomes invalid.
Possibility suffices in dialectical refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبه التي تمسكتموا بها في نفي قدرة الله تعالى فنقول : أما الجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : فهو أنه لا نزاع في أن القدرة التي فيها الشاهد لا تصلح لخلق الأجسام ولا نزاع في أنه لا بد من تعليل هذا الحكم، لكن لم لا يجوز تعليل هذا الحكم بخصوصية ماهية كل واحد من تلك القُدَر؟ أقصى ما في الباب أنه يلزم تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة، إلا ان هذا عندكم غير ممتنع، وهذا هو الحق لما بينا أن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهية لا يمتنع اشتراكها في بعض اللوازم. سلمنا أنه لا بد من تعليل هذه الأحكام المتساوية بوصف واحد مشترك بينها ولا مشترك بينها إلا كونها قدرًا، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذه القدر مشتركة في وصف، لأجل ذلك الوصف يمتنع خلق الجسم بها إلا أن ذلك الوصف لا تندرج فيه القدرة القديمة، وتندرج فيه جميع هذه القدر الحادثة؟ أقصى ما في الباب أنا لا تعرف ذلك الوصف ولكن السائل يكفيه مجرد الاحتمال وأيضًا فهذا هو عين الدليل الذي يتمسك به أصحابنا في جواز الرؤية، فإن صح هذا الدليل لزمكم القطع بجواز الرؤية على الله تعالى، وأنتم لا تقولون به. والجواب عن شبهتهم الثانية : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: تلك القدرة القديمة مخالفة لهذه القدرة التي في الشاهد؟ قوله : "ليست مخالفة تلك القدرة لهذه القدرة أعظم من مخالفة بعضها لبعض" قلنا : هذا في غاية الركاكة، لاحتمال أن تكون تلك القدرة القديمة لها خصوصية، ولا توجد تلك الخصوصية في شيء من القدر الموجودة في الشاهد فلا جرم كانت تلك القدرة صالحة لخلق الأجسام ولم تكن غيرها صالحة لهذا المعنى، ومع قيام الاحتمال بطل ما ذكرتم .
As for the objections you have clung to in negating the power of Allah, we say: As for the answer to the first objection: It is that there is no dispute that the power in the witnessed realm is not fit for creating bodies, and there is no dispute that this ruling must be explained. But why is it not permissible to explain this ruling by the specificity of the essence of each one of those powers? The most that can be said is that it entails explaining equal rulings by different causes, but this is not impossible according to you, and this is the truth because we have shown that different things in essence are not prevented from sharing some properties. We concede that these equal rulings must be explained by a single description common between them, and there is nothing common between them except being power (qudra). So why is it not permissible to say: These powers share a description, due to which description the creation of a body is impossible with them, except that that description does not include the eternal power, but includes all these contingent powers? The most that can be said is that we do not know that description, but the questioner is satisfied by mere possibility. Also, this is exactly the argument that our companions use for the permissibility of vision. So if this argument is valid, it necessitates for you a definitive ruling on the permissibility of seeing Allah, yet you do not say that. And the answer to their second objection: Why is it not permissible to say: That eternal power is different from this power in the witnessed realm? His statement: 'The difference of that power from this power is not greater than the difference of some of them from each other' — we say: This is extremely weak, because it is possible that that eternal power has a particularity, and that particularity is not found in any of the powers existing in the witnessed realm. Consequently, that power is fit for creating bodies while others are not fit for this meaning. And with the existence of possibility, what you mentioned becomes invalid.

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's response to two objections concerning divine power. The first objection argues that since created powers (qudar) cannot create bodies, God's power, being similar in concept, also cannot create bodies. Razi answers by first noting that the ruling that witnessed powers cannot create bodies must have a cause. It could be the specific essence (māhiyya) of each power, even if different causes lead to the same ruling—this is not impossible. Alternatively, if one insists on a single common description (being power), Razi suggests that the commonality might include a specific restriction that applies only to contingent powers and not to God's eternal power. The second objection argues that if God's power were different from created powers, it would be no more different than created powers differ from each other, so the difference is insufficient to grant creative ability. Razi dismisses this as extremely weak (ghāyat al-rakāka), because it is possible that God's power has a unique particularity (khuṣūṣiyya) absent in all created powers, which explains its ability to create bodies. Per possibility, the objection fails.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra, power)
خصوصية (khuṣūṣiyya, particularity)
مخالفة (mukhālafa, difference)
احتمال (iḥtimāl, possibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi provides no proof that God's power actually has a particularity.
Correction: Razi only needs to show that it is possible, not actual. The opponent's objection is not decisive because possibility undermines their claim.

Checkpoint
In responding to the second objection, why does Razi consider the opponent's statement 'the difference is not greater' as extremely weak?
A) Because it is logically impossible for two powers to differ in essence.
B) Because it is possible that God's power has a unique particularity that other powers lack.
C) Because the opponent cannot prove the similarity of differences.
D) Because Razi has already proven that all powers are identical in essence.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the second objection by asserting that God's power may have a unique particularity, making the objection's claim of insufficient difference invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, what 'powers' do you rely on—your intellect, strength, wealth—that would be absolutely nothing without God's unique particularity? Are you living as if you truly depend on that eternal power, or have you placed your trust in created abilities that, without God's special quality, cannot create or sustain anything?
?
Razi shows that the mere possibility of God's unique power is enough to refute doubt. When you face personal doubts about God's attributes or His ability to intervene in your life, do you allow the logical possibility of His perfect power to settle your heart, or do you demand certainty on your own terms?
?
The opponent assumed divine power is just like human power, only greater. Have you unconsciously reduced God to a bigger version of yourself—expecting Him to think, forgive, or act as you would? Let this lesson push you to repent from anthropomorphizing the Creator and to embrace the true 'particularity' of His ways.

Response to the second objection about qudra
Razi answers the second objection regarding a shared attribute of qudra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the uniqueness of divine power (qudra) by appealing to the concept of specificity (khususiyya) and why the opponent's argument that a shared attribute implies identical rulings fails.

Core Idea
A shared attribute does not imply identical rulings; specificity can distinguish the eternal power from created powers.

In this passage, Razi responds to the second objection against his position on divine power (qudra). The opponent argues that if all powers share the attribute of being power, then any ruling that applies to some powers should apply to all. Razi refutes this by introducing the notion of a unique specificity (khususiyya) in the eternal power, which allows it to create bodies while created powers cannot. This lesson examines both the objection and Razi's powerful response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The opponent concedes a shared attribute but argues that if creating bodies is impossible for all powers except the eternal one, there must be an additional attribute in the eternal power. Note the opponent's reliance on the 'mere possibility' of an unknown attribute.
The opponent uses the principle that a shared attribute should lead to shared rulings.
2
Understand Razi's counterargument
Razi responds by saying it is permissible that the eternal power has a unique specificity not found in any created power, which allows it to create bodies. This defeats the opponent's claim that the powers are identical in all relevant respects.
Razi also points out that the opponent's argument would commit them to affirming the permissibility of seeing God.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Notice how Razi dismisses the objection that the difference between eternal and created power is no greater than differences among created powers. He calls this 'extremely weak' because the specificity can still be unique to the eternal power.
This shows the power of possibility (ihtimal) in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أنه لا بد من تعليل هذه الأحكام المتساوية بوصف واحد مشترك بينها ولا مشترك بينها إلا كونها قدرًا، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذه القدر مشتركة في وصف، لأجل ذلك الوصف يمتنع خلق الجسم بها إلا أن ذلك الوصف لا تندرج فيه القدرة القديمة، وتندرج فيه جميع هذه القدر الحادثة؟ أقصى ما في الباب أنا لا نعرف ذلك الوصف ولكن السائل يكفيه مجرد الاحتمال وأيضًا فهذا هو عين الدليل الذي يتمسك به أصحابنا في جواز الرؤية، فإن صح هذا الدليل لزمكم القطع بجواز الرؤية على الله تعالى، وأنتم لا تقولون به. والجواب عن شبهتهم الثانية : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: تلك القدرة القديمة مخالفة لهذه القدرة التي في الشاهد؟ قوله : "ليست مخالفة تلك القدرة لهذه القدرة أعظم من مخالفة بعضها لبعض" قلنا : هذا في غاية الركاكة، لاحتمال أن تكون تلك القدرة القديمة لها خصوصية، ولا توجد تلك الخصوصية في شيء من القدر الموجودة في الشاهد فلا جرم كانت تلك القدرة صالحة لخلق الأجسام ولم تكن غيرها صالحة لهذا المعنى، ومع قيام الاحتمال بطل ما ذكرتم.
We concede that it is necessary to explain these equal rulings by a single attribute common among them, and nothing is common among them except being power (qudra). So why is it not permissible to say: These powers share an attribute, and because of that attribute, creating bodies by them is impossible, except that that attribute does not encompass the eternal power, but encompasses all these originated powers? The most one can say is that we do not know that attribute, but the questioner is satisfied with mere possibility. Moreover, this is exactly the argument that our companions use regarding the permissibility of seeing (ru'ya), so if this argument is valid, you would have to affirm the permissibility of seeing God, but you do not say it. And the answer to their second doubt: Why is it not permissible to say that that eternal power is different from this power in the witnessed world? He said: 'The difference of that power from this power is not greater than the difference of some of these from others.' We say: This is extremely weak, because it is possible that that eternal power has a specificity, and that specificity does not exist in any of the powers present in the witnessed world. Therefore, that power is suitable for creating bodies, while others are not suitable for this meaning. And with the existence of possibility, what you mentioned is invalidated.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit from the fifteenth issue on power (qudra). The opponent concedes that the various powers (eternal and originated) share a common attribute, namely being power, and then argues that if creating bodies is impossible for all these powers except the eternal one, there must be an additional attribute in the eternal power that makes it unique. The opponent further notes that even if we do not know that attribute, the mere possibility suffices. The author (Razi) then presents the answer to this second doubt: it is permissible that the eternal power has a unique specificity not found in any created power, which allows it to create bodies. He rejects the objection that the difference between eternal and created power is no greater than differences among created powers, calling this 'extremely weak' because the specificity can still exist in the eternal power alone. Thus, the opponent's argument fails because the possibility of such specificity remains.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
وصف (sifah/attribute)
خصوصية (specificity)
جواز الرؤية (permissibility of seeing God)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A shared attribute implies identical nature in all respects, so any ruling applying to some powers must apply to all.
Correction: Razi shows that a shared attribute does not preclude unique specificities; the eternal power can have a special quality that allows creation, while created powers lack it.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's answer to the second objection, why does the opponent's argument fail?
A) Because the opponent admits they do not know the attribute.
B) Because the eternal power may have a unique specificity that created powers lack.
C) Because all powers are identical in essence.
D) Because the argument is taken from the issue of seeing (ru'ya).

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the eternal power's unique specificity allows it to create bodies, while created powers lack this specificity, thereby defeating the opponent's objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the uniqueness of Allah's power, do you ever treat His attributes as if they were merely greater versions of human attributes? How does Razi's concept of 'specificity' challenge that tendency? What specific step will you take to remind yourself of this distinction in your daily thoughts about Allah?
?
The opponent's argument seemed logical, but Razi pointed out a flaw. Are there areas in your own understanding of Allah where you accept logical arguments that might have hidden assumptions? What one assumption about Allah's attributes have you never questioned? Will you investigate it today?
?
Razi uses the possibility of an unknown attribute to defend the faith. In your life, are you comfortable with not knowing everything about Allah? When you face doubts, do you allow 'mere possibility' to strengthen your iman, or do you demand certainty? What will you do this week to embrace the mystery of Allah's attributes?

Seeing as Distinct from Knowledge: Razi's Introspective Proof
Razi defines ibsar and proves its distinctness from knowledge via introspection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses introspection to prove that seeing (ibsar) is a cognitive state distinct from knowledge ('ilm).

Core Idea
Seeing adds an extra dimension beyond mere knowledge of an object.

What makes seeing different from knowing? Imagine looking at a friend's face, then closing your eyes. You still know what they look like, but something is missing. Imam Razi uses this everyday experience to argue that seeing is not just knowledge but a distinct additional state. In this passage, he presents a simple yet powerful argument that will reshape how you think about sensory perception in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the introspective scenario
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Razi sets up the experiment: looking at Zayd's face, closing eyes, then opening again.
This is an empirical, first-person argument.
2
Identify the two states
Identify the two states: (1) when eyes are closed – you have clear knowledge of the image; (2) when eyes are open – you have an additional state. Razi calls that additional state 'seeing'.
The key is the difference between the two states is felt intuitively (badahah).
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that seeing is distinct from knowledge because it is an extra state that occurs only during active looking. This establishes ibsar as a separate attribute.
This argument is foundational for discussions about divine attributes, especially whether God 'sees' in a way that is not merely His knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الإبصار فنقول : إنا إذا نظرنا على وجه زيد نظرا بالاستقصاء ثم غمضنا العين فحالة التغميض نكون عالمين بتلك الصورة علما جليا خاليا عن الشبهة، وإذا فتحنا العين مرة أخرى ونظرنا إليه علمنا بالبداهة حصول تفرقة بين الحالتين، فهذه الحالة الزائدة الحاصلة عند النظر على ذلك المرئي مغايرة للعلم الذي كان حاصلا حال تغميض العين وهذا التغاير هو الإبصار، فثبت أن الإبصار أمر مغاير للعلم.
As for seeing (ibsar), we say: When we look carefully at Zayd's face and then close our eyes, in the state of closing we are knowing that image with clear knowledge free from doubt. And when we open the eye again and look at him, we know by intuition that there is a difference between the two states. So this additional state that occurs when looking at that visible object is distinct from the knowledge that was present during the closure of the eye, and this distinctness is seeing. Thus it is established that seeing is something distinct from knowledge.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and proof that seeing (ibsar) is distinct from knowledge ('ilm). The method is introspective: when one looks at an object (e.g., Zayd's face) carefully and then closes the eyes, one still has clear knowledge of the image without doubt. When the eyes are reopened, one intuitively perceives a difference between the two states. This difference is a state additional to the knowledge already present, and this additional state is what is called 'seeing.' Thus, seeing is not reducible to knowledge; it is a separate cognitive event that occurs only when the sense organ is actively receiving visual input.

Key Terms
الإبصار (ibsar/seeing)
العلم (knowledge)
التغاير (distinctness)
البداهة (intuition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that seeing is simply a type of knowledge or that it is identical to knowledge.
Correction: Razi shows that seeing involves an additional state beyond knowledge; knowledge can exist without seeing (e.g., when eyes are closed), so they are distinct.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim is the difference between the state with eyes closed and the state with eyes open?
A) The state with eyes open has more knowledge.
B) The state with eyes open has an additional state called seeing.
C) The state with eyes closed has less clear knowledge.
D) There is no difference; both are knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Seeing is not merely knowing; it is a distinct cognitive state that occurs when the eyes are actively perceiving.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you close your eyes and recall a scene you saw, do you ever feel a subtle lack? Razi says seeing adds something knowledge alone cannot give. What does that tell you about the importance of actually 'seeing' God's signs rather than just knowing about them? What one verse of the Quran can you try to truly 'see' today, not just read?
?
If seeing is distinct from knowledge, then knowing God is not the same as witnessing His greatness. In your prayer, have you ever settled for merely knowing you are before Him, without truly 'seeing' His presence? What would change if you prayed as if you could see Him?
?
Razi’s proof depends on your own experience. So ask yourself: have you ever experienced that extra dimension when seeing something you already knew about? If so, why do you often live as if knowing about Allah is enough? What specific step can you take today to move from knowledge to spiritual vision?

Philosophers' Three Proofs of Physical Impression in Vision
Philosophers present three proofs that the eye is physically affected by the object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three empirical arguments philosophers use to claim that the eye is physically affected by the object seen.

Core Idea
Afterimages, color mixing, and pupil recoil suggest the eye is passive.

In this passage, the philosophers challenge a purely intellectual account of vision. They propose that the eye undergoes a physical change when seeing. To support this, they offer three concrete examples from everyday experience: the persistence of an afterimage, the mixing of colors after staring at green, and the involuntary recoil of the pupil from bright light. These proofs aim to show that seeing is not just knowledge but involves a real impression on the sense organ.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the philosophers frame their question as a challenge to the earlier view.
The passage begins with a rhetorical question: 'Why is it not permissible to say…'
2
Identify each of the three proofs
The passage lists three distinct empirical examples. For each, identify the phenomenon and the conclusion the philosopher draws from it.
Look for the words 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider what these proofs imply about the nature of perception. Why do the philosophers think this supports that the eye is affected? How might a theologian respond?
These proofs set up a objection that the theologian must answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : التفاوت راجع إلى أن العين تتأثر من المحسوس حال النظر إليه؟ والذي يدل على حصول التأثر وجوه : الأول: أن من نظر إلى قرص الشمس نظرا شديدا بالاستقصاء ثم غمض عينيه فإنه يتخيل كأن قرص الشمس حاضر في خياله، ولو أراد أن يدفع ذلك الخيال عجز عنه، وهذا يدل على أن الحس قد يتأثر عن المحسوس. الثاني: أن من نظر إلى روضة خضراء نظرا بالاستقصاء الشديد زمانا له قدر ثم حول حدقته إلى شيء آخر أبيض اللون فإنه لا يراه أبيض اللون، بل يرى لونه ممزوجا من البياض والخضرة، وما ذاك إلا لأن أثر الخضرة بقي في حدقته، فلما حول الحدقة إلى الشيء الأبيض امتزجت الخضرة الباقية في حدقته بذلك البياض، فأحس بذلك اللون على وصف الامتزاج. الثالث: أن من نظر إلى المضيء القوي ارتدت حدقته الباصرة مقهورة، وهذا يدل على أن الحس يقبل الأثر من المحسوس.
The philosophers said: Why is it not permissible to say that the difference is due to the eye being affected by the sensed object while looking at it? And what indicates the occurrence of effect are several aspects: First: That one who looks intensely at the disk of the sun with careful scrutiny, then closes his eyes, imagines as if the disk of the sun is present in his imagination, and if he wanted to repel that imagination he would be unable to. This indicates that the sense may be affected by the sensed object. Second: That one who looks at a green garden with intense scrutiny for a considerable time, then turns his pupil to another thing that is white, does not see it as white, but sees its color mixed from whiteness and greenness. This is only because the effect of greenness remained in his pupil; when he turned the pupil to the white thing, the remaining greenness mixed with that whiteness, so he perceived that color in the state of mixture. Third: That one who looks at a bright strong light, his seeing pupil recoils overpowered. This indicates that the sense receives an effect from the sensed object.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers challenging the purely cognitive definition of seeing. They propose that the difference between perception states could be explained by the sense organ being physically affected by the object. They provide three proofs: (1) Afterimage: looking at the sun leaves an afterimage that cannot be voluntarily dismissed, showing the eye is affected. (2) Color mixing: after staring at green, turning to white yields a mixed perception, indicating green's effect persists in the eye. (3) Overpowering light: bright light causes the pupil to contract involuntarily, demonstrating a physical impression. These arguments aim to show that seeing involves a passive impression on the sense organ rather than simply an intellectual apprehension.

Key Terms
التأثر
الحس
المحسوس
الحدقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is one of the philosophers' proofs that the eye is affected by the object?
A) The pupil adjusts to light intensity.
B) After looking at the sun, an afterimage remains.
C) The eye can see without any physical change.
D) Knowledge of an object is immediate.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use three empirical examples—afterimage, color mixing, and pupil recoil—to argue that the eye is passively affected by the object seen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that the eye cannot dismiss the afterimage of the sun. Think of a sin you have committed that still lingers in your mind, like an afterimage. How long have you tried to dismiss it but failed? What will you do today to turn that reminder into repentance, not despair?
?
The philosophers argue that the eye is affected by what it sees. Your heart is also affected by what you look at. Is there a screen or scene you regularly expose yourself to that leaves a stain on your soul? What concrete step will you take this week to guard your gaze and protect your heart?
?
When bright light hits your eye, your pupil recoils involuntarily, showing your physical weakness. Do you submit to Allah's commands as involuntarily as your eye submits to light? Or do you resist? Which command of Allah are you most struggling to surrender to, and what will you do to make that obedience as natural as your pupil's recoil?

Sight is Distinct from Knowledge: Razi's Proof
Razi defines sight as distinct from knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's introspective argument that sight is an additional cognitive state beyond mere knowledge.

Core Idea
Seeing is not merely knowing; it adds a unique state when the eyes are open.

Imam Razi begins by establishing what sight truly is. He contrasts the state of knowing an object with eyes closed to the state of seeing it with eyes open, concluding that sight adds something extra to knowledge. This foundational definition is crucial for the subsequent debate about whether God can be described as seeing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the two states Razi compares: knowing with eyes closed and seeing with eyes open.
The passage uses a simple introspection anyone can perform.
2
Identify the two states
Razi says when you close your eyes after looking, you have clear knowledge. When you open them again, an additional state appears. That extra state is sight.
3
Understand the importance
Why does Razi need to prove sight is separate from knowledge? Because if it were the same, the debate about God's sight would be trivial. This distinction sets up the challenge from the philosophers.
This connects to the lesson's objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة عشرة في كونه تعالى سميعًا بصيرًا وهي مرتبة على فصلين : الفصل الأول : في شرح حقيقة الإبصار والإسماع أما الإبصار فنقول : إنا إذا نظرنا على وجه زيد نظرا بالاستقصاء ثم غمضنا العين فحالة التغميض نكون عالمين بتلك الصورة علما جليًا خاليًا عن الشك والشبهة، وإذا فتحنا العين مرة أخرى ونظرنا إليه علمنا بالبداهة حصول تفرقة بين الحالتين، فهذه الحالة الزائدة الحاصلة عند النظر على ذلك المرئي مغايرة للعلم الذي كان حاصلا حال تغميض العين وهذا التغاير هو الإبصار، فثبت أن الإبصار أمر مغاير للعلم. قالت الفلاسفة : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : التفاوت راجع إلى أن العين تتأثر من المحسوس حال النظر إليه؟ والذي يدل على حصول التأثر وجوه : الأول: أن من نظر إلى قرص الشمس نظرًا شديدًا بالاستقصاء ثم غمض عينيه فإنه يتخيل كأن قرص الشمس حاضر في خياله، ولو أراد أن يدفع ذلك الخيال عجز عنه، وهذا يدل على أن الحس قد يتأثر عن المحسوس. الثاني: أن من نظر إلى روضة خضراء نظرًا بالاستقصاء الشديد زمانًا له قدر ثم حول حدقته إلى شيء آخر أبيض اللون فإنه لا يراه أبيض اللون، بل يرى لونه ممزوجًا من البياض والخضرة، وما ذاك إلا لأن أثر الخضرة بقي في حدقته، فلما حول الحدقة إلى الشيء الأبيض امتزجت الخضرة الباقية في حدقته بذلك البياض، فأحس بذلك اللون على وصف الامتزاج. الثالث: أن من نظر إلى المضيء القوي ارتدت حدقته الباصرة مقهورة، وهذا يدل على أن الحس يقبل الأثر من المحسوس. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : التفاوت الحاصر بين ما إذا نظرنا إلى الشيء وبين ما إذا غمضت العين هو كون الحس متأثرًا عن المحسوس، وعلى هذا التقدير وجب أن يمتنع الإبصار على الله تعالى لأن الإبصار لما كان عبارة عن هذا التأثر، وهذا التأثر من صفات الأجسام والله تعالى ليس بجـ[؟] جب أن يكون الإبصار ممتنعا على الله تعالى.
The sixteenth issue: Concerning His being Hearing and Seeing. It is arranged in two sections: The first section: On explaining the reality of sight and hearing. As for sight, we say: If we look at Zayd's face with thorough looking, then close our eyes, then in the state of closure we are knowing that image with clear knowledge free from doubt and uncertainty. And if we open the eye again and look at him, we know by intuition that a distinction occurs between the two states. So this additional state that occurs upon looking at that visible object is different from the knowledge that existed during the state of eye closure, and this difference is sight. Thus it is established that sight is something distinct from knowledge. The philosophers said: Why may it not be said that the difference is due to the eye being affected by the sensed object while looking at it? That which indicates the occurrence of affect are points: First: That one who looks at the solar disk with intense and thorough looking, then closes his eyes, he imagines as if the solar disk is present in his imagination, and if he wanted to repel that imagination he would be unable. This indicates that the sense may be affected by the sensed. Second: That one who looks at a green meadow with intense thorough looking for a considerable time, then turns his pupil to another white object, he does not see it white, but sees its color mixed with whiteness and greenness. That is only because the effect of greenness remained in his pupil, so when he turned the pupil to the white object, the remaining greenness in his pupil mixed with that whiteness, and he sensed that color in a mixed state. Third: That one who looks at a strong light, his seeing pupil recoils overpowered. This indicates that the sense receives effect from the sensed. If this is established, we say: Why may it not be said that the encompassing difference between when we look at the thing and when we close the eye is that the sense is affected by the sensed? And on this assumption, it necessarily follows that sight is impossible for God, because if sight is an expression of this affect, and this affect is an attribute of bodies, and God is not a body, then sight must be impossible for God.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage defines sight (ibṣār) and presents the first objection against affirming sight for God. Razi begins with an introspective argument: when we look at an object, then close our eyes, we still have knowledge of it, but when we open our eyes again, an additional state occurs that we recognize as distinct from mere knowledge. That additional state is sight, which is therefore distinct from knowledge (al-ʿilm). The philosophers then object: the difference between seeing and not seeing could be merely the eye being affected (ta'aththur) by the sensed object. They give three proofs for the reality of such affection: 1) Afterimage from the sun, 2) Afterimage of greenness mixing with white, 3) The eye being overpowered by strong light. They conclude that if sight is this affection, and affection is a property of bodies, then sight cannot be attributed to God, who is not a body, rendering divine seeing impossible.

Key Terms
الإبصار
العلم
التغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sight is identical to knowledge; knowing something is the same as seeing it.
Correction: Razi shows that we know an object even with eyes closed, but seeing adds a new state. Thus sight is not mere knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does Razi identify as the 'additional state' that occurs when we open our eyes after looking at an object?
A) A clearer form of knowledge
B) Sight (al-ibṣār)
C) Imagination (al-takhayyul)
D) Physical affection

Key Takeaway
Sight is a distinct cognitive state from knowledge, proven by the introspective difference between knowing with closed eyes and seeing with open eyes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi asks you to perform an experiment: know with your eyes closed, then see with them open. In your life, are you mostly living with your eyes closed – relying on secondhand knowledge of God, of yourself, of others? What would it take for you to truly open your eyes and seek that additional state? What is one relationship or truth you have been avoiding looking at directly because knowing about it feels safer? Commit to one concrete step today to move from knowledge to vision.
?
Think of a time when you assumed you 'knew' something or someone because you had information, but then when you actually 'saw' the reality, you realized you had missed something crucial. Razi says that extra something is real and distinct. Is there an area of your faith or your character where you are content with mere knowledge, afraid of the raw encounter that sight would bring? What are you protecting by keeping your eyes closed? Ask Allah for the courage to open them.
?
The extra state Razi describes is only present when the eyes are open. In your relationship with Allah, have you settled for knowing about His attributes without experiencing them through direct contemplation? How can you intentionally create moments of 'opening your eyes' - perhaps through reflection on creation or through heartfelt prayer - to gain that additional state of spiritual sight? Write down one practical change you will make this week.

The Philosophers' Objection: Sight as Physical Affection
Philosophers argue sight is physical affection, denying divine sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that sight is a physical affection of the eye, making it impossible for God to see.

Core Idea
If sight is just the eye being affected, then God cannot see because He is not a body.

After Razi defines sight as distinct from knowledge, the philosophers launch a counterargument. They propose that the difference between seeing and not seeing is simply the eye being physically affected (ta'aththur) by the object. They support this with three empirical proofs from afterimages and eye reactions. They conclude that if sight is bodily affect, it cannot apply to the incorporeal God.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
Read the passage where the philosophers present their alternative explanation for the difference between seeing and not seeing. They claim it is due to physical affection.
Notice they do not deny the difference; they reinterpret it.
2
Examine the first proof
The first proof: after staring at the sun, you see an afterimage even with eyes closed. This shows the eye retains an effect from the object.
3
Examine the second and third proofs
Second: staring at green and then looking at white makes white appear greenish, because the green effect lingers. Third: strong light makes the eye recoil, proving the sense is affected.
These are all empirical observations.
4
Understand the conclusion
The philosophers conclude: if sight is this affection, and affection is a bodily property, then God (not being a body) cannot have sight. This challenges the traditional Islamic belief that God sees.
Razi will later respond to this objection in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة عشرة في كونه تعالى سميعًا بصيرًا وهي مرتبة على فصلين : الفصل الأول : في شرح حقيقة الإبصار والإسماع أما الإبصار فنقول : إنا إذا نظرنا على وجه زيد نظرا بالاستقصاء ثم غمضنا العين فحالة التغميض نكون عالمين بتلك الصورة علما جليًا خاليًا عن الشك والشبهة، وإذا فتحنا العين مرة أخرى ونظرنا إليه علمنا بالبداهة حصول تفرقة بين الحالتين، فهذه الحالة الزائدة الحاصلة عند النظر على ذلك المرئي مغايرة للعلم الذي كان حاصلا حال تغميض العين وهذا التغاير هو الإبصار، فثبت أن الإبصار أمر مغاير للعلم. قالت الفلاسفة : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : التفاوت راجع إلى أن العين تتأثر من المحسوس حال النظر إليه؟ والذي يدل على حصول التأثر وجوه : الأول: أن من نظر إلى قرص الشمس نظرًا شديدًا بالاستقصاء ثم غمض عينيه فإنه يتخيل كأن قرص الشمس حاضر في خياله، ولو أراد أن يدفع ذلك الخيال عجز عنه، وهذا يدل على أن الحس قد يتأثر عن المحسوس. الثاني: أن من نظر إلى روضة خضراء نظرًا بالاستقصاء الشديد زمانًا له قدر ثم حول حدقته إلى شيء آخر أبيض اللون فإنه لا يراه أبيض اللون، بل يرى لونه ممزوجًا من البياض والخضرة، وما ذاك إلا لأن أثر الخضرة بقي في حدقته، فلما حول الحدقة إلى الشيء الأبيض امتزجت الخضرة الباقية في حدقته بذلك البياض، فأحس بذلك اللون على وصف الامتزاج. الثالث: أن من نظر إلى المضيء القوي ارتدت حدقته الباصرة مقهورة، وهذا يدل على أن الحس يقبل الأثر من المحسوس. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : التفاوت الحاصر بين ما إذا نظرنا إلى الشيء وبين ما إذا غمضت العين هو كون الحس متأثرًا عن المحسوس، وعلى هذا التقدير وجب أن يمتنع الإبصار على الله تعالى لأن الإبصار لما كان عبارة عن هذا التأثر، وهذا التأثر من صفات الأجسام والله تعالى ليس بجـ[؟] جب أن يكون الإبصار ممتنعا على الله تعالى.
The sixteenth issue: Concerning His being Hearing and Seeing. It is arranged in two sections: The first section: On explaining the reality of sight and hearing. As for sight, we say: If we look at Zayd's face with thorough looking, then close our eyes, then in the state of closure we are knowing that image with clear knowledge free from doubt and uncertainty. And if we open the eye again and look at him, we know by intuition that a distinction occurs between the two states. So this additional state that occurs upon looking at that visible object is different from the knowledge that existed during the state of eye closure, and this difference is sight. Thus it is established that sight is something distinct from knowledge. The philosophers said: Why may it not be said that the difference is due to the eye being affected by the sensed object while looking at it? That which indicates the occurrence of affect are points: First: That one who looks at the solar disk with intense and thorough looking, then closes his eyes, he imagines as if the solar disk is present in his imagination, and if he wanted to repel that imagination he would be unable. This indicates that the sense may be affected by the sensed. Second: That one who looks at a green meadow with intense thorough looking for a considerable time, then turns his pupil to another white object, he does not see it white, but sees its color mixed with whiteness and greenness. That is only because the effect of greenness remained in his pupil, so when he turned the pupil to the white object, the remaining greenness in his pupil mixed with that whiteness, and he sensed that color in a mixed state. Third: That one who looks at a strong light, his seeing pupil recoils overpowered. This indicates that the sense receives effect from the sensed. If this is established, we say: Why may it not be said that the encompassing difference between when we look at the thing and when we close the eye is that the sense is affected by the sensed? And on this assumption, it necessarily follows that sight is impossible for God, because if sight is an expression of this affect, and this affect is an attribute of bodies, and God is not a body, then sight must be impossible for God.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage defines sight (ibṣār) and presents the first objection against affirming sight for God. Razi begins with an introspective argument: when we look at an object, then close our eyes, we still have knowledge of it, but when we open our eyes again, an additional state occurs that we recognize as distinct from mere knowledge. That additional state is sight, which is therefore distinct from knowledge (al-ʿilm). The philosophers then object: the difference between seeing and not seeing could be merely the eye being affected (ta'aththur) by the sensed object. They give three proofs for the reality of such affection: 1) Afterimage from the sun, 2) Afterimage of greenness mixing with white, 3) The eye being overpowered by strong light. They conclude that if sight is this affection, and affection is a property of bodies, then sight cannot be attributed to God, who is not a body, rendering divine seeing impossible.

Key Terms
التأثر
الفلاسفة
المحسوس
الحدقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sight is a purely spiritual or intellectual act with no physical component.
Correction: The philosophers provide empirical evidence that sight involves physical affection of the eye, such as afterimages and recoil.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' main conclusion about divine sight?
A) God sees through a special non-physical sight
B) Sight is impossible for God because it is a bodily affection
C) Sight is identical to God's knowledge
D) The philosophers affirm that God sees

Key Takeaway
The philosophers reduce sight to physical affection, thereby excluding God from having sight, since He is not a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's sight, do you unconsciously picture it like human eyesight? How does the philosophers' attempt to reduce sight to a physical process force you to purify your concept of God's attributes? What concrete step can you take today to worship God as beyond your physical analogies?
?
You have read three ordinary experiences (afterimage, color mixing, eye recoil) used to make a claim about God. Are there everyday assumptions you hold that might be quietly shaping your beliefs about God in ways you haven't examined? Will you honestly search your heart for such assumptions today?
?
The philosophers argued that if sight is physical, God cannot see. Suppose a friend used a similar argument to question God's power or knowledge. How would your understanding of this lesson equip you to respond? Are you prepared to defend God's attributes not just with words but with a heart that truly believes He is unlike His creation?

The Mutakallimun's Defense of Divine Sight
The mutakallimun refute the physicalist reduction and argue from scripture and burden of proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's combined rational and scriptural argument that God's sight is a distinct attribute not dependent on physical eyes.

Core Idea
Sight is more than a physical effect; it can be known through scripture.

The philosophers claimed that sight requires a physical apparatus, making it impossible for God. The mutakallimun respond with a clever two-step defense: first, they use rational reflection to show that sight is not merely a physical effect; then, they appeal to scripture to affirm God's sight, placing the burden of proof on those who would condition it on physical processes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure: the mutakallimun argue, the philosophers counter, and the mutakallimun respond again.
This passage is a dialectical exchange, not a simple list.
2
Identify the first argument
The mutakallimun begin by showing that sight cannot be a mere physical effect. Find the key observation about the eye seeing the whole hemisphere at once.
This uses a rational argument from direct experience.
3
Understand the philosophers' objection
The philosophers concede that sight is distinct from the physical effect, but they argue it might still require that effect as a precondition. See how they introduce the concept of 'condition' (sharṭ).
4
See the final response
The mutakallimun shift to scripture: since sight is distinct, they rely on apparent meanings of texts that affirm God's sight. They place the burden on the opponent to prove the conditioning.
This shows the interplay of reason and revelation in kalām.
5
Reflect on the burden of proof
Notice that the mutakallimun do not need to disprove the condition; they only need to show it is not proven. The one who claims the condition must prove it.
This is a key logical move: default is to accept scripture's apparent meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب المتكلمون عنه : بأن الإبصار ليس معناه هذا التأثر فقط، وذلك لأنا إذا فتحنا العين رأينا نصف كرة العالم دفعة واحدة وحصول هذه الصورة العظيمة في الجسم الصغير محال ومن المعلوم أن موضع التأثر ليس إلا نقطة الناظر، فعلمنا أن الإيصار حالة مغايرة للعلم، ومغايرة أيضا لتأثير الحس. قالت الفلاسفة هب أن الإبصار حالة مغايرة لهذا التأثير، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذه الحالة مشروطة بحصول هذا التأثر، ولما كان الشرط ممتنع التحقق في حق الله تعالى كان المشروط أيضًا كذلك ؟ أجاب المتكلمون بأنه لما ثبت أن الإبصار حالة مغايرة لهذا التأثر فنحن إنما نعول في إثبات هذه الحالة لله تعالى على الدلائل السمعية فإن ظواهرها دالة على كونه بصيرا، ونحن متمسكون بالظواهر، إلا إذا أقام الخصم دليلا على أن الإبصار مشروط بهذا التأثر، ولما كان هذا الشرط محالا في حق الله تعالى كان المشروط أيضًا محالا ، لكن هذا إشارة إلى المعرضة ومن ادعاها فعليه البيان. فهذا هو الكلام في الإبصار
The mutakallimun answered him: That sight is not merely this affect, because when we open the eye we see half the celestial sphere at once, and the occurrence of this great image in the small body is impossible, and it is known that the locus of affect is nothing but the point of the pupil. So we know that sight is a state distinct from knowledge and also distinct from the sense's effect. The philosophers said: Grant that sight is a state distinct from this effect, but why may it not be said that this state is conditioned upon the occurrence of this affect? And since the condition is impossible to realize in the case of God, the conditioned would also be so. The mutakallimun answered: Since it is established that sight is a state distinct from this affect, we rely in affirming this state for God on scriptural evidences, for their apparent meanings indicate His being seeing. And we adhere to apparent meanings unless the opponent produces evidence that sight is conditioned by this affect. And since this condition is impossible with respect to God, the conditioned would also be impossible, but this refers to a claim; and whoever claims it must provide the proof. This is the discussion concerning sight.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage contains the mutakallimun's response to the philosophers' objection. First, they argue that sight cannot be merely a physical effect because when we open our eyes we can see a vast scene (half the celestial sphere) at once, but the physical effect only occurs at a tiny point on the pupil. Therefore, sight is a distinct state beyond both knowledge and sense-affect. The philosophers then counter: even if sight is distinct, it might still require the physical affect as a precondition. Since that precondition is impossible for God, divine sight would also be impossible. The mutakallimun answer: since we have already proven that sight is distinct from the affect, we base God's sight on scriptural evidences (dalā'il samʿiyya). Those texts apparently affirm God's sight. We uphold those appearances unless the opponent brings a proof that sight is conditioned by the affect. The burden of proof lies on the one who claims the precondition. Thus, the scriptural evidence stands until disproven.

Key Terms
المتكلمون
المغايرة
الدلائل السمعية
الظواهر
الشرط
المشروط
المعرضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun reject reason entirely and only use scripture.
Correction: They first use a rational argument to show sight is not merely physical, then appeal to scripture. Reason and revelation work together.

Checkpoint
What is the main rational argument the mutakallimun use to show that sight is not merely a physical effect?
A) The eye can only see small objects
B) The physical effect is limited to a point, but we see a large scene at once
C) The philosophers have no evidence
D) Sight is identical to knowledge

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun defend divine sight by first proving its non-physical nature through reason, then relying on scripture's apparent meaning unless the opponent proves a necessary precondition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun hold to the apparent meaning of scripture unless the opponent proves otherwise. When do you allow doubt to replace the clear words of Allah in your own heart? Are you quicker to accept doubts about Allah's attributes than to trust what He has plainly revealed?
?
The philosophers argued that divine sight is impossible because it seems to require a physical condition. Reflect: Have you ever limited Allah based on your own experience or reasoning? When have you forgotten that Allah is not bound by the conditions of His creation?
?
The mutakallimun concluded that the burden of proof is on the one who makes the claim of conditioning. In your own life, do you often place the burden of proof on Allah's revelation, demanding that it meet your standards before you accept it? Or do you let His words stand until you are convinced otherwise?

Refuting the Physicalist Account of Hearing
Razi presents philosophers' account and mutakallimun's refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument against the philosophers' wave theory of hearing and why directional perception is a critical objection.

Core Idea
Hearing cannot be just a physical wave because we perceive direction.

Imam Razi presents the philosophers' naturalistic explanation of hearing as a physical process involving air waves reaching the ear canal. The mutakallimun then refute this by pointing out a key flaw: if hearing were merely sensing the impact on the ear, we could not distinguish sound direction. This lesson examines that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the philosophers' claim and the mutakallimun's response.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core argument is complete.
2
Identify the philosophers' account
Note that they explain hearing as a chain: percussion/rupture → air wave → ear canal impact → sensation.
3
Grasp the mutakallimun's objection
The key flaw: if hearing only detects the wave at the ear canal, we could not know from which direction the sound came. Since we do, the model is incomplete.
4
Consider the analogy with touch and taste
The text hints that touch and taste also cannot perceive direction, reinforcing the special nature of hearing.
This part is cut off but the idea is clear.
5
Reflect on the theological stakes
This debate matters for divine attributes: if hearing is a physical process, then God's hearing would be corporeal, which is impossible for the mutakallimun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام في السماع فقد قالت الفلاسفة : إنه إذا حصل قرع أو قلع حصل بسببها تموج في الهواء، وذلك التموج سبب لحدوث الصوت، وإذا وصل ذلك التموج إلى سطح الصماخ أحست القوة السامعة بذلك الأثر، وذلك الإحساس هو السماع، فالمسموع في الحقيقة هو ذلك الأثر الواصل على الصماخ. قال المتكلمون هذا الكلام باطل ؛ لأن القوة السامعة لو كانت لا تسمع إلا ما يصل إلى سطح الصماخ لما كان الإنسان بقوته السامعة يدرك أنه سمع هذا الصوت هذا الجانب أو من ذلك الجانب ؛ لأنه إذا كان لا يحس إلا بما وصل إليه وجب بتلك الجوانب التي وصل منها ذلك الأثر، كما أن القوة اللامسة والذائقة من أن لا
And as for the discussion of hearing, the philosophers said: When a percussion or rupture occurs, there occurs due to it a wave in the air, and that wave is the cause of the occurrence of sound. And when that wave reaches the surface of the ear canal, the hearing power senses that effect, and that sensation is hearing. So the heard in reality is that effect reaching the ear canal. The mutakallimun said: This statement is invalid, because if the hearing power only hears what reaches the surface of the ear canal, then a person would not, by his hearing power, perceive that he heard this sound from this side or from that side; because if he only senses what reaches him, then [he would not know] those sides from which that effect reached, just as the touching and tasting power [cannot perceive direction] ...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage moves to the discussion of hearing (samāʿ). The philosophers give a physical account: sound is produced by a wave in the air reaching the ear canal, and hearing is the sensation of that effect. The mutakallimun refute this by pointing out that if hearing only perceived the immediate local effect at the ear canal, a person could not know the direction from which the sound came. Since we do perceive direction, hearing cannot be reduced to a mere physical contact. The argument is cut off, but the intended parallel is that the sense of touch and taste similarly cannot perceive direction, so hearing must be a distinct faculty that can locate sounds, which cannot be explained by the physical wave model alone.

الصوت
التموج

القلع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' wave theory of hearing is obviously correct and fully explains hearing.
Correction: The mutakallimun argue that the wave theory cannot account for our ability to perceive the direction of sound, so hearing must involve more than just physical contact.

Checkpoint

A) Sound waves do not exist

C) The ear canal is too small to detect waves
D) The philosophers contradict scripture

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun refute the physicalist account of hearing by showing that directional perception cannot be explained by mere contact with the ear, thus preserving a faculty-based understanding of divine attributes.

The Mutakallimun's Defense of God's Vision
Mutakallimun refute the philosophical objection that vision requires physical affectation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that vision requires physical affectation and the mutakallimun's rational and scriptural defense of God's vision.

Core Idea
Vision is not merely sensory affectation; it is a state distinct from knowledge and physical impression.

In this passage, the mutakallimun confront a key philosophical objection: if vision is defined as sensory affectation, then God, being non-physical, cannot see. They counter by redefining vision as a distinct state (hala) and then rely on scriptural evidence to affirm God's seeing. This lesson walks through their layered reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the philosophical argument that vision requires affectation and why it would be impossible for God.
The objection uses the definition of vision as sensory impression.
2
Analyze the first reply
The mutakallimun argue that vision is not merely affectation because we see large images at once in a small eye; it is a distinct state.
This refutes the reduction of vision to physical impression.
3
Consider the counter-objection
The philosophers concede vision is distinct but claim it may still be conditioned by affectation, making it impossible for God.
They introduce a conditional link.
4
Examine the final answer
The mutakallimun appeal to scriptural evidence and shift the burden of proof to the opponent to prove the conditioning.
They hold to apparent meanings unless a proof is given.
5
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how the mutakallimun use both rational and revealed arguments to defend divine attributes.
This shows their balanced approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : التفاوت الحاصر بين ما إذا نظرنا إلى الشيء وبين ما إذا غمضت العين هو كون الحس متأثرًا عن المحسوس، وعلى هذا التقدير وجب أن يمتنع الإبصار على الله تعالى لأن الإبصار لما كان عبارة عن هذا التأثر، وهذا التأثر من صفات الأجسام والله تعالى ليس بجسم، وجب أن يكون الإبصار ممتنعا على الله تعالى. أجاب المتكلمون عنه : بأن الإبصار ليس معناه هذا التأثر فقط، وذلك لأنا إذا فتحنا العين رأينا نصف كرة العالم دفعة واحدة وحصول هذه الصورة العظيمة في الجسم الصغير محال ومن المعلوم أن موضع التأثر ليس إلا نقطة الناظر، فعلمنا أن الإبصار حالة مغايرة للعلم، ومغايرة أيضا لتأثير الحس. قالت الفلاسفة هب أن الإبصار حالة مغايرة لهذا التأثير، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذه الحالة مشروطة بحصول هذا التأثر، ولما كان الشرط ممتنع التحقق في حق الله تعالى كان المشروط أيضًا كذلك ؟ أجاب المتكلمون بأنه لما ثبت أن الإبصار حالة مغايرة لهذا التأثر فنحن إنما نعول في إثبات هذه الحالة لله تعالى على الدلائل السمعية فإن ظواهرها دالة على كونه بصيرا، ونحن متمسكون بالظواهر، إلا إذا أقام الخصم دليلا على أن الإبصار مشروط بهذا التأثر، ولما كان هذا الشرط محالا في حق الله تعالى كان المشروط أيضًا محالا ، لكن هذا إشارة إلى المعرضة ومن ادعاها فعليه البيان. فهذا هو الكلام في الإبصار
If this is established, we say: why should it not be allowed to say: the exclusive difference between when we look at a thing and when we close the eye is that the sense is affected by the sensible. On this assumption, it necessarily follows that vision is impossible for God, because vision is an expression of this affectation, and this affectation is a property of bodies, and God is not a body, so vision must be impossible for God. The mutakallimun answered him: that vision does not mean only this affectation, because when we open the eye we see half the sphere of the world at once, and the occurrence of this huge image in a small body is absurd, and it is known that the place of affectation is only the point of the pupil, so we know that vision is a state (hala) distinct from knowledge and also distinct from sensory affectation. The philosophers said: suppose that vision is a state distinct from this affectation, but why should it not be allowed that this state is conditioned by the occurrence of this affectation? And since the condition is impossible of realization with respect to God, the conditioned is likewise impossible. The mutakallimun answered: since it is established that vision is a state distinct from this affectation, we only rely in proving this state for God on scriptural evidences (dalail sam'iyya), for their apparent meanings indicate that He is seeing (basir). We hold to the apparent meanings unless the opponent brings a proof that vision is conditioned by this affectation. And since this condition is impossible with respect to God, the conditioned is also impossible, but this is a reference to opposition (mu'arada), and whoever claims it must provide the proof. So this is the discussion on vision.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence on whether God can be described with vision (ibsar). It begins with a hypothetical objection: the only difference between seeing and not seeing is sensory affectation, so vision requires a body, making it impossible for God. The mutakallimun reply that vision is not merely affectation, because we see a huge image at once in a small eye; rather, vision is a distinct state (hala) that is neither knowledge nor sensory impression. The philosophers counter that this state may still be conditioned by affectation, but the mutakallimun appeal to revelation: God's sight is established by scriptural texts, and we accept them unless a proof of a condition is brought. The burden of proof lies on the one claiming the condition. The passage concludes by summarizing that this is the discussion on vision.

Key Terms
الإبصار
التأثر
الحس
المحسوس
الحالة
الدلائل السمعية
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun deny the physical aspect of vision entirely.
Correction: They argue that vision is not reducible to physical impression, but they do not deny that sensory affectation occurs; they say vision is more than that.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun in this passage, why is vision not merely sensory affectation?
A) Because the eye is too small to receive a large image, showing vision is a distinct state.
B) Because God cannot be affected.
C) Because the philosophers denied it.
D) Because scriptural evidence says so.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun defend God's vision by distinguishing it from physical affectation and relying on scriptural evidence, placing the burden of proof on those who claim a condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a philosophical objection to your faith, do you instinctively try to disprove it with your own reasoning, or do you trust the apparent meaning of revelation and demand proof from the objector? Which approach does this lesson teach, and what does that reveal about your reliance on God's word versus your intellect?
?

How does this lesson's distinction between human and divine vision challenge you to rethink the way you imagine God? What steps can you take to ensure your concept of God is shaped by revelation rather than by analogy with creation?
?
The mutakallimun shifted the burden of proof to the philosopher. In your own life, when someone challenges your beliefs, do you feel pressured to defend them immediately, or do you calmly ask them to provide their proof? How can you adopt the confidence of the mutakallimun, who rely on the apparent meanings of scripture unless a clear proof is given?

Refuting the Causal Theory of Hearing
Mutakallimun refute the philosophers' causal explanation of hearing by invoking direction perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument that hearing perceives sound at its source, not merely at the eardrum, based on the phenomenon of direction perception.

Core Idea
Hearing perceives direction, so it cannot be mere contact with the eardrum.

The philosophers proposed a purely causal theory of hearing: sound waves strike the eardrum and that sensation is hearing. The mutakallimun challenge this by pointing out that we can tell where a sound comes from, which would be impossible if hearing were only a local contact sense. This lesson unpacks that refutation and its implications for understanding sensory perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the two contrasting viewpoints: philosophers and mutakallimun.
The passage is dense but clear.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the philosophers' causal chain (striking/pulling → air wave → eardrum impact → hearing) and the mutakallimun's objection.
3
Understand the direction perception argument
The mutakallimun argue that if hearing were only contact with the eardrum, we could not perceive direction. Analyze this analogy with touch and taste.
Touch and taste also sense only what reaches them, but they don't detect direction.
4
Draw the conclusion
Conclude that hearing must perceive sounds at their source; this refutes the reductionist explanation.
This argument supports the mutakallimun's view that hearing is a distinct faculty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام في السماع، فقد قالت الفلاسفة : إنه إذا حصل قرع أو قلع حصل بسببها تموج في الهواء، وذلك التموج سبب لحدوث الصوت، وإذا وصل ذلك التموج إلى سطح الصماخ أحست القوة السامعة بذلك الأثر، وذلك الإحساس هو السماع، فالمسموع في الحقيقة هو ذلك الأثر الواصل على الصماخ. قال المتكلمون هذا الكلام باطل ؛ لأن القوة السامعة لو كانت لا تسمع إلا ما يصل إلى سطح الصماخ لما كان الإنسان بقوته السامعة يدرك أنه سمع هذا الصوت من هذا الجانب أو من ذلك الجانب ؛ لأنه إذا كان لا يحس إلا بما وصل إليه وجب بتلك الجوانب التي وصل منها ذلك الأثر، كما أن القوة اللامسة والذائقة لما كانتا لا تدركان إلا ما يصل إليهما لا جرم لا تدركان الجهة التي وصل منها المحسوس إليهما، ولما كانت القوة السامعة تميز بين جهة وجهة علمنا أنها تدرك الأصوات حيث وجدت الأصوات. فهذا هو الكلام الملخص في حقيقة السمع والبصر.
As for the discussion on hearing, the philosophers said: if there occurs a striking or a pulling, there occurs due to them a wave in the air, and that wave is a cause for the occurrence of sound, and when that wave reaches the surface of the eardrum, the hearing power senses that effect, and that sensation is hearing; so what is heard in reality is that effect reaching the eardrum. The mutakallimun said: this statement is false, because if the hearing power only heard what reaches the surface of the eardrum, then a man with his hearing power would not perceive that he heard this sound from this direction or that direction; because if it only senses what reaches it, it would be necessary with respect to the directions from which that effect reached, as the power of touch and taste, since they only perceive what reaches them, therefore they do not perceive the direction from which the sensible reached them. And since the hearing power distinguishes between one direction and another, we know that it perceives sounds where sounds exist. So this is the summary discussion on the reality of hearing and sight.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by the mutakallimun of the philosophers' causal theory of hearing. The philosophers define hearing as the sensation of an air wave impact on the eardrum. The mutakallimun counter that if hearing were merely contact with the eardrum, then we would not be able to perceive the direction of sound, just as touch and taste cannot perceive direction. Since we do perceive direction, hearing must perceive sounds at their source, not just at the eardrum. This shows that hearing is a distinct faculty.

Key Terms
السماع
القرع
القلع
التموج
الصوت
الصماخ
القوة السامعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun deny any physical process in hearing.
Correction: They do not deny physical processes; they deny that hearing is reducible to physical impact on the eardrum. They accept sound waves but argue that hearing perceives the sound at its source, not just at the ear.

Checkpoint
What is the mutakallimun's main objection to the philosophers' theory of hearing?
A) That sound does not travel through air.
B) That hearing cannot be localized to the eardrum because direction is perceived.
C) That God does not have hearing.
D) That the eardrum is not affected.

Key Takeaway
Hearing perceives direction, so it cannot be reduced to mere physical contact; rather, it perceives sounds at their origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Take a moment to thank Allah for this ability.

If your hearing can distinguish direction, does that not hint at a reality where God's attributes are not bound by physical chains? Can you think of an aspect of your faith that you have reduced to mere material processes? Resolve to correct that today.
?
The mutakallimun argued that hearing perceives at the source, not at the eardrum. In your spiritual life, are you only reacting to what reaches you superficially, or are you seeking the source of truth? Commit to one action this week that connects you directly to the source of guidance.
