The Mu'tazila Theory of Divine Speech
Razi presents the Mu'tazila definition of divine speech as created sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila definition of divine speech as created sounds and how they analogize from human communication.

Core Idea
Divine speech, for the Mu'tazila, is created sounds indicating God's will.

In this lesson, we explore the Mu'tazila position on divine speech as presented by Imam Razi. He highlights that while the term 'Mutakallim' is agreed upon, its meaning is disputed. The Mu'tazila argue that speech consists of conventional sounds created in a body to convey inner states, and they apply this analogy to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text straight through to get a feel for Razi's logical presentation. Then follow with the English translation.
Notice the phrase 'أصوات مقطعة بهذه التقطيعات المخصوصة' – segmented sounds with specific segmentations.
2
Identify the analogy
The Mu'tazila base their view on human need for communication. Understand how they move from human conventional sounds to divine speech.
They say Allah creates sounds in a body to indicate His will or dislike.
3
Reflect on the disagreement
Razi begins by noting that the umma agrees on the word but not the meaning. Consider why this distinction matters for theological debate.
The Mu'tazila definition is very different from the Ash'ari view of eternal speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني: في إثبات كونه تعالى متكلما
اعلم أن الأمة متفقة على إطلاق لفظ المتكلم على الله تعالى، إلا أن هذا الاتفاق ليس إلا في اللفظ وأما المعنى فغير متفق عليه. أما المعتزلة فقالوا : إن الإنسان لا يمكنه أن يعيش وحده بل ما لم يشتغل كل واحد بإعانة الآخر لم يحصل لكل واحد منهم مقصوده بالتمام، وما لم يعرف كل أحد ما في قلب الآخر من جهات الحاجات لا يمكنه الاشتغال بإعانته، فاحتاج الإنسان إلى وضع طريق يعرف به غيره ما في قلبه من فنون الحاجات، فاصطلحوا على جعل هذه الأصوات المقطعة بهذه التقطيعات المخصوصة معرفة لما في قلوبهم من الأحوال، وقد كان يمكنهم وضع طريق آخر سوى هذا الطريق من الإشارة والإيماء وتصفيق اليد والكتابة، إلا أن هذا الطريق كان أسهل وأيسر.
المسألة السابعة عشرة: إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنه تعالى إذا أراد شيئًا أو كره شيئًا خلق هذه الأصوات المخصوصة في جسم من الأجسام لتدل هذه الأصوات على كونه تعالى مريدا لذلك الشيء المعين أو كارها له أو كونه حاكمًا به بالنفي أو بالإثبات، وهذا هو المراد من كونه تعالى متكلما.
Chapter Two: On Proving that He, Exalted, is Mutakallim (Speaker)
Know that the umma is agreed upon applying the word 'speaker' (mutakallim) to Allah, Exalted, except that this agreement is only in the wording; as for the meaning, it is not agreed upon. As for the Mu'tazila, they said: Man cannot live alone; rather, unless each one occupies himself with assisting the other, the complete purpose is not attained for each one of them, and unless each one knows what is in the heart of the other regarding kinds of needs, he cannot engage in assisting him. So man needed to devise a way by which others know what is in his heart of various needs. So they agreed to make these segmented sounds with these specific segmentations indicative of what is in their hearts of states. And it was possible for them to devise another way besides this way, such as pointing, gesturing, clapping the hand, and writing, except that this way was easier and simpler.
The Seventeenth Issue: If you know this, we say: When He, Exalted, wills something or dislikes something, He creates these specific sounds in some body, so that these sounds indicate that He, Exalted, is willing that specific thing or disliking it, or that He is judging it by negation or affirmation. And this is what is meant by His, Exalted, being mutakallim.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila theory of speech. First, Razi states that the umma agrees on the word 'mutakallim' for Allah, but the meaning is disputed. Then he explains the Mu'tazila position: human beings need to communicate, so they conventionally agreed to use segmented sounds to indicate inner states. This is the origin of human speech. Then, by analogy, the Mu'tazila say that when Allah wills or dislikes something, He creates specific sounds in a body (like a tree or an angel) to indicate His will, dislike, or judgment. This is their definition of divine speech: created sounds in a substrate that convey meaning. Razi presents this as the Mu'tazila view without yet critiquing it.

Key Terms
متكلم
أصوات مقطعة
تقطيعات
المعنى
اللفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree on the meaning of divine speech.
Correction: Razi points out that while the word 'mutakallim' is agreed upon, the meaning is disputed; the Mu'tazila define it as created sounds.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila definition of divine speech according to this passage?
A) Eternal speech subsisting in God's essence.
B) Created sounds in a body indicating God's will.
C) A mental concept.
D) The same as human speech.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila define divine speech as created sounds in a body that communicate God's will, analogizing from human conventional speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you consciously intend to convey what is truly in your heart, or do you sometimes use words as empty sounds? How can you make your speech more like the 'indicative' function described in this passage?
?
If divine speech, according to the Mu'tazila, is merely created sounds that indicate God's will, does that make your prayer and recitation feel less connected to God directly? How does your own belief about the nature of the Qur'an affect your intimacy with it?
?
The passage says humans needed to communicate to survive. Have you ever treated communication as merely a survival tool rather than a sacred trust? What step will you take today to speak with greater truthfulness, knowing that your words reveal your inner state?

Razi's Critique of the Ash'ari Objection
Razi criticizes the Ash'ari objection to created speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of the Ash'ari objection to the Mu'tazila theory of divine speech, and why he considers the objection weak.

Core Idea
Razi argues the Ash'ari objection reduces to a linguistic quibble.

Razi presents an internal critique within the Ash'ari school. The standard Ash'ari objection is that divine speech cannot subsist in another being. Razi finds this objection weak and dissects it into two possible disputes: one about meaning (which is possible) and one about wording (which is merely linguistic). This passage shows Razi's willingness to challenge his own school's positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: objection, response, then two-part analysis.
Pay attention to the phrase "إما أن تكون في المعنى أو في اللفظ".
2
Identify the two parts of Razi's analysis
Identify the two sub-arguments: the meaning-based dispute and the wording-based dispute. Under meaning, note the two points Razi makes about possibility.
Razi's first point under meaning is about Allah's power to create sounds; the second is about making them indicative of will.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why Razi calls the wording dispute 'purely linguistic' and why he thinks it has no connection with meaning. How does this weaken the Ash'ari objection?
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نازعهم أصحابنا فيه وقالوا إنه يمتنع أن يكون متكلما بكلام قائم بالغير : كما أنه يمتنع أن يكون متحركًا بحركة قائمة بالغير، وساكنا بسكون قائم بالغير وعندي : أن هذه المنازعة ضعيفة ؛ لأن هذه المنازعة إما أن تكون في المعنى أو في اللفظ .
أما المعنى، فهنا شيئان
أحدهما : أنه تعالى قادر على خلق هذه الأصوات المنقطعة بالتقطيعات المخصوصة في جسم جمادي أو حيواني، وهذا أمر لا يمكن النزاع فيه؛ لأن خلق هذه الأصوات والحروف في الجسم الجمادي أو الحيواني ممكن، والله تعالى قادر على كل الممكنات. والثاني : أن الله تعالى جعل تلك الأصوات المخصوصة معرفة لكونه تعالى مريدا لبعض الأشياء وكارهًا لبعضها، وهذا أيضًا غير ممتنع، وإذا سلم هذان المقامان عن الطعن فقد سلمنا لهم صحة كونه تعالى متكلما بالمعنى الذي أرادوه. وأما المنازعة في اللفظ فهو أن من فعل هذه الأصوات المخصوصة - وهي الحروف المركبة في الجسم - لغرض أن يعرف غيره ما يريده أو يكرهه فهل يسمى في اللغة أم لا ؟ ومعلوم أن هذا البحث بحث لغوي محض، وليس للمعنى به تعلق البتة.
Our companions (Ash'aris) disputed them (the Mu'tazila) in this and said: It is impossible for Him to be mutakallim with speech subsisting in another, just as it is impossible for Him to be moving with a movement subsisting in another, or stationary with a stationariness subsisting in another. 
In my view, this dispute is weak, because this dispute is either about meaning or about wording.
As for meaning, there are two things:
One: He, Exalted, is powerful over creating these segmented sounds with specific segmentations in an inanimate or animate body. This is something that cannot be disputed, because creating these sounds and letters in an inanimate or animate body is possible, and Allah is powerful over all possibilities.
Two: Allah, Exalted, made those specific sounds indicative of His being willing of some things and disliking of others. This also is not impossible. If these two points are conceded as valid, then we have conceded to them the correctness of His being mutakallim in the meaning they intended.
As for the dispute in wording, it is whether the one who does these specific sounds—which are letters composed in a body—for the purpose of making others know what He wills or dislikes, is called 'mutakallim' in the language or not. And it is known that this investigation is a purely linguistic investigation, and it has no connection with the meaning at all.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage contains Razi's critique of the Ash'ari objection to the Mu'tazila theory of divine speech. First, he presents the Ash'ari objection: it is impossible for Allah to have speech subsisting in another, just as it is impossible for Him to have movement subsisting in another. Razi then says this objection is weak. He analyzes it into two possibilities: the dispute is either about meaning (al-ma'nā) or about wording (al-lafẓ). Regarding meaning, Razi identifies two points that are undeniable: (1) Allah can create sounds in any body, animate or inanimate, since He is omnipotent; (2) Allah can make those sounds indicative of His will or dislike. Both are possible, so the Mu'tazila meaning of mutakallim is acceptable. Regarding wording, the question is whether the agent of such sounds is called mutakallim in Arabic. This is a purely linguistic question, not a theological one about the meaning. Thus Razi dismisses the Ash'ari objection as either conceding the Mu'tazila meaning or being a matter of language.

Key Terms
منازعة
معنى
لفظ
قادر
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari objection is decisive and disproves the Mu'tazila theory of created speech.
Correction: Razi shows the objection is weak; it either concedes the Mu'tazila meaning (since creating sounds is possible) or reduces to a linguistic dispute with no theological weight.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's main point in his critique of the Ash'ari objection?
A) The Ash'ari objection is strong.
B) The objection is either about meaning (possible) or wording (linguistic).
C) The Mu'tazila are wrong.
D) Speech must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Razi reduces the Ash'ari objection to either a conceded point or a linguistic issue, thus defending the Mu'tazila position on created speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement between scholars, do you immediately take sides, or do you first ask whether the dispute is about meaning or terminology? How can you apply Razi's method to your own study of differing views?
?
Razi concedes the opponent's two points on meaning before redirecting the argument. Is there a theological view you strongly oppose in which you can honestly concede valid points? What would that concession cost you, and why might it actually strengthen your position?
?
Think of a disagreement you've had about religion or anything important. Was it ultimately about words rather than substance? If you recognized that, would you have been able to resolve it faster? Commit to identifying such disputes in the future and focusing on the core meaning.
